Answer to the Second Objection on Infinite Regress
The author refutes the objection that an infinite series cannot be described as a sum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the coherence of describing an infinite causal series as a sum, using logical predication.

Core Idea
Infinity can be a predicate of the sum, not of each part.

In this passage, the author takes up a second objection: if a series of causes and effects is infinite, how can it be called a sum or whole? The objector thinks sum implies finitude. The author counters with two logical proofs, showing that the predicate 'infinity' attaches to the whole collection, not to individuals.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objector's claim and the author's response.
Notice that the objector ties the concept of 'sum' to finiteness.
2
Identify the two proofs
The author gives two distinct proofs. For each, identify how the subject-predicate relationship is used to show that the sum can be described as infinite.
The first proof focuses on the predicate 'infinity' having the sum as subject; the second on the meaning of 'the whole' as the entirety of existents.
3
Consider the logical implications
Reflect on why this matters: if the objection were valid, the entire argument about infinite regress would collapse. The author defends it by clarifying what 'sum' means in this context.
This shows the importance of precision in theological logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : « تلك الأسباب والمسببات، إنما يعقل وصفها بكونها مجموعا وجملة وكلا، لو ثبت كونها متناهية . فالجواب : لا نسلم أن الأمر كما ذكرتم، والدليل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا حيث قلنا : الأسباب والمسببات لا نهاية لها، فقد جعلنا اللانهاية محمولا، ومعلوم أنه ليس موضوع هذا المحمول كل واحد من تلك الأسباب، بل ليس إلا المجموع، فثبت أنه يمكن وصفه بكونه مجموعا وجملة، سواء فرضناه متناهيا أو غير متناه. الثاني : إنا إذا قلنا ما لا نهاية له موجود، فقد حكمنا على تلك الأمور لا نهاية لها بأنها موجودة، ونحن لا نريد بالكل والجملة والمجموع إلا تلك الموجودات بأسرها
And as for the second question, which is his statement: 'Those causes and effects can only be rationally described as a sum, whole, and totality if they are established as finite.' The answer: We do not accept that the matter is as you have mentioned, and the proof for that has two aspects: First: When we say that the causes and effects have no end, we have made infinity a predicate, and it is known that the subject of this predicate is not each individual of those causes, but rather only the sum. So it is established that it can be described as a sum and a whole, whether we consider it finite or infinite. Second: When we say that that which has no end exists, we have judged those things that have no end as existing, and we do not mean by 'the whole', 'the totality', and 'the sum' except those existents in their entirety.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objector claimed that an infinite series cannot be described as a sum or whole unless it is finite. The author gives two proofs that it can. First, when we attribute infinity to the series, we are not attributing it to each individual but to the sum, so the sum can be described as infinite. Second, when we say 'something infinite exists,' we are referring to the collection, not each item individually. So the sum is what is described, not the individuals.

Key Terms

جواب
لا نهاية
مجموع
محمول
موضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite series cannot be described as a sum because only finite things can be summed.
Correction: The sum is the subject of the predicate 'infinity'; the individuals are not, so the sum can be infinite.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof given to show that an infinite series can be described as a sum?
A) The sum is not the predicate of individuals
B) Each individual is infinite
C) The series must be finite to be a sum
D) The sum is nothing other than the individuals

Key Takeaway
The sum of an infinite series is a valid subject for the predicate 'infinite,' allowing it to be described as a sum or whole.

?

Answer to the Third Objection on Infinite Regress
The author refutes the claim that the sum of regress is caused by its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sum of an infinite regress cannot be explained by each part causing the next, and how the author refutes that objection.

Core Idea
Neither the individuals nor their relations can be the cause of the sum without circularity or absurdity.

The author now tackles a third objection to the infinite regress argument. The objector claims that the entire chain could be explained if each element is caused by the next. The author dismantles this by distinguishing two possible causes—the individuals themselves or their relations of reliance—and showing both lead to impossibility. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector asks: why not say the sum exists because each individual relies on another, ad infinitum? This is the core of the objection.
3
Distinguish the two concepts
The author points out that the objector's statement implies two distinct concepts: the sum of the individuals' essences, and the reliance-relations that are accidental to them.
These two concepts are not identical.
4
Examine why the individuals cannot be the cause
If the cause is the individuals themselves, then the sum is caused by its own parts, which is circular—the thing causing itself.
That is logically impossible.
5
Examine why the relations cannot be the cause
If the cause is the reliance-relations, then accidents (the relations) are prior to their substances (the individuals), which is also impossible.
The substance is prior in rank to the accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يقال إن تلك الجملة إنما وجدت لأجل أن كل واحد من أحادها استند إلى واحد آخر، إلى غير النهاية . . . فالجواب : عنه : أن نقول : هذا أيضاً باطل لأنا إذا قلنا : الجملة إنما وجدت لاستناد كل واحد من أحاد تلك الجملة إلى واحد آخر، فقد حصل ههنا مفهومان : - أحدهما : مجموع ذوات تلك الأحاد الثاني : كون كل واحد منها مستندا إلى الآخر، ولا شك في تغاير هذين المفهومين، إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول : هذا الذي جعلتموه علة لتلك الجملة، إما أن يكون هو تلك الأحاد أو تلك الاستنادات العارضة لتلك الأحاد، والأول باطل لأن مجموع تلك الآحاد هو عين ذلك المجموع، فتعليل ذلك المجموع بتلك الأحاد يقتضي تعليل الشيء بنفسه، وهو محال . والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأن تلك الاستنادات أحوال عارضة لتلك الآحاد، ولو جعلنا هذه الاستنادات علل لتلك الآحاد، لزم جعل العوارض عللا للمعروضات، وذلك محال، لأن المعروض متقدم بالرتبة على العارض، فلو جعلنا العارض علة للمعروض، لزم تقدم كل واحد منها على الآخر وهو محال، فيثبت سقوط هذا السؤال، وههنا آخر الكلام في تقرير هذا البرهان ( والله ولي الرحمة والغفران )
And as for the third question, which is his statement: 'Why is it not permissible to say that that sum came into existence because each one of its individuals relied on another, ad infinitum?' The answer: We say that this is also false because if we say that the sum came into existence due to the reliance of each one of the individuals of that sum on another, then here two concepts arise: one: the sum of the essences of those individuals; the second: the fact that each one of them is reliant on the other, and there is no doubt about the difference between these two concepts. Once you understand this, we say: This thing that you have made a cause for that sum is either those individuals or those reliance-relations that are accidents of those individuals. The first is false because the sum of those individuals is identical to that sum, so making that sum caused by those individuals requires making a thing caused by itself, which is impossible. The second is also false because those reliance-relations are accidental states of those individuals, and if we make these accidents causes of the individuals, it would entail making accidents causes of the substances, which is impossible because the substance is prior in rank to the accident, so if we make the accident a cause of the substance, it would require the priority of each one over the other, which is impossible. Thus, this question is refuted, and here ends the discourse on establishing this proof (and Allah is the possessor of mercy and forgiveness).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection that the infinite chain can be explained by each element being caused by the next. The author distinguishes two concepts: the sum of the individuals, and the relations of reliance. The cause of the sum cannot be the individuals themselves because that would be circular. It cannot be the relations because they are accidents of the individuals, making accidents prior to substances, which is impossible. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
جملة
أحاد
استناد
علة
عارض
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each individual causing the next explains the entire infinite chain.
Correction: The author shows that neither the individuals nor their relations can be the cause of the sum without circularity or absurdity.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the reliance-relations to be the cause of the sum?
A) They are identical to the sum
B) They are accidents and cannot be prior to their substances
C) They are infinite
D) They are external to the sum

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress cannot be explained by each part causing the next, as that leads to either self-causation or accidents prior to substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it take for you to live in constant awareness of that dependence?

Second Proof for Invalidating Infinite Regress
A concise proof that the sum of possibles requires a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concise contingency argument for a necessary being and why it avoids extensive divisions.

Core Idea
The sum of all contingent things requires a cause that is not contingent.

In this lesson, we examine a more compact formulation of the argument against infinite regress. Rather than enumerating many categories, the author focuses on the sum of possible beings as a whole and argues that it must depend on a being that is necessary in itself. This is a key proof in Islamic theology for the existence of a Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise style and the key terms: mumkin (possible), wajib (necessary), mughayir (distinct).
2
Identify the logical steps
The proof proceeds in a logical chain: assumption, deduction about the sum, deduction about individuals, conclusion about a distinct cause, and final necessity.
List each premise and conclusion in order.
3
Understand why the cause cannot be possible
The cause is distinct from the entire sum of possibles and from each individual. Anything that is entirely distinct from all possibles cannot itself be possible.
If it were possible, it would be part of the sum, contradicting its distinctness.
4
Reflect on the conciseness
The author notes that this proof avoids the many divisions mentioned earlier. Appreciate how focusing on the sum simplifies the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني في إبطال التسلسل : اعلم أنه يمكن ذكر البرهان الذي قدمنا ذكره بحيث يصير أشد اختصارا مما سبق ذكره فنقول : لو تسلسلت الأسباب والمسببات إلى غير النهاية، لكانت تلك الجملة من حيث إنها جملة ممكنة، ولكان كل واحد من آحاد تلك الجملة أيضا ممكنا، وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب مغاير له، فلهذه الجملة سبب مغاير لها من حيث إنها تلك الجملة، ومغاير لكل واحد من أحاد تلك الجملة، وكل ما كان مغايرا لجملة الممكنات، وكان مغايرا لكل واحد من آحاد الممكنات، فهو ليس بممكن، وكل موجود ليس بممكن فهو واجب لذاته، وهو المطلوب، فثبت بهذا الطريق : وجوب انتهاء جملة الممكنات إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وهو المنظوب، ويظهر من هذا البيان : أنه يمكن ذكر هذا البرهان من غير أن يحتاج فيه إلى تلك التقسيمات الكثيرة التي ذكرناها
The second proof for invalidating infinite regression: Know that it is possible to mention the proof we have already presented in a way that becomes more concise than what was mentioned before. We say: If the causes and effects regressed infinitely, then that sum, insofar as it is a sum, would be possible, and each one of the individuals of that sum would also be possible. And every possible thing must have a cause distinct from it. So this sum has a cause distinct from it insofar as it is that sum, and distinct from each one of the individuals of that sum. And everything that is distinct from the sum of possibles and distinct from each one of the individuals of the possibles is not possible. And every existent that is not possible is necessary in itself. That is what is sought. So it is established by this method: the necessity of the sum of possibles terminating at an existent that is necessary in essence, which is the desired. And it appears from this explanation that it is possible to mention this proof without needing the many divisions we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof against infinite regress. It argues that if the chain of causes and effects is infinite, then the entire chain as a whole is contingent (possible), and each member is contingent. Every contingent needs a cause distinct from it. So the entire chain needs a cause distinct from the chain and distinct from each member. That cause cannot itself be contingent, so it must be necessary. Thus, the chain must end in a necessary being.

Key Terms
برهان
إبطال التسلسل
ممكن
واجب لذاته
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sum of possible things could be its own cause.
Correction: The proof shows that the cause must be distinct from the entire sum and from each individual, so it cannot be part of the sum or the sum itself. Therefore, it must be external and not possible.

Checkpoint
Why must the cause of the sum of possibles be not possible?
A) Because it is distinct from all possibles
B) Because it is infinite
C) Because it is a cause
D) Because it is a sum

Key Takeaway
The sum of all possible beings must depend on a self-necessary being distinct from every possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the necessary being is the only non-possible reality, then everything else is constantly in need of Him. Does your heart feel that need every moment, or have you become heedless of it? Commit to one concrete action today that reminds you of your absolute need for Allah.

The Fifth Proof: Comparing Infinite Sets
Presents the fifth proof: comparing two infinite sets leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing two allegedly infinite sets of causes leads to a contradiction, demonstrating that an infinite regress is impossible.

Core Idea
Comparing two infinite chains shows they cannot both be infinite.

Islamic theologians formulated multiple proofs against infinite regress of causes. This fifth proof uses a clever comparison: if we take two different contingent beings, each with its own infinite chain of causes, then the combined set would be larger than either alone — but an infinite cannot be larger than another infinite. This contradiction shows the assumption of an infinite regress is false.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the passage and note that the proof begins by assuming a contingent being with its infinite chain of causes.
The proof uses two such beings.
2
Follow the comparison
The argument then compares the number of causes in one chain versus the combined causes of two chains.
The combined chain should be larger.
3
Identify the contradiction
If both chains are infinite, they cannot differ in magnitude, but the combined chain is definitely larger. This is a contradiction (khalaf).
The conclusion is that the original chain must be finite.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
This proof is a reductio ad absurdum: assume infinite regress, derive a contradiction, therefore the assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس : إذا فرضنا موجودا واحدا من الممكنات ، فهو مع علله أعداد غير متناهية ، ثم إذا اعتبرنا واحدا آخر فهو أيضاً مع جملة علله أعداد أخرى غير متناهية ، وإذا كان كذلك فعدد الجملتين أكثر لا محالة من عدد الجملة الواحدة، وكل ما كان أقل من غيره ، فهو متناه فعدد الجملة الواحدة متناه ، وقد فرضناه غير متناه . هذا خلف .
The fifth proof: If we assume one existent from among contingent things, then along with its causes it is an infinite number, then if we consider another, it also along with the totality of its causes is another infinite number. And if this is so, then the number of the two totalities is inevitably greater than the number of the one totality. And everything that is less than something else is finite. So the number of the one totality is finite, but we have assumed it to be infinite. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes. The argument works by comparing two sets of an infinite chain. If you take one contingent existent along with its infinite chain of causes, that constitutes one infinite set. Then take another contingent existent with its own infinite chain; that is a second infinite set. Since both sets are infinite, each is equal in magnitude, but because the second set includes the first plus additional members, it is larger. But if both are infinite, they cannot differ in size. Therefore the assumption that they are both infinite is false. The conclusion: the chain must be finite. The proof ends with 'this is a contradiction' (khalaf).

Key Terms
برهان
متناھیة
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite sets cannot be compared at all, so the proof is invalid.
Correction: The proof assumes a specific case where two sets are clearly related (one is a subset of the other), and derives a contradiction under that assumption, which is a standard logical technique.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof conclude about the total number of causes when two contingent existents are considered?
A) Both totals are infinite and equal.
B) The total of one is less than the other, so it must be finite.
C) The totals are infinite and unequal.
D) Both totals are finite.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that an infinite regress of causes leads to a logical contradiction, proving the chain must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. If your life were just a link in an endless chain, would you have any ultimate accountability? How does knowing there must be a First Cause change the way you view your every decision and action?
?
This argument forces you to choose: either an infinite regress which is absurd, or a First Cause that is self-sufficient. Have you ever truly felt the weight of needing Someone uncaused to ground your existence? How does that feeling move you to respond to Him today?
?
The contradiction arises because one infinite set is larger than another. In your own life, do you sometimes treat your problems as if they are 'infinite'—overwhelming and without end? How might acknowledging that all finite things must have a stopping point give you hope and perspective?

The Middle Term Argument Against Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents the sixth proof against infinite regress using the logical properties of 'middle' and 'endpoint'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of causes and effects into last effect, first cause, and intermediates leads to the logical necessity of an endpoint in any causal chain, proving the existence of a Necessary Being.

Core Idea
An infinite causal chain has no endpoint, so every link is a middle—but a middle must depend on an endpoint, which is the Necessary Being.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which uses the logical properties of 'middle' and 'endpoint' to refute infinite regress. The argument begins by defining three types of entities in a causal chain, then shows that an infinite chain would be self-contradictory, ultimately affirming the existence of a Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three categories
The proof first distinguishes three roles: the last effect (only an effect, not a cause), the first cause (only a cause, not an effect), and intermediates (both cause and effect). Notice the defining property of each.
These categories are crucial for the logical structure of the argument.
2
Consider the supposition of an infinite chain
Imagine the causal chain extends infinitely without beginning or end. Under this supposition, every link becomes an intermediate—none has the property of being a true endpoint.
3
Analyze the dependence of the middle
An intermediate depends on something above it. If the whole chain is just a collection of intermediates, it must either depend on something outside the chain (an endpoint) or be self-sufficient. The proof examines both possibilities.
If self-sufficient, each link would be an endpoint, contradicting its status as a middle.
4
Conclude the necessity of an endpoint
The only consistent outcome is that the chain depends on an endpoint—a cause that is not itself an effect. This endpoint is the Necessary Existent. Thus, denying an endpoint leads to a contradiction, proving that an endpoint must exist.
This is the key conclusion: the negation of the endpoint forces its affirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس : إن المعلول الآخر له علة ، ولعلته علة ، فالمعلول الآخر خاصيته أنه معلول ، وليس بعلة ، والعلة الأولى لو حصلت ، لكان خاصيتها أنها علة ، وليست بمعلولة ، وأما المتوسطات فهي مشتركة في صفة واحدة ، وهي كون كل واحدة منها علة لما تحتها ، ومعلولا لما فوقها . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو فرضنا ذهاب العلل والمعلولات إلى غير النهاية ، لكان الكل في حكم الوسط ، ولم يحصل لشيء منها خاصية الطرف البتة ، ثم نقول : هذا الوسط إن استند إلى شيء ليس له خاصية الوسط ، فذاك هو المطلوب وإن لم يستند إلى شيء بهذه الصفة، كان الوسط غنيا عن الاستناد إلى الطرف ، وما لم يستند إلى غيره فهو طرف وليس بوسط ، فالوسط ليس بوسط ، وإذا كان كذلك فعلة المعلول الأخر وجب أن لا تستند إلى غيرها ، لأن التقدير تقدير الوسط لا يجب استناده إلى غيره ، وإذا كانت هذه العلة غنية عن الاستناد إلى شيء ثالث ، كانت طرفا ، فكانت واجبة الوجود لذاتها ، فيثبت أن نفي الطرف يوجب إثباته ، فوجب أن يكون هذا النفي باطلا ، وأن يكون إثبات طرف الممكنات أمراً واجباً ، وهو المطلوب .
The sixth proof: The last effect has a cause, and its cause has a cause. The last effect has the property that it is an effect and not a cause. The first cause, if it existed, would have the property that it is a cause and not an effect. As for the intermediate things, they share one attribute, namely that each one of them is a cause for what is below it and an effect for what is above it. If this is established, we say: If we suppose the going of causes and effects to infinity, then everything would be in the status of the middle, and none of them would acquire the property of the endpoint at all. Then we say: If this middle depends on something that does not have the property of the middle, then that is what is sought. And if it does not depend on anything with this attribute, then the middle is independent of depending on the endpoint, and whatever does not depend on another is an endpoint and not a middle, so the middle is not a middle. If this is so, then the cause of the last effect must not depend on something else, because the supposition is the supposition of the middle not requiring dependence on another. And if this cause is independent of dependence on a third thing, then it is an endpoint, so it is necessary of existence in itself. Thus it is established that the negation of the endpoint necessitates its affirmation, so this negation must be false, and the affirmation of an endpoint for contingent things must be necessary, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof against infinite regress, based on the properties of 'middle' (wasat) and 'endpoint' (taraf). The proof first characterizes three types: the last effect (only caused, not cause), the first cause (only cause, not effect), and intermediates (both cause and effect). If the causal chain were infinite, every link would be an intermediate, lacking the property of being an ultimate cause. The argument then considers that such an intermediate chain must either depend on something that is not a middle (an endpoint) – which is the desired conclusion – or else the chain is self-sufficient without an endpoint. But if it is self-sufficient, then each link would be an endpoint, contradicting its status as a middle. Therefore, to avoid contradiction, the chain must have an endpoint, i.e., a necessary existent. Thus, negating an endpoint leads to affirming it, so the negation is false.

Key Terms
معلول
علة
وسط
طرف
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might believe that an infinite causal chain can exist without any ultimate cause, sustained by a series of intermediate causes.
Correction: The proof shows that an infinite chain of intermediates would have no endpoint, but each intermediate depends on a prior cause, requiring an ultimate endpoint to avoid logical contradiction. Therefore, an infinite regress is impossible.

Checkpoint
In the sixth proof, what is the status of each link in an infinite causal chain?
A) Each is an endpoint.
B) Each is a middle.
C) Each is both middle and endpoint.
D) Each is neither middle nor endpoint.

Key Takeaway
The negation of an endpoint in a causal chain logically forces the affirmation of an endpoint, proving the necessity of a First Cause that is Necessary in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the negation of an endpoint forces its affirmation, then your denial of God's necessity is not just a belief but a logical contradiction. How does it feel to know that your reasoning itself demands a Creator? Will you surrender to that truth, or will you continue to live as if you are the endpoint of your own existence?
?
Consider the 'middles' in your life: your job, your relationships, your health. You naturally treat them as ends in themselves. But this proof shows that nothing middle can stand alone. Where have you been treating a middle as if it were an endpoint? What would change if you truly saw everything as pointing beyond itself to the Necessary One?

Defending the First Proof Against the Composition Objection
Presents and refutes an objection to the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof against a possible being being a cause is defended against an objection that appeals to compositeness.

Core Idea
Composition does not allow a possible being to be both receptive and effective.

This passage presents the core argument that a possible being cannot be a cause because its essence is defined by receptivity. An objection suggests compositeness might solve the problem, but the response shows it fails. Let's read the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of the first proof
The possible being's essence necessitates receptivity. It is established that its essence is the cause of this receptivity. So if it were also effective in causing another being, one essence would necessitate two distinct outcomes.
This premise is rooted in the principle 'from one only one issues'.
2
See the objection: compositeness as a solution
Objectors propose that the possible being could have a composite essence: one part for receptivity, another for effect. This would avoid the violation of the principle.
3
Follow the response: the effective part is not possible
The response shows that on this assumption, the part responsible for receptivity is not effective, and the part responsible for effect is not possible (since it lacks receptivity). Therefore, no part qualifies as an effective possible being.
This directly refutes the objection and affirms the original proof.
4
Recognize the key term: 'the one does not issue from one except one'
This philosophical principle underlies the proof. The objectors try to circumvent it via compositeness, but the response shows the attempt fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن الممكن هو الذي ماهينه مقتضية لقبول الوجود والعدم، وثبت أن ماهية الممكن علة لهذه القابلية ، فلو كانت مؤثرة في وجود شيء آخر ، لكانت الماهية الواحدة مقتضية للقبول وللتأثير ، فيكون الواحد صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، وذلك باطل للوجوه التي يذكرها الفلاسفة ، في أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ) فإن ( (۱) ، قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال ( إن ذلك ) (۲) الممكن المؤثر (۳) في وجود الغير تكون ماهيته مركبة ، وهو بأحد الجزءين يقتضي القبول ، وبالجزء الثاني (٤) يقتضي التأثير ؟ فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير الجزء الذي يقتضي القابلية ليس بمؤثر ، والجزء الذي يقتضي التأثير ليس بممكن ، فالذي هو ليس بممكن ليس بمؤثر ، والذي هو مؤثر ليس بممكن ، وهو المطلوب
The first proof: The possible is that whose essence necessitates the reception of existence and nonexistence, and it is established that the essence of the possible is the cause of this receptivity. So if it were effective in the existence of something else, then one essence would necessitate both reception and effect, and thus from one more than one would issue, and that is false for the reasons that the philosophers mention, namely that from one only one issues. They (the objectors) said: Why is it not permissible to say that that possible effective in the existence of another has a composite essence: by one part it necessitates reception, and by the second part it necessitates effect? We say: According to this assumption, the part that necessitates receptivity is not effective, and the part that necessitates effect is not possible. So that which is not possible is not effective, and that which is effective is not possible. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof and the response to an objection. The proof argues that the essence of a possible being is characterized by receptivity to existence and nonexistence; this receptivity is caused by its essence. If that same essence were also effective in causing another being, then one essence would produce two effects (receptivity in itself and existence in another), violating the philosophical principle 'from one only one issues'. The objector suggests that the possible being could have a composite essence, with one part for receptivity and another for effect. The response counters that on this assumption, the part responsible for receptivity is not effective, and the part responsible for effect is not possible (since it does not have receptivity, a defining feature of the possible). Therefore, no part qualifies as an effective possible being, proving the original claim.

التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is accepted, i.e., compositeness allows a possible being to be a cause.
Correction: The response shows that compositeness does not work because the effective part would not be possible, and thus cannot be a cause.

C) The possible's essence necessitates only reception, and effect would require a separate principle

Key Takeaway
The possible being cannot be a cause because its essence is solely for receptivity, and any effective part would not be possible.

Second Proof: All Possibles Need One Necessary Cause
Argues that all possible beings need one specific cause, which must be necessary, and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the existence of a single necessary cause based on the concept of possibility, and how an objection against it is addressed.

Core Idea
Possibility is a single concept requiring a single specified cause, which must be necessary.

This lesson presents the second rational proof for the existence of a necessary being. The proof argues that all possible beings, due to their shared nature of possibility, must depend on one specific cause, which cannot itself be possible. An objection is then raised and the beginning of a response is given, distinguishing two ways of considering a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the logical flow of the argument become clear.
The text ends abruptly; the full refutation is not included.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify how possibility (الإمكان) creates a need for a specific agent, not an unspecified one. Notice why the unspecified cannot exist externally.
3
Examine the objection
Read the objector's claim that possibility only requires a cause qua cause, not a specific cause. Consider how that would allow multiple causes.
This challenges the conclusion that all possibles need one cause.
4
Analyze the beginning of the response
The response distinguishes between considering the cause's specific essence and considering it merely as a cause. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The text cuts off, but the direction of the refutation is clear.

The second proof: Possibility is the cause of need for the effective agent. Either it is the cause of need for an agent unspecified, or it is the cause of need for an agent specified in itself. The first is false, because the unspecified, insofar as it is unspecified, has no existence externally, since everything that exists externally is specified. So what is not specified cannot exist externally, and everything that does not exist externally cannot be needed externally. When this division is invalidated, it is established that the essence of possibility is the cause of need for something specified. And the essence of possibility is one, insofar as it is the essence of possibility. Therefore all possibles must need that specified thing, because equality in the cause necessitates equality in the effect. When this is established, we say: if that specified thing were possible in itself, then it would need itself and be the cause of itself, which is impossible. So it is established that all possibles need one specific thing, and that that specified thing cannot be possible in itself, and the existent that is not possible in itself is necessary in itself. So it is established that all possibles depend on an existent that is necessary in itself, which is the desired conclusion. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that possibility is the cause of need for the cause insofar as it is a cause, and many causes when taken insofar as they are causes, the concept of being causes is a single concept, and possibility, although it is a single concept, only necessitates need for the cause insofar as it is a cause, so it does not necessitate attributing all possibles to one cause? We say: The cause is either considered in its specific essence or considered merely as being a cause and effective in another. As for the second consideration... (text breaks off)
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof and an objection with the beginning of an answer. The proof proceeds as follows: Possibility (الإمكان) is the cause of need for an effective agent. The agent must be specific (معين), not unspecified, because the unspecified has no external existence. Since possibility is a single concept, all possible beings must need the same specific agent. If that agent were itself possible, it would need itself, which is impossible. Therefore, it must be necessary in itself. The objector argues that possibility only requires a cause qua cause, and many causes share the concept of cause, so each possible could have its own cause. The response begins by distinguishing between considering the cause's specific essence or merely its causality. The text cuts off at 'As for the second consideration...' so the full refutation is not present.

واجب الوجود

Misconception: Possibility could require multiple different causes because the need is just for 'a cause' in general.
Correction: The proof shows that the unspecified cause has no external existence, and since possibility is a single concept, the need must be for a single specified cause. The objection tries to avoid this by saying need is for cause qua cause, but the response (though incomplete) distinguishes between cause as a concept and cause as a specific entity.

Refutation of Combination Producing a New Effect
The author argues that combining inert parts cannot produce a new causal property without infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining two causally inert parts cannot produce a new causal property without falling into infinite regress or other logical problems.

Core Idea
Inert parts cannot magically produce a new effect; any new state requires a cause, leading to regress.

The author now tackles a key objection to his causal theory: if single entities cannot produce effects, could their combination do so? He presents a powerful refutation that examines every possibility and shows that each leads to an unacceptable consequence. Read carefully to follow the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into details.
Note the logical structure: initial assumption, two horns, three subcases.
2
Identify the initial dilemma
The author sets up two possibilities when inert parts combine: either they remain unchanged (so composite is still non-effecting) or a supervening state occurs. Pause to see why both are problematic.
3
Analyze the three subcases of the second horn
If a new state occurs, what causes it? Trace each option: each part, one specific part, or the composite as a whole. Notice how the last leads to infinite regress.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان كل واحد من الجزءين ليس له أثر أصلا ، فهذه الأجزاء عند اجتماعها ، إما أن يقال : إنها بقيت كما كانت قبل الاجتماع ، أو ما بقيت كذلك ، بل حدث بسبب الاجتماع حالة زائدة ، فإن كان الأول يجب أن يبقى المجموع عند الاجتماع غير مؤثر (۱) ، كما أنها كانت قبل الإجتماع غير مؤثرة ، وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه حدث عند الاجتماع أمر زائد ، فنقول : المقتضى حدوث هذه الحالة الزائدة. إن كان كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء ، فحينئذ يعود ما ذكرنا من اجتماع المؤثرات المستقلة على الأثر الواحد ، وإن كان المقتضى لها أحد تلك الأجزاء بعينه ، فحينئذ يكون الموجب والمؤثر هو ذلك المفرد فقط ، وإن كان المقتضي لحدوث تلك الحالة الزائدة هو المجموع عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، وهو أنه هل حدث عند الاجتماع حالة زائدة ولو إلى غير نهاية ؟ وهو محال ، وقد لا يظن توجيه النقوض على هذه الحجة ، إلا أن التأمل التام يدل على أنها ليست صحيحة ) وبالله الهداية والإرشاد) (۳).
If each of the two parts had no effect at all, then when these parts are combined, either it is said that they remained as they were before combination, or they did not remain so, but rather a supervening state occurred due to combination. If the first, then the composite must remain non-effecting (1) when combined, just as they were non-effecting before combination. If the second – that a supervening thing occurred at combination – we say: the necessitator of the occurrence of this supervening state: if it is each one of those parts, then what we mentioned about the gathering of independent effectors on a single effect returns; if the necessitator is one particular part, then the necessitator and effector is that single [part] only; if the necessitator of the occurrence of that supervening state is the composite, then the first division returns concerning it – namely, did a supervening state occur at combination, even ad infinitum? That is impossible. And one might think that objections can be directed against this argument, but thorough reflection indicates that it is not sound – and with Allah is guidance and direction (3).
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the claim that combining two inert parts produces a new causal property. The author begins by assuming each part individually has no effect. Then he considers two possibilities when they combine: either they remain unchanged (so the combination also has no effect), or a new state (حالة زائدة) arises. If a new state arises, he asks what causes it. If each part causes it, then multiple independent causes act on one effect, which is problematic. If one specific part causes it, then the effect is really from that part alone, not the combination. If the composite as a whole causes it, then the same question applies to the composite's own composition, leading to an infinite regress. The author concludes that the argument is not sound upon thorough reflection, despite appearances.

Key Terms

أثر
اجتماع
حالة زائدة
مقتضى
مؤثر
مستقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that aggregating inert parts can produce a new property without a cause.
Correction: The argument shows that if a new state arises, it must have a cause, and every possible cause leads to a contradiction or regress, so the claim is false.

Checkpoint

A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It implies multiple independent causes for a single effect.
C) It contradicts the initial assumption.
D) It makes the composite non-effecting.

Key Takeaway
Combining causally inert parts cannot generate a new effect; any alleged new state would require a cause, leading to regress or other contradictions.

The Impossibility of Attributing Events to Temporal Causes
The author shows that positing originated causes leads to circularity or regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing events to a chain of temporal causes inevitably leads to either circularity or infinite regress, thereby demolishing the rational proof for a necessary existent.

Core Idea
Temporal causes cannot be ultimate; only an eternal cause avoids circularity or regress.

In this densely argued passage, the author tests the consequences of supposing that events are caused by other originated events. He presents two branches: either the temporal cause exists before its effect or simultaneously with it. Each branch leads to a logical impasse — infinite regress or circularity — showing that the very principle that every contingent thing needs a cause would collapse. This argument forces the conclusion that only an eternal, non-temporal cause can coherently explain the existence of events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Note the dialectical style: the author sets up a conditional argument.
The footnotes (1), (2), (4) are markers in the original text; ignore them for now.
2
Identify the main disjunction
The passage begins by stating the second case (from a prior division) and then moves to the correct view. Locate the key division: the effecting cause (المؤثر) is either originated (محدث) or eternal (قديم).
This is the first major fork in the argument.
3
Trace the consequences of an originated cause
Focus on the two sub-branches when the cause is originated: (a) the cause existed before the effect but did not remain with it, and (b) the cause exists simultaneously with the effect. For each, work through the consequences the author draws.
The first leads to a regress of causes with no first cause; the second leads to either circularity (the cause being its own effect) or another regress.
4
Understand the overall conclusion
The author concludes that attributing events to a chain of temporal causes forces the abandonment of one of the premises in the original proof for God's existence. Reflect on why this shows the necessity of an eternal, uncaused cause.
This is a classic kalam demonstration via impossibility of alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو الثاني فقد حصل الممكن المحدث لاعن مؤثر ، وذلك يبطل قولكم : إن الممكن المحدث لا بد له من مرجح ، وإن كان الحق هو الأول ، وهو أن هذه الحوادث مفتقرة إلى المؤثر ، فنقول : ذلك المؤثر ، إما أن يكون محدثا أو قديماً ، فإن كان محدثا ، فإما أن يقال : إن ذلك المحدث كان موجوداً قبله ، بمعنى أن كل جزء من أجزاء هذه الحوادث ، فإنه معلول للجزء السابق عليه ، وإما أن يقال : إن ذلك المؤثر المحدث يكون موجودا مع هذا الأثر ( أو لا يكون (1) فإن كان الحق هو القسم الأول ، فقد جوزتم أن يكون المؤثر في وجود هذا الحادث شيئاً كان موجوداً قبل هذا الحادث ولم يبق معه، وإذا جوزتم ذلك ، فجوزوا استناد كل ممكن إلى موجود آخر كان موجوداً) (۲) قبله، ولم يبق معه، لا إلى أول، وعلى هذا التقدير فلا يمكنكم إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، ولا يمكنكم أن تقولوا : إن إثبات حوادث لا أول لها محال ، لأن هذا صريح قول الفلاسفة فكيف يمكنهم ) إنكاره؟ وإن كان الحق هو الثاني، وهو أن علة وجود هذا الحادث شيء آخر حدث معه ، فنقول : علة وجود هذا الحادث ، إما أن يكون هو الحادث الذي هو معلوله ، وإما أن يكون حادثا آخر ، والأول يوجب الدور ، والآخر (4) يوجب التسلسل ، فثبت أنا إذا أسندنا هذه الحوادث إلى علل حادثة ، لزمنا هدم مقدمة من المقدمات المذكورة في أصل الدليل
the second [case] — then a contingent originated thing occurs without an effecting cause, and that invalidates your statement: 'the contingent originated thing must have a preponderator.' But if the truth is the first [case], which is that these events are in need of an effecting cause, then we say: that effecting cause is either originated or eternal. If it is originated, then either it is said that that originated [cause] existed before it, meaning that every part of these events is an effect of the part preceding it, or it is said that that originated effecting cause exists together with this effect (or not (1)). If the truth is the first division, then you have permitted that the effecting cause in the existence of this event is something that existed before this event and did not remain with it. And if you permit that, then permit the dependence of every possible upon another existent that existed before it and did not remain with it, with no first [cause]. On this assumption, you cannot prove a necessary existent in itself, and you cannot say that establishing events with no beginning is impossible, because this is the explicit statement of the philosophers — so how can they deny it? But if the truth is the second [division], which is that the cause of the existence of this event is something else that occurred with it, then we say: the cause of the existence of this event is either the event that is its effect, or it is another event. The first necessitates circularity, and the other (4) necessitates infinite regress. So it is established that if we attribute these events to originated causes, we are forced to abandon one of the premises mentioned in the original proof.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument. It begins by stating the second case (presumably from a previous division) that would allow a contingent originated thing to occur without a cause, contradicting the principle that every contingent originated thing needs a preponderator. The author then presents the correct view: events need an effecting cause. He divides the effecting cause into originated or eternal. Focusing on the originated cause, he explores two subcases. First, if the cause existed before the effect but not with it, then every part of the event is the effect of the preceding part, leading to an infinite regress without a first cause — which entails the impossibility of proving a necessary existent. Second, if the cause exists with the effect, then the cause of the event is either the event itself (circularity) or another event (regress). Thus, attributing events to originated causes forces abandonment of one of the premises of the original proof that a contingent originated thing must have a preponderator. The author does not decide which premise is abandoned, but shows that any result is self-defeating.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a temporal cause can exist before its effect without causing an infinite regress, because each cause is a different event.
Correction: The passage shows that if each event is caused by a prior event, and there is no first cause, then the chain never started — which contradicts the claim that the universe had a beginning and needs a first cause. Moreover, this aligns with the philosophers' view of an eternal universe, which the theologian seeks to refute.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Attributing events to temporal causes forces an infinite regress or circularity, demonstrating that only an eternal, self-sufficient cause can coherently explain the existence of contingent things.

Yet, do you live as if the world is self-sufficient, relying on secondary causes without constant consciousness of Allah? What specific habit can you adopt to remind yourself that every moment is sustained by the Eternal?

Two Problems with an Eternal Cause for Temporal Events
The author examines two possibilities for an eternal cause of events, both leading to contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal cause for temporal events leads to logical contradictions, specifically the problems of preponderance without a preponderator and infinite regress.

Core Idea
An eternal cause that is complete necessitates its effect eternally; a delayed effect requires an unjustified preponderance.

This passage examines a common objection to the cosmological argument: could an eternal being be the cause of temporal events? The author shows that this leads to two impossible outcomes, reinforcing the need for a temporal cause of the universe's beginning. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two subcases introduced. The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first subcase
The eternal cause is assumed complete in all respects in pre-eternity. If so, its effect should exist eternally. Why does it not? This creates a need for a preponderator, but none is available.
This is the preponderance without a preponderator problem.
3
Identify the second subcase
The eternal cause is incomplete; then the missing conditions must arise. How do they arise? Either without cause (another preponderator problem) or through another cause, leading to regress or circularity.
This leads to three unacceptable options.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both subcases lead to contradictions, so the assumption of an eternal cause for daily events is false. This supports the need for a temporal cause.
The conclusion is that one premise of the original proof must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : المقتضى لحدوث الحوادث اليومية (0) قديم أزلي ، فنقول : ذلك القديم إما أن يقال : إنه كان تاما في الأزل في جميع الأمور المعتبرة في تأثيره في هذا الحادث اليومي ، أو ما كان كذلك ، فإن كان الأول فنقول : فذلك المؤثر التام كان موجوداً (قبل حدوث هذا الحادث ان هذا الحادث ما كان موجوداً) (۱) وقد وجد الآن مع وجود هذا الحادث ، فاختصاص حدوث هذا الحادث بهذا الوقت مع أن نسبة ذلك المؤثر التام إلى الوقتين على السوية ، يكون (ذلك) (۷) رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، وأما إذا قلنا : إن ذلك المؤثر ما كان تاما في جميع الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثرا في هذا الحادث، ثم حدث مجموع تلك الأمور بحدوث ذلك المجموع . إن كان لا سبب . فقد وقع الممكن لا عن مرجح ، وإن كان عن سبب عاد التقسيم الأول فيه، فيقتضي إما إلى إسناد المعلول الحاضر إلى علة سابقة ، وإما إلى وقوع الدور ، وإما إلى وقوع التسلسل ، فثبت أن حدوث هذه الحوادث اليومية ، يقتضي إفساد مقدمة واحدة من المقدمات المذكورة في ذلك الدليل
As for the second division: that the efficient cause for the occurrence of daily events is an eternal pre-eternal [being]. So we say: that eternal [cause] is either said to have been complete in pre-eternity in all matters considered in its effecting this daily event, or not to have been so. If it is the first, we say: that complete effecting cause existed before the occurrence of this event (but this event did not exist) and now it exists with the existence of this event. So the specification of the occurrence of this event for this time, while the relation of that complete cause to the two times is equal, constitutes a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator. And if we say that that effecting cause was not complete in all matters considered in its being an effecting cause for this event, then the totality of those matters occurred with the occurrence of that totality. If there is no cause, then the possible occurred without a preponderator; and if it is through a cause, then the first division returns in it, leading either to attributing the present effect to a preceding cause, or to the occurrence of circularity, or to the occurrence of infinite regress. So it is established that the occurrence of these daily events necessitates the corruption of one of the premises mentioned in that proof.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage treats the second division of the cause: an eternal cause. Two subcases are considered. First, if the eternal cause was complete in all respects in pre-eternity, then since it existed before the event and the event did not, but now it does, the occurrence of the event at a specific time rather than another constitutes a preponderance without a preponderator — impossible. Second, if the cause was not complete, then the totality of necessary conditions occurs at the time of the event. If this totality occurs without a cause, the event happens without a preponderator. If it occurs through a cause, then that cause itself falls under the same division (originated or eternal), leading back to the same problems of regress or circularity. The conclusion is that the occurrence of daily events requires the corruption of one of the premises of the original proof.

Misconception: One might assume an eternal cause can choose to create at a specific time without a reason.
Correction: The passage argues that if the cause is complete, its effect would be eternal; a delayed effect requires a preponderating factor, which is absent, making it impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
An eternal cause cannot produce temporal effects without leading to logical contradictions, so the cause of the universe's beginning must itself be temporal.

Philosophers' Answer: Infinite Regress with a Condition
Philosophers' answer to the causation dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' solution to the causation dilemma: every event is preceded by another event without a first, with the passing of the previous event as a condition.

Core Idea
Every event is preceded by another event without a first.

The philosophers acknowledge the problem is overwhelming, but they offer a solution grounded in their worldview. They argue that each event is caused by an eternal existent, but only once the preceding event expires. This leads to an infinite chain of events with no beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the philosophers' answer once. Then read the translation. Notice how they present their solution as the only possible answer.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا إلى أول' (without a first).
2
Understand the 'one word' principle
The philosophers say the answer hinges on one word: that every event is preceded by another event, without a first. Grasp how this avoids the problem of an infinite regress.
The 'word' is the acceptance of an infinite series.
3
Examine the condition for emanation
The eternal existent causes the next event only when the previous event passes away. This condition means that no two events coexist from the same cause.
The term 'فيضان' (emanation) reflects their metaphysical framework.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this answer depends on allowing an infinite regress of events. How does this differ from a first cause?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الفلاسفة أجابوا عن هذا السؤال بجواب يناسب مذهبهم ، والمتكلمون أجابوا عنه بجواب آخر يناسب مذهبهم . أما الفلاسفة فقد قالوا : هذا إشكال قاهر قوي ، ولا جواب عنه إلا بحرف واحد ، وذلك الحرف إنما يستقيم على قولنا : إن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر، لا إلى أول وتقريره ، أن يقال المؤثر في وجود هذه الحوادث : موجود قديم أزلي، إلا أن شرط فيضان هذا الحادث عند زوال ذلك الحادث السابق . فكل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر ، فإن انقضاء الحادث ( المتقدم )(۱) شرط في كون ذلك الموجود القديم علة لحدوث الحادث المتأخر . وعلى هذا الطريق قيل : كل حادث حادث لا إلى أول . وأما المتكلمون فقالوا : هذا السؤال ( إنما ) (۲) صعب على الفلاسفة لأنهم جوزوا حدوث حادث قبل حادث ، لا إلى أول ، وأما نحن فلا نقول به ، من المحالات ، وإذا كان كذلك وجب انتهاء الحوادث كلها إلى مؤثر عندنا وهو قديم .
Know that the philosophers answered this question with an answer suitable to their school, and the theologians answered it with another answer suitable to their school. As for the philosophers, they said: This is an overwhelming, strong problem, and there is no answer to it except with one word, and that word is only valid on our statement: that every event is preceded by another event, without a first. Its exposition: one says that the effecting cause in the existence of these events is an eternal pre-eternal existent, except that the condition for the emanation of this event is the passing away of that previous event. So every event is preceded by another event, for the passing away of the preceding event is a condition for that eternal existent to be a cause for the occurrence of the subsequent event. By this way it is said: every event occurs, without a first. As for the theologians, they said: This question is only difficult for the philosophers because they permit the occurrence of an event before an event, without a first. As for us, we do not say that; it is among the impossibilities. And since this is so, it is necessary that all events end at an effecting cause according to us, and it is eternal.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of two responses to the preceding dilemma. First, the philosophers' answer: they acknowledge the overwhelming problem but offer a solution based on their acceptance of an infinite series of events. They claim that each event is caused by an eternal existent, but only when the preceding event expires. Thus every event is preceded by another, with no first event. Second, the theologians' answer: they note that the question is only problematic for philosophers because they allow an infinite regress of events. Theologians, by contrast, consider such regress impossible. Therefore, a series of events must terminate at an eternal cause. The passage thus contrasts two worldview positions.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
حادث مسبوق بحادث
لا إلى أول
فيضان
انقضاء
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think philosophers deny causality completely. In fact, they affirm a causal chain with no first cause.
Correction: Philosophers do not deny causality; they posit an infinite regress of events with a condition for causation by an eternal being.

Checkpoint

A) The eternal existent must change its essence.
B) The previous event must pass away.
C) The new event must be necessary.
D) No condition is needed.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers solve the problem by accepting an infinite chain of events, each caused by an eternal being when the previous event ends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Theologians' Answer: All Events End at an Eternal Cause
Theologians' answer to the causation dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' argument that an infinite regress of events is impossible and that all events must terminate at an eternal cause.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; all events end at an eternal cause.

The theologians take a different approach: they argue that the problem only arises for philosophers because they accept an infinite regress. For theologians, such a regress is impossible, so the chain of events must have a beginning at an eternal cause. This contrasts sharply with the philosophers' assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologians' passage
Read the Arabic passage for the theologians' answer and its translation. Notice how they pinpoint the source of the problem.
The phrase 'من المحالات' (among impossibilities) is crucial.
2
Identify the core disagreement
The theologians state that the question is difficult only for philosophers because philosophers allow an infinite regress of events. Theologians reject this as impossible.
Understand the difference in starting assumptions.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, all events must terminate at an effecting cause that is eternal. This is the theologians' solution.
This conclusion aligns with a theistic worldview.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this answer preserves a first cause. Why might theologians see an infinite regress as problematic?

First Critique: The Philosophers' Answer is Weak
The first questioner states the philosophers' answer is weak and gives the first aspect of the critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first questioner's initial critique that the philosophers' answer fails to address the division of the cause.

Core Idea
The philosophers' answer is weak because it does not resolve the division of the cause.

This passage begins a critical response to the philosophers' argument about causality. The first questioner asserts the weakness of their answer and outlines the first reason: the division of the cause into temporal or eternal is not resolved by their words. We will examine this critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: claim, announcement of aspects, first aspect.
The passage breaks off after the first aspect; more aspects are implied.
2
Identify the claim and the first aspect
Identify the first questioner's claim that the philosophers' answer is weak. Then find the first aspect: the division from the question is not repelled.
3
Understand why this constitutes a weakness
Reflect on why failing to repel the division makes the answer weak. The division likely challenges the philosophers' position, and their answer does not address it.
This connects to the broader debate on causality.
4
Reflect on the structure of the critique
Notice that the first questioner signals multiple aspects (وجوه) but only gives the first. This sets up a series of objections.

The Objection from Daily Events
Presents a logical dilemma stemming from daily events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how daily events create a dilemma that forces either an uncaused event or an infinite regress, thereby undermining a key premise in the proof of a preponderant cause.

Core Idea
Daily events expose a fatal dilemma: either no cause for a possible, or an endless chain of causes.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the perfect preponderant cause argument. If an effect-causing agent was incomplete in some condition, the totality of conditions itself must be accounted for. The objection shows that this leads to an absurdity, threatening the entire proof. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Notice the two branches: either the totality has no cause (possible occurs without preponderant) or it has a cause (which leads to recursion).
3
Understand the recursion
If the totality has a cause, that cause is either complete or not, repeating the problem. This forces either circularity or infinite regress.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The objection concludes that daily events invalidate one of the premises in the original proof. See how this connects to the broader argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا قلنا : إن ذلك المؤثر ما كان تاما في جميع الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثرا في هذا الحادث، ثم حدث مجموع تلك الأمور بحدوث ذلك المجموع . إن كان لا سبب . فقد وقع الممكن لا عن مرجح ، وإن كان عن سبب عاد التقسيم الأول فيه، فيقتضي إما إلى إسناد المعلول الحاضر إلى علة سابقة ، وإما إلى وقوع الدور ، وإما إلى وقوع التسلسل ، فثبت أن حدوث هذه الحوادث اليومية ، يقتضي إفساد مقدمة واحدة من المقدمات المذكورة في ذلك الدليل . واعلم أن الفلاسفة أجابوا عن هذا السؤال بجواب يناسب مذهبهم ، والمتكلمون أجابوا عنه بجواب آخر يناسب مذهبهم . أما الفلاسفة فقد قالوا : هذا إشكال قاهر قوي ، ولا جواب عنه إلا بحرف واحد ، وذلك الحرف إنما يستقيم على قولنا : إن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر، لا إلى أول وتقريره ، أن يقال المؤثر في وجود هذه الحوادث : موجود قديم أزلي، إلا أن شرط فيضان هذا الحادث عند زوال ذلك الحادث السابق . فكل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر ، فإن انقضاء الحادث المتقدم شرط في كون ذلك الموجود القديم علة لحدوث الحادث المتأخر . وعلى هذا الطريق قيل : كل حادث حادث لا إلى أول . وأما المتكلمون فقالوا : هذا السؤال إنما صعب على الفلاسفة لأنهم جوزوا حدوث حادث قبل حادث ، لا إلى أول ، وأما نحن فلا نقول به ، عندنا وهو من المحالات ، وإذا كان كذلك وجب انتهاء الحوادث كلها إلى مؤثر عندنا وهو قديم .
And as for when we say: that the effect-causing agent was not complete in all the matters considered in its being an effect-causing agent for this event, then the totality of those matters came into existence with the occurrence of that totality. If there is no cause, then the possible has occurred without a preponderant, and if it is due to a cause, then the first division returns to it, leading either to attributing the present effect to a prior cause, or to the occurrence of circularity, or to the occurrence of infinite regress. So it is established that the occurrence of these daily events necessitates the corruption of one of the premises mentioned in that proof. And know that the philosophers answered this question with an answer befitting their school, and the theologians answered it with another answer befitting their school. As for the philosophers, they said: This is an overwhelming and strong objection, and there is no answer to it except for one point, and that point is only valid based on our statement that every event is preceded by another event, without a first. Its exposition is that the effect-causing agent in the existence of these events is an eternal pre-eternal existent, except that the condition for the emanation of this event is the passing away of that prior event. So every event is preceded by another event, for the expiry of the prior event is a condition for that eternal existent being a cause for the occurrence of the later event. And on this path it is said: every event occurs without a first. As for the theologians, they said: This question is only difficult for the philosophers because they allowed the occurrence of an event before an event without a first, but we do not say that; it is among the impossibilities for us. And if that is the case, then it is necessary that all events terminate to an effect-causing agent, which with us is eternal.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an objection and two answers. It first presents an objection: if an effect-causing agent (mu’aththir) was not complete in all respects for producing a given event, then the totality of those conditions comes into existence. If that totality has no cause, the possible occurs without a preponderant (tarjih). If it has a cause, the original division recurs, leading to one of three impossibilities: tracing an effect to an earlier cause, circularity (dawr), or infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus daily events corrupt one premise of the proof. The philosophers reply that every event is preceded by another without a first; an eternal agent causes each event when the prior event ceases. The theologians reject infinite regress as impossible and assert that all events must terminate in a single eternal cause.

حادث
ممكن
مرجح
دور
تسلسل

Checkpoint

A) The possible occurs without a preponderant.
B) The agent must be complete.
C) The events are eternal.
D) Circularity occurs.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that daily events can force a contradiction in the causal proof unless infinite regress or circularity is accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an event in your life that seems to have no clear cause or explanation, do you immediately search for a worldly cause, or do you turn to the ultimate Cause? What does your immediate reaction reveal about your real belief in Allah as the efficient cause of all things?
?
This lesson shows that denying a complete cause leads to absurdity. Have you ever, even for a moment, attributed something in your life to 'luck' or 'chance'? How does that implicitly contradict the principle that every possible needs a preponderant? Will you consciously repent and reassert your tawheed in causality today?
?
If you truly believe that every event has a complete cause from Allah, then why do you still worry about outcomes? Your anxiety implies that you think the cause might be incomplete. How can you actively combat this lack of trust by recalling the logical necessity of a complete cause in every daily event?

Philosophers' and Theologians' Answers
Presents two opposing answers to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct answers to the objection from daily events: the philosophers' acceptance of infinite regress and the theologians' rejection of it.

Core Idea
Philosophers accept an endless chain of events; theologians cut it short with an eternal cause.

After presenting the objection, the text now turns to how philosophers and theologians each respond. Their answers reveal fundamental differences in their views on causality and the possibility of an infinite regress. Let's compare the two.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' answer
Read the Arabic and translation for the philosophers' response. Note that they accept an endless series of events, each caused by a prior event's expiry.
2
Understand the philosophers' condition
They say the eternal agent exists, but the condition for a new event is the passing of the previous event. This avoids the need for a first event.
3
Read the theologians' answer
Read the theologians' response. They reject infinite regress as impossible and say all events must terminate in an eternal cause.
4
Contrast the two positions
Compare the two: philosophers allow an infinite chain; theologians demand a first cause. Notice how each answer rescues the argument for a preponderant.

The Dilemma of the Complete Cause
The questioner's first argument showing the weakness of the philosophers' answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first critique of the philosophers' answer: the dilemma of the complete cause and its implications for causality and pre-eternity.

Core Idea
The eternal cause cannot be both complete and produce a delayed effect.

The questioner presents a powerful argument showing that the philosophers' answer fails to resolve the original dilemma. He uses a division (taqsim) to expose a fatal flaw: if the eternal cause was complete in pre-eternity, the later occurrence of the event would be without a preponderant; if not, the new totality itself requires a cause, leading to absurdities. This critique strikes at the heart of the philosophers' causal theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the structured logical style: first a claim, then a division, then consequences.
2
Identify the two divisions
Find where the questioner splits the eternal cause into two possibilities: being complete in all relevant respects in pre-eternity or not. Mark these as the two horns of the dilemma.
3
Understand the first horn
If the cause was complete yet the event occurred later without any change, then the possible would exist without a preponderant (murajjih). This is impossible in kalam.
This is the core of the first critique: a complete cause cannot produce a delayed effect without a new factor.
4
Analyze the second horn and its four options
If the cause was incomplete, the missing conditions came later. Their occurrence must have a cause: either no cause, a prior cause, its own effect (circle), or another event (regress). Each is rationally unacceptable.
Work through each option briefly to see why it corrupts a premise of the original proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال السائل الأول : أما جواب الفلاسفة فضعيف . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إن التقسيم الذي ذكرناه في السؤال لا يندفع بهذا الكلام ، لأنا قلنا : إن ذلك المؤثر القديم، إما أن يقال : إنه كان تاما في الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثرا في وجود ذلك الحادث، أو ما كان كذلك ، فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، فنقول : إن ذلك المؤثر التام كان حاصلا في الأزل ، وما ما كان هذا الحادث موجودا ، ثم حدث هذا الحادث بعد ذلك ، مع أنه لم يتغير أمر من الأمور المعتبرة في حدوث هذا الحادث البتة ، فحينئذ يلزم رجحان الممكن لا لمرجح ، وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن كل الأمور المعتبرة في كون ذلك القديم علة لهذا الحادث ما كان موجودا في الأزل ، ثم إنه حدث ذلك المجموع ، فحدوث ذلك المجموع، إما أن يكون لا لسبب أو يكون لسبب سابق ، أو يكون علية معلول ، وهو الدور ، أو يكون علية حادث آخر ، وهو التسلسل فيثبت أنه لا بد في الجواب من التزام أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة ، وعلى كل التقديرات فإنه يفسد مقدمة من المقدمات المعتبرة في أصل ذلك الدليل
The first questioner said: As for the philosophers' answer, it is weak. Its clarification is from several aspects: First: Indeed the division we mentioned in the question is not repelled by this speech, for we said: That eternal effect-causing agent, either it is said: it was complete in the matters considered in its being an effect-causing agent for the existence of that event, or it was not like that. If the truth is the first, then we say: That complete effect-causing agent was realized in pre-eternity, and this event was not existing, then this event occurred after that, while none of the matters considered in the occurrence of this event changed at all, then the preponderance of the possible without a preponderant becomes necessary. As for the second division: that all the matters considered in that eternal being a cause for this event were not existing in pre-eternity, then that totality occurred. So the occurrence of that totality: either it is without a cause, or it is due to a preceding cause, or it is caused by its effect, which is circularity, or it is caused by another event, which is infinite regress. So it is established that in the answer it is necessary to commit to one of these four modes, and in all cases it corrupts one of the premises considered in the foundation of that proof.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is the first of several critiques against the philosophers' answer. The questioner argues that the philosophers' reply does not avoid the earlier division (taqsim) of the original objection. The eternal cause (al-mu’aththir al-qadim) was either complete (taman) in all relevant respects in pre-eternity or not. If it was complete, then since the event did not exist in pre-eternity but later occurred without any new condition, the possible would exist without a preponderant (murajjih). If it was not complete, then the missing conditions came into existence later, so the occurrence of that new totality itself requires a cause: either no cause, or a prior cause, or its own effect (circularity), or another event (infinite regress). All these options invalidate some premise of the original proof. Thus the philosophers' answer fails.

مؤثر قديم
تام
أزل
مرجح
ممكن
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' answer successfully avoids the original problem.
Correction: The questioner shows that the answer leads to either an impossible preponderance without a preponderant or one of four unacceptable consequences.

Checkpoint
In the first critique, if the eternal cause was complete in pre-eternity, what follows?
A) The event must have occurred in pre-eternity.
B) The possible would occur without a preponderant.
C) The cause must have changed.
D) The event is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first critique forces the philosophers to confront a dilemma: either the eternal cause was complete and the event's delay is inexplicable, or it was incomplete and the emergence of the missing conditions requires a regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument shows that any attempt to explain how an eternal cause produces a temporal effect without change runs into logical difficulties. In your own life, do you ever feel tension between your belief in an unchanging God and your experience of change in your relationship with Him? How do you resolve that tension without falling into the traps of preponderance without preponderant or infinite regress?
?

Are you willing to accept that God's creative act is beyond human logical categories, even if that means embracing a mystery? What comfort or discomfort does that bring you?
