The New State of Causation
The questioner's second argument against the philosophers' answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second critique against the philosophers' attempt to explain causation between a prior and later event using an eternal cause, and why it leads to a destructive dilemma.

Core Idea
The new state of causation forces a dilemma: either violation of the causal principle or infinite regress.

This lesson presents the second of three critiques against the philosophers' answer. The questioner now focuses on the eternal cause itself: if its becoming effective for the later event is a new state, then that state is an event requiring a cause. The argument then explores the unacceptable consequences, showing that the philosophers' position is internally inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the conditional structure and the alternatives presented.
2
Identify the key terms and logical structure
Identify terms like 'علة قديمة', 'حادث متقدم', 'حادث متأخر', 'مؤثر', 'حكم حادث', 'دور', 'تسلسل'. Understand the logical flow: the question about the eternal cause's effectiveness, the new state, and its consequences.
3
Reflect on the critique's impact
Consider why this critique undermines the philosophers' proof. Think about the dilemma: either the new state needs no cause (contradiction) or needs a cause (leading to circularity or regress).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب : إن تلك العلة القديمة حال ما كان الحادث المتقدم موجودا. إما كانت مؤثرة في وجود الحادث المتأخر ، ثم بعد انقضاء الحادث المتقدم ، صار مؤثرا في وجود الحادث المتأخر ؟ فيكون ذلك الموجود القديم، ثم مؤثرا في وجود هذا الحادث المتأخر حكم حادث ، فإن لم يفتقر إلى المؤثر ، فقد فسد قولكم : الحادث لا بد له من مؤثر ، وإن افتقر إلى المؤثر فالمؤثر فيه ، إن كان هو وجود الحادث المتقدم ، فقد أسندتم المتأخر : إلى عدم المتقدم ، وإن أسندتموه إلى معلوله لزم الدور ، وإن أسندتموه إلى حادث آخر ، لزم التسلسل ، والكل يهدم دليلكم
And the second aspect in clarifying the weakness of this answer: That eternal cause, when the prior event was existing, was it effect-causing in the existence of the later event? Then after the expiry of the prior event, did it become effect-causing in the existence of the later event? Then that eternal existent becomes effect-causing in the existence of this later event as a new state. If it does not need an effect-causing agent, then your statement that 'an event must have an effect-causing agent' is invalid. And if it needs an effect-causing agent, then that effect-causing agent: if it is the existence of the prior event, then you have attributed the later event to the non-existence of the prior event; and if you attribute it to its effect, then circularity becomes necessary; and if you attribute it to another event, then infinite regress becomes necessary. And all of this destroys your proof.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This is the second critique. The questioner examines the eternal cause's (al-‘illa al-qadima) relationship to the later event. While the prior event existed, was the eternal cause already effective for the later event? After the prior event ceases, the eternal cause becomes effective—this effectiveness is a new state (hukm hadith). If this new state does not need a cause, then the principle that every event needs an effect-causing agent (mu’aththir) is violated. If it does need a cause, then that cause could be: (1) the existence of the prior event, which would amount to attributing the later event to the non-existence of the prior event (thus unjustified); (2) the effect of that cause, leading to circularity; (3) another event, leading to infinite regress. All these outcomes undermine the philosophers' argument for an infinite chain of events.

Key Terms
علة قديمة
حادث متقدم
حادث متأخر
مؤثر
حكم حادث
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the eternal cause's effectiveness is eternal and unchanging, so no new state occurs.
Correction: The critique shows that the effectiveness changes with the presence/absence of the prior event, so the new state is indeed an event requiring a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second critique, if the eternal cause's becoming effective after the prior event ceases is considered a new event, what follows?
A) It must be caused by the prior event's existence.
B) It must be caused by something, otherwise the principle 'every event needs a cause' fails.
C) It is eternal and needs no cause.
D) It is caused by the later event.

Key Takeaway
The second critique shows that the eternal cause's new state of effectiveness leads to either rejecting the causal principle or accepting an infinite regress, thus undermining the philosophers' argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers assumed that the eternal cause's new state was not a separate event. But the passage shows this assumption leads to contradiction. Think of a time when you assumed something small in your faith (e.g., 'this blessing came from my effort') was not a separate act of Allah. How might this assumption be hiding a contradiction in your reliance on Him? Will you trace every new state in your life back to its true Creator starting today?
?
The argument forces the philosophers to either abandon their causal principle or accept infinite regress. In your own life, when you face a dilemma between a principle you hold and the evidence, do you honestly re-examine the principle, or do you cling to it despite contradictions? What principle in your belief about Allah's action in the world might need re-examination based on this lesson?

First Refutation: The Problem of Preponderance Without a Preponderator
First refutation: dilemma of the eternal cause's sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' assumption that the eternal cause was fully sufficient in eternity leads to the absurdity of a preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
If the cause is fully sufficient in eternity, any later event is a preponderance without a preponderator.

The philosophers argued that the eternal cause produces contingent events without change. The first refutation examines this claim by asking whether the cause was fully sufficient in eternity. If it was, the later occurrence of a contingent event would be a preponderance without any preponderator—an impossibility. If not, a new set of conditions must arise, leading to further absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how the argument is presented as a conditional dilemma.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage divides the scenario into two: either the cause was fully sufficient in eternity or not. Find each case and its consequence.
The first case leads directly to the tarjih problem.
3
Understand the four unacceptable outcomes
In the second case, the new collection of conditions must be caused. The passage lists four possibilities—no cause, prior cause, circularity, or infinite regress—all of which undermine the philosophers' proof.
These are classical reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه مؤثرا في وجود ذلك الحادث، أو ما كان كذلك، فإن كان الحق هو الأول، فنقول: إن ذلك المؤثر التام كان حاصلا في الأزل، وما كان هذا الحادث موجودا، ثم حدث هذا الحادث بعد ذلك، مع أنه لم يتغير أمر من الأمور المعتبرة في حدوث هذا الحادث البتة، فحينئذ يلزم رجحان الممكن لا لمرجح، وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن كل الأمور المعتبرة في كون ذلك القديم علة لهذا الحادث ما كان موجودا في الأزل، ثم إنه حدث ذلك المجموع، فحدوث ذلك المجموع، إما أن يكون لا لسبب أو يكون لسبب سابق، أو يكون علة معلول، وهو الدور، أو يكون علة حادث آخر، وهو التسلسل فيثبت أنه لا بد في الجواب من التزام أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة، وعلى كل التقديرات فإنه يفسد مقدمة من المقدمات المعتبرة في أصل ذلك الدليل. والوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب: إن تلك العلة القديمة حال ما كان الحادث المتقدم موجودا، إما كانت مؤثرة في وجود الحادث المتأخر، ثم بعد انقضاء الحادث المتقدم، صار مؤثرا في وجود الحادث المتأخر؟ فيكون ذلك الموجود القديم، ثم مؤثرا في وجود هذا الحادث المتأخر حكم حادث، فإن لم يفتقر إلى المؤثر، فقد فسد قولكم: الحادث لا بد له من مؤثر، وإن افتقر إلى المؤثر فالمؤثر فيه، إن كان هو وجود الحادث المتقدم، فقد أسندتم المتأخر إلى عدم المتقدم، وإن أسندتموه إلى معلوله لزم الدور، وإن أسندتموه إلى حادث آخر، لزم التسلسل، والكل يهدم دليلكم. والوجه الثالث في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب: أن نقول الحادث المتقدم، إما أن يكون فناؤه وانقضاؤه لذاته أو لغيره، فإن كان الأول كان ممتنع الوجود لذاته، فوجب أن لا يوجد البتة، وإن كان الثاني فليس ههنا سبب يزيله ويوجب عدمه، إلا طريان الحادث المتأخر فعلى هذا يكون زوال المتقدم موقوفا على طريان هذا الحادث المتقدم، وأنتم جعلتم فيضان هذا الحادث المتأخر عن العلة القديمة مشروطا باقتضاء الحادث وذلك يوجب الدور، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: أن الجواب الذي عوّل الفلاسفة عليه في دفع هذا الإشكال ضعيف.
Its being effectual in the existence of that contingent event, or not being such. If the truth is the first, we say: that complete effectual was present in eternity, and this contingent event did not exist, then this contingent event occurred afterwards, while no matter among the matters considered in the occurrence of this contingent event changed at all; then necessarily a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator follows. As for the second division: that all the matters considered in that eternal being a cause for this contingent event were not present in eternity, then that collection occurred; so the occurrence of that collection either is without a cause, or is due to a prior cause, or is a cause of an effect which is circularity, or is a cause of another contingent event which is infinite regress. So it is established that in the answer one must commit to one of these four ways, and in all estimations it corrupts a premise among the premises considered in the foundation of that proof. The second way in explaining the weakness of this answer: that eternal cause at the time when the prior contingent event existed, was it effectual in the existence of the later contingent event, then after the passing of the prior contingent event, it became effectual in the existence of the later contingent event? So that existence of the eternal entity, then being effectual in the existence of this later contingent event, is a contingent ruling; if it does not need an effectual, then your statement 'the contingent event must have an effectual' is spoiled; and if it needs an effectual, then the effectual in it, if it is the existence of the prior contingent event, then you have ascribed the later to the non-existence of the prior; and if you ascribe it to its effect, then circularity follows; and if you ascribe it to another contingent event, then infinite regress follows; and all of this demolishes your proof. The third way in explaining the weakness of this answer: we say the prior contingent event, either its annihilation and passing is by itself or by another; if the first, then it would be impossible of existence by itself, so it must not exist at all; and if the second, then there is no cause that removes it and necessitates its non-existence except the advent of the later contingent event. So on this basis, the passing of the prior is dependent upon the advent of this prior contingent event, and you have made the flow of this later contingent event from the eternal cause conditional upon the exigency of the contingent event, and that necessitates circularity. So it is established by these three ways that the answer upon which the philosophers relied in repelling this difficulty is weak.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' answer to a problem about the eternal cause and the occurrence of contingent events. It presents three arguments (wujuh) showing the weakness of the philosophers' response. The first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) begins by dividing the scenario into two cases: either the eternal cause was fully sufficient in eternity or not. If it was fully sufficient, then the later occurrence of the contingent event would be a preponderance (tarjih) of a possible without any preponderator (murajjih), which is impossible. If it was not fully sufficient, then the collection of conditions that makes it sufficient becomes a new contingent event. This new event must have a cause, leading to one of four unacceptable consequences: no cause at all, a prior cause (infinite regress), circularity (dor), or another contingent event (infinite regress). All these undermine the proof. The second argument considers the eternal cause's state at two times: when a prior contingent event existed and after it passed. If the cause was already effectual for the later event, then its becoming effectual after the prior event's passing is a new contingent event, requiring its own effectual—leading to circularity or regress. If it was not yet effectual, the same problem arises. The third argument focuses on the prior event's passing: if it perishes by itself, it would be impossible; if by another, that other is the later event, which then depends on the prior event's passing, creating circularity. All three arguments conclude that the philosophers' answer is weak.

Key Terms
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
رجحان (tarjih)
مرجح (murajjih)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' answer avoids absurdity because the eternal cause can produce multiple effects at different times without change.
Correction: If the cause is fully sufficient in eternity, any delay in the effect would require a new factor, which is a denial of the cause's completeness. The argument shows that the philosophers' answer leads to tarjih bila murajjih.

Checkpoint
In the first argument against the philosophers, what is the consequence if the eternal cause is fully sufficient in eternity?
A) The contingent event must occur immediately.
B) The eternal cause must change.
C) The occurrence of the later event would be a preponderance without a preponderator.
D) The later event must be caused by another contingent event.

Key Takeaway
If the eternal cause is complete in eternity, any later contingent event is a preponderance without a preponderator, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness events in your daily life that seem random or without apparent cause, do you ever subconsciously assume they 'just happened'? How does this lesson's demonstration of the logical impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for you to consciously trace every contingent event back to its ultimate Preponderator?
?
Think of a specific blessing or trial that entered your life unexpectedly. Can you identify a moment when you might have overlooked the true Cause behind it, attributing it to mere chance or secondary factors? How would your faith and gratitude change if you consistently acknowledged that nothing occurs without a sufficient reason rooted in the Divine?
?
Is there an area of your life—your habits, your relationships, or your spiritual practices—where you operate as though something can happen without a sufficient cause or purpose? How does the logical need for a preponderator call you to reexamine that area, and what concrete step can you take today to align your actions with the reality that every effect has a Cause?

Second Refutation: The Eternal Cause's State and the Need for an Effectual
Second refutation: dilemma of the eternal cause's effectuality over time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second refutation exposes a dilemma about the eternal cause's effectuality at different times, leading to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
The eternal cause's becoming effectual after the prior event is a new contingent ruling requiring its own cause.

The second refutation focuses on the relationship between the eternal cause and two successive contingent events. It asks whether the cause was already effectual for the later event when the prior event existed. The answer leads to a dilemma that undermines the philosophers' claim that the cause produces events without change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic excerpt and its translation. Pay attention to the question posed at the beginning.
The argument is structured as a conditional question.
2
Follow the two branches
The passage considers two possibilities: either the eternal cause was already effectual for the later event when the prior existed, or it became effectual after the prior passed.
Each branch leads to a different set of consequences.
3
Identify the logical outcomes
Trace how each branch leads to either circularity (dor) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which demolish the philosophers' proof.
The conclusion is that the philosophers' answer fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the second refutation, if the eternal cause was already effectual for the later event during the prior event, what is the status of its later effectuality?
A) It is a necessary attribute of the cause.

C) It is a non-existent property.
D) It is a simultaneous effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that every contingent event—even a new 'ruling' about the eternal cause—requires an effectual. In your life, do you treat your good deeds, or even your faith, as automatic continuations of past states, or do you recognize each moment's obedience as a fresh contingent act needing God's constant support? What changes if you truly live as if each act of worship is a new contingent event depending entirely on God?
?
When you consider the philosophers' attempt to avoid a cause for the later event, you see that they tried to anchor causality in the past. Are there areas in your life where you rely on past decisions or past grace rather than seeking God's fresh guidance each day? What would it mean for you to repent not just of sins but of living as if God's action in your life is a fixed thing rather than a continuous creation?
?
This passage forces you to admit that even God's 'being effectual' is not an automatic given but a contingent reality—God freely chooses to act. How does this challenge any sense of entitlement you might have toward God's blessings? Do you live with the humble awareness that each new moment of existence, health, or ability is a new creation from God, not something you can take for granted?

Third Refutation: Circularity in the Passing of the Prior Event
Third refutation: circularity in annihilation of prior event.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third refutation exposes circularity by analyzing the annihilation of the prior event and its dependence on the later event.

Core Idea
The prior event's passing depends on the later event, which depends on the prior event—circularity.

The third refutation examines the prior contingent event's annihilation. It asks whether the prior event perishes by itself or by something else. If by itself, it would be impossible; if by another, the only candidate is the later event. This creates a circular dependency that refutes the philosophers' answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The argument is short but packed.
Notice the use of conditional reasoning.
2
Analyze the two options for annihilation
The prior event's annihilation is either by itself or by another. Self-annihilation is impossible because it would imply self-contradiction.
Self-annihilation would mean the prior event is impossible of existence by essence.
3
Trace the circularity
If annihilated by another, the only possible cause is the later event. But the later event's existence from the eternal cause is conditioned upon the prior event's exigency. This creates a cycle where the prior's passing depends on the later, and the later depends on the prior.
Circularity invalidates the entire account.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه مؤثرا في وجود ذلك الحادث، أو ما كان كذلك، فإن كان الحق هو الأول، فنقول: إن ذلك المؤثر التام كان حاصلا في الأزل، وما كان هذا الحادث موجودا، ثم حدث هذا الحادث بعد ذلك، مع أنه لم يتغير أمر من الأمور المعتبرة في حدوث هذا الحادث البتة، فحينئذ يلزم رجحان الممكن لا لمرجح، وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن كل الأمور المعتبرة في كون ذلك القديم علة لهذا الحادث ما كان موجودا في الأزل، ثم إنه حدث ذلك المجموع، فحدوث ذلك المجموع، إما أن يكون لا لسبب أو يكون لسبب سابق، أو يكون علة معلول، وهو الدور، أو يكون علة حادث آخر، وهو التسلسل فيثبت أنه لا بد في الجواب من التزام أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة، وعلى كل التقديرات فإنه يفسد مقدمة من المقدمات المعتبرة في أصل ذلك الدليل. والوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب: إن تلك العلة القديمة حال ما كان الحادث المتقدم موجودا، إما كانت مؤثرة في وجود الحادث المتأخر، ثم بعد انقضاء الحادث المتقدم، صار مؤثرا في وجود الحادث المتأخر؟ فيكون ذلك الموجود القديم، ثم مؤثرا في وجود هذا الحادث المتأخر حكم حادث، فإن لم يفتقر إلى المؤثر، فقد فسد قولكم: الحادث لا بد له من مؤثر، وإن افتقر إلى المؤثر فالمؤثر فيه، إن كان هو وجود الحادث المتقدم، فقد أسندتم المتأخر إلى عدم المتقدم، وإن أسندتموه إلى معلوله لزم الدور، وإن أسندتموه إلى حادث آخر، لزم التسلسل، والكل يهدم دليلكم. والوجه الثالث في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب: أن نقول الحادث المتقدم، إما أن يكون فناؤه وانقضاؤه لذاته أو لغيره، فإن كان الأول كان ممتنع الوجود لذاته، فوجب أن لا يوجد البتة، وإن كان الثاني فليس ههنا سبب يزيله ويوجب عدمه، إلا طريان الحادث المتأخر فعلى هذا يكون زوال المتقدم موقوفا على طريان هذا الحادث المتقدم، وأنتم جعلتم فيضان هذا الحادث المتأخر عن العلة القديمة مشروطا باقتضاء الحادث وذلك يوجب الدور، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: أن الجواب الذي عوّل الفلاسفة عليه في دفع هذا الإشكال ضعيف.
Its being effectual in the existence of that contingent event, or not being such. If the truth is the first, we say: that complete effectual was present in eternity, and this contingent event did not exist, then this contingent event occurred afterwards, while no matter among the matters considered in the occurrence of this contingent event changed at all; then necessarily a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator follows. As for the second division: that all the matters considered in that eternal being a cause for this contingent event were not present in eternity, then that collection occurred; so the occurrence of that collection either is without a cause, or is due to a prior cause, or is a cause of an effect which is circularity, or is a cause of another contingent event which is infinite regress. So it is established that in the answer one must commit to one of these four ways, and in all estimations it corrupts a premise among the premises considered in the foundation of that proof. The second way in explaining the weakness of this answer: that eternal cause at the time when the prior contingent event existed, was it effectual in the existence of the later contingent event, then after the passing of the prior contingent event, it became effectual in the existence of the later contingent event? So that existence of the eternal entity, then being effectual in the existence of this later contingent event, is a contingent ruling; if it does not need an effectual, then your statement 'the contingent event must have an effectual' is spoiled; and if it needs an effectual, then the effectual in it, if it is the existence of the prior contingent event, then you have ascribed the later to the non-existence of the prior; and if you ascribe it to its effect, then circularity follows; and if you ascribe it to another contingent event, then infinite regress follows; and all of this demolishes your proof. The third way in explaining the weakness of this answer: we say the prior contingent event, either its annihilation and passing is by itself or by another; if the first, then it would be impossible of existence by itself, so it must not exist at all; and if the second, then there is no cause that removes it and necessitates its non-existence except the advent of the later contingent event. So on this basis, the passing of the prior is dependent upon the advent of this prior contingent event, and you have made the flow of this later contingent event from the eternal cause conditional upon the exigency of the contingent event, and that necessitates circularity. So it is established by these three ways that the answer upon which the philosophers relied in repelling this difficulty is weak.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' answer to a problem about the eternal cause and the occurrence of contingent events. It presents three arguments (wujuh) showing the weakness of the philosophers' response. The first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) begins by dividing the scenario into two cases: either the eternal cause was fully sufficient in eternity or not. If it was fully sufficient, then the later occurrence of the contingent event would be a preponderance (tarjih) of a possible without any preponderator (murajjih), which is impossible. If it was not fully sufficient, then the collection of conditions that makes it sufficient becomes a new contingent event. This new event must have a cause, leading to one of four unacceptable consequences: no cause at all, a prior cause (infinite regress), circularity (dor), or another contingent event (infinite regress). All these undermine the proof. The second argument considers the eternal cause's state at two times: when a prior contingent event existed and after it passed. If the cause was already effectual for the later event, then its becoming effectual after the prior event's passing is a new contingent event, requiring its own effectual—leading to circularity or regress. If it was not yet effectual, the same problem arises. The third argument focuses on the prior event's passing: if it perishes by itself, it would be impossible; if by another, that other is the later event, which then depends on the prior event's passing, creating circularity. All three arguments conclude that the philosophers' answer is weak.

Key Terms
فناء (fana')
حادث متقدم (hadith mutaqaddim)
حادث متأخر (hadith muta'akhkhir)
دور (dawr)
علة قديمة (illah qadimah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prior event's annihilation is a simple passing without cause.
Correction: If it passes without cause, it would be self-annihilating, which is impossible. If caused, it must be by the later event, creating circularity.

Checkpoint
In the third refutation, what is the consequence if the prior event's annihilation is by itself?
A) It would be a miracle.
B) It would be impossible for it to exist at all.
C) It would depend on the later event.
D) It would prove the philosophers' answer.

Key Takeaway
The prior event's annihilation by the later event creates a circular dependency that refutes the philosophers' account of causation.

Or do you habitually act as though you are self-sufficient, assuming things 'just happen' without a deeper cause? Let this argument remind you to trace every blessing back to its true Source.
?
When you encounter a logical difficulty in your own thinking (like a circular argument you've unknowingly accepted), do you humbly re-examine your position, or do you defend it? This passage exposes the weakness in the philosophers' answer—are you willing to expose your own weaknesses for the sake of truth?
?

Think about something in your life that has passed away: a relationship, a habit, a trial. Could it truly have vanished on its own, or was there a cause? How does this affect your gratitude or repentance?

Two Refutations of the Theologians' Answer
Two arguments exposing the weakness of the theologians' answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments that expose the weakness of the theologians' response to the problem of eternal causality and contingent events.

Core Idea
The theologians' answer leads to absurdities: impossible transformations or preponderance without a preponderator.

In this passage, the author presents two powerful refutations of the theologians' attempted solution to the problem of how an eternal cause can produce contingent events. The first focuses on the nature of possibility and its origin, while the second examines the conditions required for causation. These arguments reveal that the theologians' position is internally inconsistent and ultimately fails to resolve the difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two distinct 'wujuh' (ways) signaled by 'الأول' and 'الوجه الثاني'.
2
Identify the first argument's structure
Trace the disjunction: either the possibility of contingent events has a first event or it does not. Consider the consequences of each branch.
The argument shows absurdity in both cases.
3
Identify the second argument's structure
Trace the disjunction: either the eternal cause possessed all conditions from eternity or not. Consider the implications for each branch.
Again, both branches lead to a contradiction or regress.
4
Compare both arguments
Notice that both arguments end with the same conclusion: the theologians' answer is weak because it results in preponderance without a preponderator or an absurdity.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The passage concludes that these arguments cover the entire difficulty and its answer. Understand why the theologians' position is deemed insufficient.
This connects to the broader debate on divine causality and temporal creation.

As for the answer of the theologians, it is also weak due to considerations: The first: that the soundness of the occurrence of contingent events, either it has a first, or it does not have a first. If it has a first, then before the presence of that beginning it was impossible by itself, then it became possible by itself, and this is absurd; and supposing its acceptance, then the particularization of this transformation to that beginning would be a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator. And if we say that it is not by itself [sound] for the soundness of the occurrence of contingent events, then your statement 'the occurrence of contingent events without a first is impossible' falls. The second way in explaining the weakness of this answer: that the eternal effectual, was it comprising all the matters considered in its being effectual in the existence of the world, or was it not such? If the first, then with the presence of all those matters in eternity, the world was not present in eternity, then it occurred in what never ceases, so the possible occurred without a preponderator. And if the second, then the totality of the matters considered in effectuality is a contingent ruling, and the division returns in it. This is the sum of the discourse in expounding this tremendous difficulty and the answer to it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage critiques the theologians' answer to the problem of the eternal cause and contingent events. It presents two arguments (wujuh). The first argument begins by considering whether the possibility of contingent events has a first event or not. If it has a first, that event was impossible before its own occurrence, then became possible—which is absurd, and even if granted, the particularization of the transformation at that moment would be a preponderance without a preponderator. If it has no first event, then the theologians' claim that an infinite series of contingent events is impossible collapses. The second argument examines whether the eternal cause possessed all conditions for being effectual from eternity. If it did, the world would have existed from eternity, but it didn't—so its later occurrence is a preponderance without a preponderator. If it did not, then the set of conditions becomes a contingent entity itself, leading back to the same problem. The passage concludes by summarizing that this covers the entire discussion of this tremendous difficulty and its answer.

انقلاب (inqilab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the first argument proves that an infinite series of contingent events is possible.
Correction: The argument does not prove that; it shows that the theologians' own reasoning leads to a contradiction when combined with eternal causality.

Checkpoint

B) The eternal cause must change.
C) The first event must have a prior cause.
D) The theologians cannot prove God's existence.

Key Takeaway
The theologians' answer fails because it either leads to an absurd transformation of possibility or to a preponderance without a preponderator when the eternal cause's conditions are considered.

Weakness of the Philosophers' and Theologians' Answers
Author critiques philosophers' and theologians' responses to the problem of temporal events from an eternal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both the philosophers' and theologians' responses to the problem of temporal events from an eternal cause are considered weak in classical kalām.

Core Idea
The eternal cause cannot produce a temporal effect without leading to circularity or contingency.

This passage is a critical analysis of two major responses to a central objection in kalām: how can an eternal being cause a temporal world? The author first summarizes why the philosophers' answer fails, then provides two detailed arguments against the theologians' position. This lesson unpacks those critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the structure: first a brief mention of the philosophers' weakness, then two detailed faces for the theologians.
2
Identify the two arguments against the theologians
The first argument deals with the possibility of events having a beginning; the second with the completeness of the eternal agent's agency.
3
Understand the logical structure
Notice how each argument leads to a contradiction or an infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the core problem
The core issue is that any explanation of the transition from eternity to time seems to require a new factor, which itself would be contingent.

على طريان هذا الحادث المتقدم، وأنتم جعلتم فيضان هذا الحادث المتأخر عن العلة القديمة مشروطا باقتضاء الحادث وذلك يوجب الدور، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: أن الجواب الذي عوّل الفلاسفة عليه في دفع هذا الإشكال ضعيف. وأما جواب المتكلمين. فهو أيضاً ضعيف لوجوه: الأول: إن صحة حدوث الحوادث، إما أن يكون لها أول، وإما أن لا يكون لها أول، فإن كان لها أول، فقد كان قبل حضور ذلك المبدأ ممتنعا لذاته، ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته، وهذا محال، وبتقدير تسليمه فاختصاص هذا الإنقلاب بذلك المبدأ يكون رجحانا للممكن لا لمرجح، وأما إن قلنا: إنه لا أول لصحة حدوث الحوادث، فحينئذ سقط قولكم: إن حدوث حوادث لا أول لها: محال. والوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب: إن المؤثر القديم، هل كان مستجمعا لجميع الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثرا في وجود العالم، أو ما كان كذلك؟ فإن كان الأول فحينئذ مع حصول كل تلك الأمور في الأزل، ما كان العالم حاصلا في الأزل، ثم حصل فيما لا يزال، فقد وقع الممكن، لا عن مرجح، وإن كان الثاني فمجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية: حكم حادث. ويعود التقسيم فيه. فهذا جملة الكلام في تقرير هذا السؤال الهائل والجواب عنه. أما الفلاسفة فقد أجابوا عنه: بأن غاية هذا السؤال: أن كون المؤثر القديم مؤثرا في وجود هذا الحادث، حكم حادث فلا بد له من سبب. فنقول: مؤثرية المؤثر في الأثر ليس موجودا زائدا، وإلا لكان ممكنا، ولكان مفتقرا إلى المؤثر، فحينئذ يلزم افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر، وهو محال، فيثبت أن المؤثرية ليست موجودا حادثا، فلم يلزم افتقاره إلى السبب. وأما جواب المتكلمين فالبحث المستقصى عنه مذكور في مسألة القدم والحدوث. وبالله التوفيق.
Upon the occurrence of this preceding event, and you made the emanation of this later event from the eternal cause conditional upon the necessity of the event, and that necessitates circularity. So it is established by these three aspects that the answer upon which the philosophers relied in repelling this problem is weak. As for the answer of the theologians, it is also weak for reasons: First: The possibility of the occurrence of events, either it has a beginning or it does not have a beginning. If it has a beginning, then before the presence of that principle it was impossible in itself, then it became possible in itself, and this is impossible. And granting its acceptance, the specialization of this change to that principle would be a preponderance of the possible without a preponderant. And if we say that there is no beginning for the possibility of events, then your statement that the occurrence of events without a beginning is impossible falls away. The second aspect in explaining the weakness of this answer: The eternal agent, was it possessing all the considerations necessary for being an agent in the existence of the world, or not? If the first, then with the occurrence of all those things in pre-eternity, the world did not occur in pre-eternity, then it occurred in what will not cease, so the possible occurred without a preponderant. And if the second, then the totality of the considerations in the agency is a contingent judgment, and the division applies to it. So this is the sum of the discussion in establishing this tremendous problem and the answer to it. As for the philosophers, they answered it by saying that the extent of this problem is that the eternal agent's being an agent in the existence of this event is a contingent judgment, so it must have a cause. We say: The agency of the agent in the effect is not an additional existent, otherwise it would be possible and would need an agent, then it would need another agent, and that is impossible. So it is established that the agency is not a contingent existent, so it does not necessitate its need for a cause. As for the answer of the theologians, the detailed research about it is mentioned in the issue of pre-eternity and temporality. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage critiques both the philosophers' and the theologians' responses to a key objection in kalām: how can an eternal cause produce a temporal effect? The author first summarizes three arguments that show the philosophers' answer is weak. Then he lists two specific objections to the theologians' answer. The first objection concerns whether the possibility of events has a beginning: if it does, a thing impossible in itself becomes possible without cause; if not, the impossibility of an infinite regress is conceded. The second objection questions whether the eternal agent was complete in agency from eternity; if so, the world should have existed eternally, but it didn't; if not, agency becomes contingent. The author then presents the philosophers' actual answer: that agency is not a real, additional attribute, so it is not a contingent event requiring a cause. He refutes this by arguing that if agency were additional, it would require another agent in infinite regress. Finally, he refers the theologians' answer to another discussion. The passage concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

?

Refutation of the Philosophers' Answer Regarding Agency
Author presents and refutes the philosophers' answer regarding the nature of agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that agency is not an additional attribute and why the author argues it leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The philosophers' attempt to avoid contingency by denying agency as an attribute fails because it assumes agency is not an existent, which is indefensible.

After critiquing both groups, the author now presents the philosophers' specific answer to the problem: that the agency of the eternal cause is not an additional existent, so it does not require a cause. He then refutes this by arguing that if agency were additional, it would be a contingent being needing another agent, leading to an infinite regress. This lesson focuses on that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' answer
It claims that the agency of the eternal cause is not an additional attribute, thus not a contingent event needing a cause.
2
Understand the author's objection
He argues that if agency were additional, it would be contingent and require another cause, leading to an infinite regress.
3
Notice the logical structure
The author uses a conditional argument: if agency is additional, then it is possible and needs an agent, which leads to infinite regress, which is impossible, so agency cannot be additional.
4
Reflect on implications
This shows the difficulty of explaining how an eternal cause acts without introducing temporal attributes.

على طريان هذا الحادث المتقدم، وأنتم جعلتم فيضان هذا الحادث المتأخر عن العلة القديمة مشروطا باقتضاء الحادث وذلك يوجب الدور، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: أن الجواب الذي عوّل الفلاسفة عليه في دفع هذا الإشكال ضعيف. وأما جواب المتكلمين. فهو أيضاً ضعيف لوجوه: الأول: إن صحة حدوث الحوادث، إما أن يكون لها أول، وإما أن لا يكون لها أول، فإن كان لها أول، فقد كان قبل حضور ذلك المبدأ ممتنعا لذاته، ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته، وهذا محال، وبتقدير تسليمه فاختصاص هذا الإنقلاب بذلك المبدأ يكون رجحانا للممكن لا لمرجح، وأما إن قلنا: إنه لا أول لصحة حدوث الحوادث، فحينئذ سقط قولكم: إن حدوث حوادث لا أول لها: محال. والوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب: إن المؤثر القديم، هل كان مستجمعا لجميع الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثرا في وجود العالم، أو ما كان كذلك؟ فإن كان الأول فحينئذ مع حصول كل تلك الأمور في الأزل، ما كان العالم حاصلا في الأزل، ثم حصل فيما لا يزال، فقد وقع الممكن، لا عن مرجح، وإن كان الثاني فمجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية: حكم حادث. ويعود التقسيم فيه. فهذا جملة الكلام في تقرير هذا السؤال الهائل والجواب عنه. أما الفلاسفة فقد أجابوا عنه: بأن غاية هذا السؤال: أن كون المؤثر القديم مؤثرا في وجود هذا الحادث، حكم حادث فلا بد له من سبب. فنقول: مؤثرية المؤثر في الأثر ليس موجودا زائدا، وإلا لكان ممكنا، ولكان مفتقرا إلى المؤثر، فحينئذ يلزم افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر، وهو محال، فيثبت أن المؤثرية ليست موجودا حادثا، فلم يلزم افتقاره إلى السبب. وأما جواب المتكلمين فالبحث المستقصى عنه مذكور في مسألة القدم والحدوث. وبالله التوفيق.
Upon the occurrence of this preceding event, and you made the emanation of this later event from the eternal cause conditional upon the necessity of the event, and that necessitates circularity. So it is established by these three aspects that the answer upon which the philosophers relied in repelling this problem is weak. As for the answer of the theologians, it is also weak for reasons: First: The possibility of the occurrence of events, either it has a beginning or it does not have a beginning. If it has a beginning, then before the presence of that principle it was impossible in itself, then it became possible in itself, and this is impossible. And granting its acceptance, the specialization of this change to that principle would be a preponderance of the possible without a preponderant. And if we say that there is no beginning for the possibility of events, then your statement that the occurrence of events without a beginning is impossible falls away. The second aspect in explaining the weakness of this answer: The eternal agent, was it possessing all the considerations necessary for being an agent in the existence of the world, or not? If the first, then with the occurrence of all those things in pre-eternity, the world did not occur in pre-eternity, then it occurred in what will not cease, so the possible occurred without a preponderant. And if the second, then the totality of the considerations in the agency is a contingent judgment, and the division applies to it. So this is the sum of the discussion in establishing this tremendous problem and the answer to it. As for the philosophers, they answered it by saying that the extent of this problem is that the eternal agent's being an agent in the existence of this event is a contingent judgment, so it must have a cause. We say: The agency of the agent in the effect is not an additional existent, otherwise it would be possible and would need an agent, then it would need another agent, and that is impossible. So it is established that the agency is not a contingent existent, so it does not necessitate its need for a cause. As for the answer of the theologians, the detailed research about it is mentioned in the issue of pre-eternity and temporality. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage critiques both the philosophers' and the theologians' responses to a key objection in kalām: how can an eternal cause produce a temporal effect? The author first summarizes three arguments that show the philosophers' answer is weak. Then he lists two specific objections to the theologians' answer. The first objection concerns whether the possibility of events has a beginning: if it does, a thing impossible in itself becomes possible without cause; if not, the impossibility of an infinite regress is conceded. The second objection questions whether the eternal agent was complete in agency from eternity; if so, the world should have existed eternally, but it didn't; if not, agency becomes contingent. The author then presents the philosophers' actual answer: that agency is not a real, additional attribute, so it is not a contingent event requiring a cause. He refutes this by arguing that if agency were additional, it would require another agent in infinite regress. Finally, he refers the theologians' answer to another discussion. The passage concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

Correction: The author's conclusion that agency is not a contingent existent is based on the impossibility of infinite regress, not on acceptance of the philosophers' claim.

The Cause's Effectiveness is Not an Additional Existent
The author proves that the cause's effectiveness is not an additional entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the effectiveness of a cause is not a separate created entity, and why thinking otherwise leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
Effectiveness is not an extra thing; it's the very activity of the cause.

This lesson tackles a subtle point in Islamic causal theory: is the 'effectiveness' of a cause a distinct thing added to the cause? The author argues forcefully that it cannot be, using a simple but powerful reductio ad absurdum. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the first part: the effectiveness of the cause is not an additional existent. This is the thesis.
2
Follow the reductio
Notice the conditional: if it were additional, it would be contingent and would need a cause, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is the logical backbone.
3
See the conclusion
Therefore, effectiveness is not a temporal existent; it does not require a cause. The author also references the broader discussion on eternity and temporality.
The conclusion connects to a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : مؤثرية المؤثر في الأثر (۱) ليس موجودا زائدا ، وإلا لكان ممكنا ، ولكان مفتقرا إلى المؤثر ، فحينئذ يلزم افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر ، وهو ( محال ) (۲) ، فيثبت أن المؤثرية ليست موجودا حادثا ، فلم يلزم افتقاره إلى السبب ، وأما جواب المتكلمين فالبحث المستقصى عنه مذكور في مسألة القدم والحدوث ) وبالله التوفيق ( (٣) .
We say: the effectiveness of the cause upon the effect is not an additional existent; otherwise it would be possible and would be in need of a cause, so then it would require another cause, which is impossible. So it is established that the effectiveness is not a temporal existent, so it does not necessitate a cause. As for the answer of the theologians, the detailed investigation is mentioned in the issue of eternity and temporality. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the effectiveness (muthiriyya) of a cause on its effect is not a separate, additional entity. The argument progresses reductio ad absurdum: If the effectiveness were an additional existent, it would be contingent (mumkin) and would itself require a cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the effectiveness is not a temporal entity, so it does not need a cause. The author notes that the detailed theological response is found in the discussion on eternal versus temporal (qidam wa huduth). The passage guards against the misconception that the causal connection is a created thing.

Refuting the Objection: A Body Cannot Be Necessary Through Its Parts
Razi presents an objection that a body might be necessary due to its parts and gives the philosophers' refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that a body cannot be necessary through its parts, because the parts (hyle and form) are themselves contingent and caused.

Core Idea
Hyle and form are inseparable but neither causes the other; both are effects of a single external cause.

This passage from Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a crucial objection: even if a body is contingent in itself, could it become necessary because its parts are necessary? The philosophers respond by showing that the parts (hyle and form) are themselves contingent, being effects of a separate cause. This argument reinforces that contingency pervades the entire essence of the body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: the opponent suggests that although the body is possible in itself, it might become necessary due to the necessity of its parts (hyle and form).
The objection assumes that parts could be necessary, thus necessitating the whole.
2
Establish inseparability of hyle and form
The philosophers first prove that hyle and form are inseparable (mutalāzimān) in existence. They consider whether this inseparability is in essence or in existence, and reject the former because we can conceive one without the other.
If they were inseparable in essence, no separate proof would be needed, but a proof is given.
3
Show neither can cause the other
Then they demonstrate that neither hyle nor form can be the cause of the other: hyle is receptive, so it cannot be active; form inheres in hyle, so it depends on it and cannot be its cause.
This eliminates the possibility that one part makes the other necessary.
4
Conclude they share a single cause
Since the two are inseparable but not causally related, they must be effects of a single external cause. Anything that is an effect is contingent in itself, so the body is contingent in its entirety and in its parts.
Thus the objection fails; the body remains possible in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال الجسم وإن كان ممكنا لذاته، إلا أنه يجب لوجوب جزئه، وهو الهيولي والصورة؟ أجاب الحكماء عنه: بأنه ثبت الدليل بأنه يمتنع خلو الهيولي عن الصورة، ويمتنع خلو الصورة عن الهيولي، فيثبت كونهما متلازمين، فنقول: هذا التلازم، إما أن يكون بين ماهيتهما كما في المضافين أو بين وجوديهما. والأول باطل، وإلا لامتنع أن تعقل أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر، فكان يجب أن يفتقر في إثبات تلازمهما إلى برهان منفصل ولما بطل هذا، ثبت أنهما متلازمين في الوجود، ثم نقول يستحيل كون الهيولي علة للصورة، لأن الهيولي من حيث هي هي قابلة للصورة، فلو كانت مؤثرة فيها، لكان الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا معا، وهو محال، ويستحيل كون الصورة علة للهيولي، لأن الصورة حالة في الهيولي، فتكون مفتقرة إليها، والمفتقر إلى الشيء، يمتنع كونه علة لوجوده، فثبت أنه لا الهيولي علة لوجود الصورة، ولا الصورة علة لوجود الهيولي، وقد ثبت كونهما متلازمين في الوجود وكل شيئين متلازمين في الوجود لا في الماهية ولا يكون أحدهما علة للآخر، فلا بد وأن يكونا معلولي علة واحدة، إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لكان كل واحد منهما غنيا عن صاحبه، وعن كل ما يتفقر إليه صاحبه وذلك يمنع من القول بثبوت الملازمة فثبت أن الهيولي والصورة معلولا علة منفصلة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن لذاته، فثبت أن الجسم كما أنه ممكن الوجود بحسب تمام ماهيته، فهو أيضاً ممكن الوجود بحسب كل جزء من أجزاء ماهيته وذا تمام الكلام في هذا الدليل.
For if they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the body, although it is possible in itself, is necessary due to the necessity of its part, which is hyle and form? The philosophers answered: Because the proof has established that it is impossible for hyle to be devoid of form, and impossible for form to be devoid of hyle, so their being mutually inseparable (mutalāzimān) is established. So we say: This inseparability is either between their essences as in correlatives, or between their existences. The first is false, otherwise one of them could not be conceived while disregarding the other, and then it would be necessary to rely on a separate proof to establish their inseparability. And when this is invalidated, it is established that they are inseparable in existence. Then we say: It is impossible for hyle to be a cause of form, because hyle in itself is receptive of form; if it were effective in it, one thing would be both receptive and active, which is impossible. And it is impossible for form to be a cause of hyle, because form inheres in hyle, so it depends on it; that which depends on a thing cannot be a cause of its existence. So it is established that neither is hyle the cause of the existence of form, nor is form the cause of the existence of hyle, while their inseparability in existence has been established. And every two things that are inseparable in existence—not in essence—and neither is a cause of the other, must be the effects of a single cause. For if it were not so, each of them would be independent of its companion and of everything its companion depends on, and that would prevent affirming the inseparability. So it is established that hyle and form are effects of a separate cause, and everything that is thus is possible in itself. So it is established that the body, just as it is possible in existence with respect to its complete essence, is also possible in existence with respect to every part of its essence. And this completes the discussion of this proof.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector says: Why can't we say that the body, though contingent in itself, becomes necessary because its parts (hyle and form) are necessary? The philosophers answer: It has already been proven that hyle and form cannot exist without each other; they are inseparable (mutalāzimān). Now, this inseparability is either in their essences (as in correlatives like father and son) or in their existence. The first is false because we can conceive one without the other (we can think of hyle without thinking of form), and if they were inseparable in essence, we wouldn't need a separate proof to establish their inseparability. So they are inseparable in existence. Next, they argue that neither can be the cause of the other. Hyle is receptive of form; if it caused form, it would be both passive (receptive) and active, which is impossible. Form inheres in hyle, so it depends on hyle; that which depends on something cannot be its cause. Therefore, neither causes the other. Now, two things that are inseparable in existence but not in essence, and neither causes the other, must be effects of a single cause. If they were not, each would be independent of the other and of all that the other depends on, which would contradict their inseparability. So hyle and form are effects of a cause outside themselves (a separate cause), and anything that is an effect of another is contingent (possible in itself). Hence, the body is contingent both as a whole and in every part of its essence.

صورة
متلازمين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the parts of a body are necessary, the whole body becomes necessary.
Correction: The parts (hyle and form) are themselves contingent because they are effects of an external cause; thus the whole body remains contingent.

Checkpoint

C) Because form is more perfect than hyle.

Key Takeaway
The body's contingency is not just in its totality but also in each of its parts; nothing escapes contingency except the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are made of matter and form, both contingent. Do you genuinely feel your utter dependence on God in every moment, or do you act as if your existence is self-sufficient? What would it look like to live today with that constant awareness?
?
The argument shows that neither matter causes form nor form causes matter—they are both caused by God. In your own life, do you sometimes mistakenly think that your material situation causes your spiritual state, or vice versa, rather than seeing both as effects of God's decree? Can you identify one area where you need to realign your attribution of cause?

First Proof: Body's Possibility via Matter and Form as Co-Effects
Philosophers prove body's possibility by showing matter and form are co-effects of a separate cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers prove that body is possible in itself by showing that its two inseparable components—matter and form—must be effects of a single, separate cause.

Core Idea
Matter and form are inseparable yet neither causes the other, so they must share a single cause.

This lesson presents the first of the philosophers' proofs for the possibility of body. The argument addresses a potential objection: if body is possible, its parts might be necessary. Through a careful analysis of the relationship between matter and form, the proof demonstrates that both are effects of an external cause, establishing the contingency of body itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the initial objection
Notice the question about body's necessity due to its parts.
3
Follow the analysis of concomitance
See how the proof distinguishes essence-concomitance from existence-concomitance.
Understand why essence-concomitance is rejected.
4
Examine the mutual causation impossibility
Understand why matter cannot cause form and vice versa.
Focus on the contradictions.
5
Conclude the single cause
See how the proof concludes that both must be effects of one cause, establishing body's possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال الجسم وإن كان ممكنا لذاته، إلا أنه يجب لوجوب جزئه، وهو الهيولي والصورة؟ أجاب الحكماء عنه: بأنه ثبت الدليل بأنه يمتنع خلو الهيولي عن الصورة، ويمتنع خلو الصورة عن الهيولي، فيثبت كونهما متلازمين، فنقول: هذا التلازم، إما أن يكون بين ماهيتهما كما في المضافين أو بين وجوديهما. والأول باطل، وإلا لامتنع أن تعقل أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر، فكان يجب أن يفتقر في إثبات تلازمهما إلى برهان منفصل. ولما بطل هذا، ثبت أنهما متلازمين في الوجود، ثم نقول يستحيل كون الهيولى علة للصورة، لأن الهيولى من حيث هي هي قابلة للصورة، فلو كانت مؤثرة فيها، لكان الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا معا، وهو محال. ويستحيل كون الصورة علة للهيولى، لأن الصورة حالة في الهيولى، فتكون مفتقرة إليها، والمفتقر إلى الشيء، يمتنع كونه علة لوجوده. فثبت أنه لا الهيولى علة لوجود الصورة، ولا الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى، وقد ثبت كونهما متلازمين في الوجود. وكل شيئين متلازمين في الوجود لا في الماهية ولا يكون أحدهما علة للآخر، فلا بد وأن يكونا معلولي علة واحدة، إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لكان كل واحد منهما غنيا عن صاحبه، وعن كل ما يتفقر إليه صاحبه، وذلك يمنع من القول بثبوت الملازمة. فثبت أن الهيولي والصورة معلولا علة منفصلة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن لذاته، فثبت أن الجسم كما أنه ممكن الوجود بحسب تمام ماهيته، فهو أيضاً ممكن الوجود بحسب كل جزء من أجزاء ماهيته. وذا تمام الكلام في هذا الدليل
It may be said that body, although possible in itself, is necessary due to the necessity of its part, namely matter and form? The philosophers answered: because the proof is established that it is impossible for matter to be devoid of form, and impossible for form to be devoid of matter, so their being concomitant is established. We say: this concomitance is either between their essences as in the case of correlatives, or between their existences. The first is false, otherwise one of them could not be conceived while being heedless of the other, and it would then be necessary to resort to a separate proof to establish their concomitance. When this is falsified, it is established that they are concomitant in existence. Then we say: it is impossible for matter to be the cause of form, because matter as such is receptive of form; if it were effective in it, then one thing would be both receptive and active simultaneously, which is impossible. And it is impossible for form to be the cause of matter, because form inheres in matter, thus it is dependent on it, and what is dependent on a thing cannot be the cause of its existence. So it is established that neither is matter the cause of the existence of form, nor is form the cause of the existence of matter, and it has been established that they are concomitant in existence. And any two things that are concomitant in existence—not in essence—and neither is the cause of the other, must necessarily be two effects of a single cause; for if it were not so, each would be independent of the other and of everything on which the other depends, and that would prevent the affirmation of the concomitance. Thus it is established that matter and form are effects of a separate cause, and everything that is so is possible in itself. So it is established that body, just as it is possible in existence with respect to its complete essence, is also possible in existence with respect to every part of its essence. And this completes the discussion of this proof.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from the philosophers for the possibility of body in itself. The proof proceeds as follows: The initial question considers whether body, though possible in itself, might be necessary due to the necessity of its parts (matter and form). The philosophers answer that a proof has already established that matter and form are inseparable: matter cannot exist without form, nor form without matter. This establishes their concomitance. The proof then analyzes this concomitance. If it were a concomitance of essence (like two correlatives such as father and son), then one could not be conceived without the other, but that is not the case—we can conceive matter while ignoring form. Therefore the concomitance is in existence, not essence. Next, it examines whether matter could be the cause of form. Matter is receptive to form; if it were also active in producing form, the same thing would be both receptive and active—a contradiction. So matter cannot cause form. Similarly, form cannot cause matter because form inheres in matter and depends on it; what depends on something cannot be its cause. Since neither causes the other, and they are concomitant in existence, they must both be effects of a single, separate cause. If they had separate causes, each would be independent of the other, contradicting their necessary concomitance. Therefore matter and form are co-effects of one cause, and anything that is an effect of another is possible in itself. Hence body is possible in itself, both in its complete essence and in each part of its essence. The passage ends by stating that this completes the proof.

Key Terms
الهيولي
الصورة
المتلازمة
العلة
المعلول
الماهية
الوجود
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If body is composed of necessary parts, then body is necessary.
Correction: The proof shows that the parts themselves are contingent because they are effects of a cause, so body's composition does not entail necessity.

Checkpoint
Why must matter and form have a single cause according to this proof?
A) Because they are both simple substances.
B) Because they are concomitant in existence and neither causes the other.
C) Because matter is the cause of form.
D) Because form is the cause of matter.

Key Takeaway
Body is possible in itself because its inseparable components, matter and form, are co-effects of a single cause, making them possible as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every composite thing, including your own body, is proven to be dependent on a single cause, have you truly internalized that your very existence is not self-sufficient? How does that shape your daily reliance on Allah, not just in need but in every breath?
?
The proof shows that matter and form, though inseparable, cannot cause each other and must share one cause. When you look at the harmony of your own body—how its parts work together—do you see it as a sign of the One who holds it all together, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you truly saw every moment as sustained by that single cause?
?
Body is possible in every part of its essence. Nothing about you is self-necessary. Does your heart feel the weight of that dependency, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What is one concrete way you can acknowledge your complete need for Allah today, beyond just reciting words?

Preliminary Objections to the Philosophers' Proof
Preliminary objections and dismissals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector raises preliminary challenges to the philosophers' proof regarding the composition and causation of matter and form, and why these challenges are dismissed as already settled.

Core Idea
The early objections are dismissed because they were already refuted in previous discussions.

Before the main objection against the philosophers' proof, the objector raises several preliminary challenges concerning the composition of body, the concomitance of matter and form, and the possibility of one causing the other. Each time, the response is that the issue has already been thoroughly addressed. This lesson presents these initial objections and the way they are set aside.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: the objector concedes each point but raises a new question.
Each objection is met with 'we have already discussed this.'
2
Identify the four objections
The objector makes four distinct points: (1) composition of body, (2) concomitance of matter and form, (3) matter causing form, (4) form causing matter. List them and see how each is dismissed.
The dismissals refer to earlier sections of the work.
3
Understand the rhetorical strategy
The objector uses a dialectical style: 'sallamnā dhālika' (we grant that) then 'lā naslim' (we do not grant) or 'falā yajūz' (why not). This shows a step-by-step concession and challenge.
This style is common in kalām debates.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why these objections are raised if they are already answered. They serve to clear the ground for the main objection and test the opponent's consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه: أن يقال: أما القول بأن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة، فقد سبق البحث فيه. سلمنا ذلك، لكن لا نسلم كون الهيولى والصورة متلازمين، والكلام على دلائلهم في إثبات هذا التلازم قد سبق. سلمنا ذلك، فلم لا يجوز كون الهيولى علة للصورة؟ قوله: «لأنها قابل والقابل لا يكون فاعلا» قلنا: الكلام في أن الشيء الواحد البسيط، لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معا، سبق بالاستقصاء. سلمنا ذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الصورة علة للهيولى؟ قوله: «الصورة مفتقرة إلى الهيولى والمفتقر إلى الشيء لا يكون علة له» قلنا: قد أجبنا عن هذا الحرف في باب مباحث الهيولى والصورة. سلمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون واحد منهما علة للآخر، فلم قلتم بأنه لما كان الأمر كما ذكرتم وجب كونهما معلولي علة منفصلة؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن المضافين كما تلازما في الماهية لذاتيهما، من غير أن يكون لأحدهما تقدم على الآخر البتة، فكذلك الهيولى والصورة تلازما في الوجود لذاتيها، من غير أن يكون لأحدهما تقدم على الآخر البتة؟ ثم نقول: قولكم إنهما تلازما لكونهما معلولي علة واحدة: كلام ضعيف. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: إن عندكم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، وعلى هذا التقدير يمتنع كونها معلولي علة واحدة دفعة واحدة، بل يجب كون أحدهما واسطة بين العلة وبين الثاني، وحينئذ يرتفع القول يكون أحدهما علة للثاني من بعض الوجوه. الثاني: إنكم زعمتم بأن الصورة شريكة للعملة في المؤثرية، ولا معنى لهذا الشريك، إلا ما يكون جزء العلة، والدليل الذي ذكرتم في إبطال كون الصورة علة للهيولى قائم بعينه في إبطال كون الصورة جزء لعلة الهيولى. وهذا تمام القول في هذا الباب
And the objection to it: it may be said: As for the claim that body is composed of matter and form, that has already been discussed. We grant that, but we do not grant that matter and form are concomitant, and the discussion of their proofs for establishing this concomitance has already preceded. We grant that, but why is it not permissible for matter to be the cause of form? His statement: 'Because it is receptive, and what is receptive cannot be an agent'—we say: the discussion that one simple thing cannot be both receptive and agent together has already been exhaustively treated. We grant that, but why is it not permissible for form to be the cause of matter? His statement: 'Form is dependent on matter, and what is dependent on a thing cannot be its cause'—we say: we have already answered this point in the chapter on investigations of matter and form. We grant that it is not permissible for one of them to be the cause of the other, so why do you claim that since the matter is as you mentioned, they must be two effects of a separate cause? And why is it not permissible to say that just as the two correlatives are concomitant in essence by themselves, without one having any priority over the other, similarly matter and form are concomitant in existence by themselves, without one having any priority over the other? Then we say: your statement that they are concomitant because they are effects of one cause is weak. Its explanation is from two aspects: First: according to you, the One does not issue from It except one thing, and on this estimation, it is impossible for them to be effects of one cause all at once; rather, one of them must be an intermediary between the cause and the second, and then the claim that one is the cause of the second in some respects is removed. Second: you claim that form shares with the cause in being effective, and this partner means nothing other than being part of the cause, and the very proof you mentioned to refute form being the cause of matter stands to refute form being a part of the cause of matter. And this completes the discussion in this section.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections to the philosophers' proof and their responses. The objector first challenges the very composition of body from matter and form, but acknowledges that argument has been addressed earlier. He then denies that matter and form are concomitant, but is reminded that this has been proven previously. Next, he asks why matter cannot cause form, but the reply (that a receptive thing cannot be an agent) is defended as already settled. Similarly, he asks why form cannot cause matter, but the reply (form is dependent on matter) is also considered previously settled. The crucial objection comes when the objector grants that neither causes the other, but questions why they must have a separate cause. He proposes an alternative: just as correlatives (like father and son) are essentially concomitant without one being prior, so matter and form could be existentially concomitant by themselves without a cause. The philosopher responds that this is weak, giving two reasons. First, the philosophers' own principle (the One only issues one) implies that if they are both effects of one cause, they cannot come simultaneously; one must mediate, which would entail a kind of causality between them. Second, the philosophers claim that form shares with the cause in being effective (as part of the cause), but this contradicts their own proof that form cannot cause matter—if it were part of the cause, it would be a cause in some sense. The conclusion is that the objection fails and the original proof stands.

Key Terms
الاعتراض
الهيولى
الصورة
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these objections are new and have not been answered.
Correction: Each objection is explicitly said to have been discussed and refuted in previous sections.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the objection that matter cannot be the cause of form?
A) It is possible because matter is active.
B) Matter is receptive and cannot be an agent; this has been discussed.
C) The objection is irrelevant.
D) The objector must provide a proof.

Key Takeaway
The preliminary objections are dismissed by referring to earlier discussions where they were already refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an objection to your beliefs, do you find yourself quickly dismissing it by saying 'scholars have already answered that' without understanding the answer? What is one such objection you need to research for yourself to strengthen your faith?
?
This passage shows how prior knowledge is used to counter doubts. Are you building your aqeedah systematically, or are you relying on scattered facts? What is one foundational proof you will revisit this week to make it your own?

The Core Objection and Its Refutation
Main objection and two-part refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's alternative proposal that matter and form are self-concomitant like correlatives, and the philosopher's two-part refutation based on the principle of the One and the impossibility of form being a partner in causality.

Core Idea
Self-concomitance of matter and form is refuted by the principle of the One and the impossibility of form being a partner in causality.

After the preliminary objections are dismissed, the objector presents his main challenge: maybe matter and form are concomitant by themselves, like correlatives, without needing a separate cause. The philosopher responds with two powerful arguments: first, the principle that the One only produces one thing at a time, and second, that the objector's own proof against form causing matter also rules out form being part of the cause. This lesson unpacks this crucial exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the main objection
The objector grants that neither matter nor form causes the other, but questions why they must have a separate cause. He proposes an analogy with correlatives (like father and son) that are concomitant without one causing the other.
This is the heart of the challenge.
2
Understand the first refutation
The philosopher invokes the principle 'al-wāhid lā yaṣdur ʿanhu illā al-wāhid' (the One only issues one). If both were effects of the same cause, they could not come simultaneously; one must mediate, implying a causal relation between them.
This uses the philosophers' own doctrine against them.
3
Understand the second refutation
The objector's claim that form is a 'partner' (sharīk) in the cause's effectiveness means form is part of the cause. But the earlier proof that form cannot cause matter (because it depends on matter) also shows it cannot be part of the cause.
This exposes a contradiction in the objector's position.
4
Synthesize the outcome
The refutation shows that the objector's alternative is untenable, and the original conclusion that matter and form require a separate cause remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the objection to it: it may be said: As for the claim that body is composed of matter and form, that has already been discussed. ?
