Can Hyle Cause Form? Objections and Answers
Author raises two objections to the philosophers' denial of causality between hyle and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the philosophers' arguments that hyle cannot cause form and that form cannot cause hyle.

Core Idea
A receiver can be an agent; dependence does not preclude causality.

In this passage, the author engages with the philosophers' claim that matter (hyle) and form are mutually necessary and cannot be causally related. He first concedes their inseparability but then questions why hyle cannot be the cause of form, and why form cannot be the cause of hyle. The philosophers' replies are met with references to earlier discussions that undermine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and translation carefully. Note the structure of concession (sallamna) followed by objection.
The author uses a dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the two questions the author raises: why hyle cannot be cause of form, and why form cannot be cause of hyle. Note the philosophers' responses.
The first objection deals with the receiver-agent distinction; the second with dependence.
3
Understand the author's counters
The author does not give new arguments but refers to previous discussions. Why is this effective? Because it shows the philosophers' own principles are inconsistent.
This is a common method: appealing to previously established points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصورة متلازمين، والكلام على دلائلهم في إثبات هذا التلازم قد سبق. سلمنا ذلك، فلم لا يجوز كون الهيولى علة للصورة؟ قوله: لأنها قابل والقابل لا يكون فاعلا. قلنا: الكلام في أن الشيء الواحد البسيط، لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معا، سبق بالاستقصاء. سلمنا ذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الصورة علة للهيولى؟ قوله: الصورة مفتقرة إلى الهيولى والمفتقر إلى الشيء لا يكون علة له. قلنا: قد أجبنا عن هذا الحرف في باب مباحث الهيولى والصورة. سلمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون واحد منهما علة للآخر، فلم قلتم بأنه لما كان الأمر كما ذكرتم وجب كونهما معلولي علة منفصلة؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن المضافين كما تلازما في الماهية لذاتيهما، من غير أن يكون لأحدهما تقدم على الآخر البتة، فكذلك الهيولى والصورة تلازما في الوجود لذاتيها، من غير أن يكون لأحدهما تقدم على الآخر البتة؟ ثم نقول: قولكم إنهما تلازما لكونهما معلولي علة واحدة: كلام ضعيف. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: إن عندكم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، وعلى هذا التقدير يمتنع كونها معلولي علة واحدة دفعة واحدة، بل يجب كون أحدهما واسطة بين العلة وبين الثاني، وحينئذ يرتفع القول يكون أحدهما علة للثاني من بعض الوجوه. الثاني: إنكم زعمتم بأن الصورة شريكة للعلة في المؤثرية، ولا معنى لهذا الشريك، إلا ما يكون جزء العلة، والدليل الذي ذكرتم في إبطال كون الصورة علة للهيولى قائم بعينه في إبطال كون الصورة جزءا لعلة الهيولى. وهذا تمام القول في هذا الباب.
And form are mutually necessary, and the discussion of their proofs for establishing this mutual necessity has preceded. We concede that, so why is it not permissible for hyle to be the cause of form? His statement: 'Because it is a receiver, and the receiver cannot be an agent.' We say: The discussion that a single simple thing cannot be both receiver and agent together has preceded exhaustively. We concede that, so why is it not permissible for form to be the cause of hyle? His statement: 'Form is dependent on hyle, and something dependent on something cannot be its cause.' We say: We have already answered this point in the chapter on hyle and form. We concede that it is not permissible for one of them to be the cause of the other, so why do you say that since the matter is as you mentioned, they must be effects of a separate cause? And why is it not permissible to say: Just as two relationals are mutually necessary in essence by themselves, without one having any priority over the other at all, so too hyle and form are mutually necessary in existence by themselves, without one having any priority over the other at all? Then we say: Your statement that they are mutually necessary because they are effects of one cause is a weak statement. Its explanation is from two aspects: First, according to you, one cannot originate except one, and on this estimation it is impossible for them to be effects of one cause at once; rather, one of them must be an intermediary between the cause and the second, and then the claim that one of them is a cause for the second in some respects is removed. Second, you claimed that form is a partner to the cause in effecting (al-mu'aththiriyya), and the meaning of this partner is nothing other than being a part of the cause. And the very proof you mentioned for nullifying form being a cause for hyle stands identically in nullifying form being a part of the cause of hyle. And this completes the statement in this chapter.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that matter (hyle) and form are mutually necessary and must be effects of a separate cause. The author proceeds in an objection-and-answer format: First, he concedes (sallamna) that hyle and form are inseparable, but questions why hyle cannot be the cause of form. The philosophers' reply that hyle is a receiver (qabil) and a receiver cannot be an agent (fa'il) is countered by noting that a simple thing being both receiver and agent has been previously discussed. Similarly, the objection that form cannot be the cause of hyle because it depends on hyle is answered by referring to an earlier discussion. Second, the author concedes that neither is the cause of the other, but then asks why they must be caused by a separate cause rather than being mutually necessary in existence by themselves, like relationals (al-mudafayn). Finally, the author refutes the claim that they are effects of one cause, giving two reasons: (1) According to the philosophers' own principle 'from one only one proceeds', they cannot both be direct effects of one cause; one must be an intermediary, which would make it a cause of the other in some sense. (2) If form is a 'partner' in effecting, this means it is part of the cause, but the same argument used to deny form being a cause also denies it being a part of the cause. The passage ends the chapter.

صورة (form)

قابل (receiver)
فاعل (agent)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if hyle is a receiver, it cannot be an agent under any circumstances.
Correction: The author argues that the issue of a single simple thing being both receiver and agent has been discussed and is not universally impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author refutes two specific arguments against causality between hyle and form by referencing prior discussions that undermine the philosophers' reasoning.

Mutual Necessity Without a Cause? The Author's Alternative
Author proposes mutual necessity by themselves and refutes separate cause claim with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's proposal that matter and form are mutually necessary by themselves, like relationals, and his two-fold refutation of the philosophers' separate cause argument.

Core Idea
Mutual necessity does not demand an external cause; the philosophers' own principles contradict their claim.

After establishing that neither hyle nor form is the cause of the other, the author questions the philosophers' leap to a separate cause. He offers an alternative: just as two relationals (like father and son) are mutually dependent by essence without priority, so too matter and form can be mutually necessary in existence without a cause. He then dismantles the philosophers' counter-claim with two powerful criticisms rooted in their own philosophical principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the shift from objections to positive proposal and then refutation.
The author uses 'sallamna' again to concede, then questions the necessity of an external cause.
2
Identify the analogy with relationals
The author compares hyle and form to 'al-mudafayn' (two relationals). Understand how this analogy supports mutual necessity without a cause.
Relationals like father-son are co-dependent by definition, not by an external cause.
3
Analyze the two refutation points
The author gives two reasons why the philosophers' claim that they are effects of one cause is weak: (1) the one-only principle prevents two simultaneous effects, (2) form as partner in effecting contradicts their own argument against form being a cause.
These are internal critiques using the philosophers' own doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصورة متلازمين، والكلام على دلائلهم في إثبات هذا التلازم قد سبق. سلمنا ذلك، فلم لا يجوز كون الهيولى علة للصورة؟ قوله: لأنها قابل والقابل لا يكون فاعلا. قلنا: الكلام في أن الشيء الواحد البسيط، لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معا، سبق بالاستقصاء. سلمنا ذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الصورة علة للهيولى؟ قوله: الصورة مفتقرة إلى الهيولى والمفتقر إلى الشيء لا يكون علة له. قلنا: قد أجبنا عن هذا الحرف في باب مباحث الهيولى والصورة. سلمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون واحد منهما علة للآخر، فلم قلتم بأنه لما كان الأمر كما ذكرتم وجب كونهما معلولي علة منفصلة؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن المضافين كما تلازما في الماهية لذاتيهما، من غير أن يكون لأحدهما تقدم على الآخر البتة، فكذلك الهيولى والصورة تلازما في الوجود لذاتيها، من غير أن يكون لأحدهما تقدم على الآخر البتة؟ ثم نقول: قولكم إنهما تلازما لكونهما معلولي علة واحدة: كلام ضعيف. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: إن عندكم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، وعلى هذا التقدير يمتنع كونها معلولي علة واحدة دفعة واحدة، بل يجب كون أحدهما واسطة بين العلة وبين الثاني، وحينئذ يرتفع القول يكون أحدهما علة للثاني من بعض الوجوه. الثاني: إنكم زعمتم بأن الصورة شريكة للعلة في المؤثرية، ولا معنى لهذا الشريك، إلا ما يكون جزء العلة، والدليل الذي ذكرتم في إبطال كون الصورة علة للهيولى قائم بعينه في إبطال كون الصورة جزءا لعلة الهيولى. وهذا تمام القول في هذا الباب.
And form are mutually necessary, and the discussion of their proofs for establishing this mutual necessity has preceded. We concede that, so why is it not permissible for hyle to be the cause of form? His statement: 'Because it is a receiver, and the receiver cannot be an agent.' We say: The discussion that a single simple thing cannot be both receiver and agent together has preceded exhaustively. We concede that, so why is it not permissible for form to be the cause of hyle? His statement: 'Form is dependent on hyle, and something dependent on something cannot be its cause.' We say: We have already answered this point in the chapter on hyle and form. We concede that it is not permissible for one of them to be the cause of the other, so why do you say that since the matter is as you mentioned, they must be effects of a separate cause? And why is it not permissible to say: Just as two relationals are mutually necessary in essence by themselves, without one having any priority over the other at all, so too hyle and form are mutually necessary in existence by themselves, without one having any priority over the other at all? Then we say: Your statement that they are mutually necessary because they are effects of one cause is a weak statement. Its explanation is from two aspects: First, according to you, one cannot originate except one, and on this estimation it is impossible for them to be effects of one cause at once; rather, one of them must be an intermediary between the cause and the second, and then the claim that one of them is a cause for the second in some respects is removed. Second, you claimed that form is a partner to the cause in effecting (al-mu'aththiriyya), and the meaning of this partner is nothing other than being a part of the cause. And the very proof you mentioned for nullifying form being a cause for hyle stands identically in nullifying form being a part of the cause of hyle. And this completes the statement in this chapter.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that matter (hyle) and form are mutually necessary and must be effects of a separate cause. The author proceeds in an objection-and-answer format: First, he concedes (sallamna) that hyle and form are inseparable, but questions why hyle cannot be the cause of form. The philosophers' reply that hyle is a receiver (qabil) and a receiver cannot be an agent (fa'il) is countered by noting that a simple thing being both receiver and agent has been previously discussed. Similarly, the objection that form cannot be the cause of hyle because it depends on hyle is answered by referring to an earlier discussion. Second, the author concedes that neither is the cause of the other, but then asks why they must be caused by a separate cause rather than being mutually necessary in existence by themselves, like relationals (al-mudafayn). Finally, the author refutes the claim that they are effects of one cause, giving two reasons: (1) According to the philosophers' own principle 'from one only one proceeds', they cannot both be direct effects of one cause; one must be an intermediary, which would make it a cause of the other in some sense. (2) If form is a 'partner' in effecting, this means it is part of the cause, but the same argument used to deny form being a cause also denies it being a part of the cause. The passage ends the chapter.

Fourth Proof: Bodies Composed of Bodyness and Determination
Author presents the fourth proof for the contingency of bodies using composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for the contingency of bodies based on the composition of bodyness and determination.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because their shared bodyness and individual determination cannot cause each other.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah for the contingency of bodies. The argument analyzes the relationship between the universal attribute 'bodyness' and the specific 'determination' of each body, showing that they must be combined by an external cause. Building on the concept of composition introduced earlier, this proof demonstrates that every body depends on something else for its existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: observation, three possibilities, refutations, conclusion.
Pay attention to the key terms: الجسمية and التعين.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The author lists three logical relationships between bodyness and determination. Find them in the text: bodyness entails determination, determination entails bodyness, or neither entails the other.
These possibilities exhaust all options.
3
Understand the refutations
The first possibility is rejected because it would make all bodies identical. The second is rejected because determination is an attribute of the body and depends on it, so it cannot be a cause. Follow the reasoning closely.
The second refutation uses the principle that what depends on something cannot be its cause.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since both possibilities are false, the combination of bodyness and determination in each body must be caused by an external cause. This proves that every body is contingent in itself.
Contingent means its existence depends on a cause outside itself.
5
Note the assumption
The author notes that this proof relies on determination being a positive concept (ثبوتي), which was discussed earlier. Reflect on why this matters for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة في بيان أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها : إن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومتباينة في التعين ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة ، وكل واحد منها مركب من الجسيمة التي بها يشارك سائر الأجسام ، ومن التعين الذي به يمتاز عن سائر الأجسام. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن تكون الجسمية مستلزمة لذلك التعيين ، وإما أن يكون ذلك التعين مستلزما للجسمية ، وإما أن لا يكون كل واحد منهما مستلزما للآخر . والأول باطل ، وإلا لزم أن يكون كل جسم موصوفا بذلك التعيين ، فيكون كل جسم ذلك المعين ، هذا خلف . والثاني أيضا باطل ، وذلك لأن تعين ذلك الجسم نعت من نعوته ، وصفة من صفاته ، ونعت الشيء يفتقر إلى ذلك الشيء ، والمفتقر إلى الشيء لا يكون علة ، فثبت أنه لا الجسمية علة لذلك التعين ، ولا ذلك التعين علة للجسمية ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان وجب أن يكون اجتماع هذين القيدين معلول علة منفصلة ، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون كل جسم معين معلولا لعلة منفصلة ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن لذاته. واعلم أن هذه الحجة، مبينة على أن التعين مفهوم ثبوتي، والكلام فيه قد تقدم في هذا الباب.
The fourth proof for establishing that bodies are contingent in themselves: Bodies are equal in bodyness (al-jismiyya) and distinct in determination (al-ta'ayyun). That by which they share is not that by which they differ. Each one of them is composed of the bodyness by which it shares with other bodies, and of the determination by which it is distinguished from other bodies. If this is established, we say: Either bodyness entails that determination, or that determination entails bodyness, or neither entails the other. The first is false, otherwise it would follow that every body is described by that determination, so every body would be that specific thing; this is absurd. The second is also false, because the determination of that body is a quality (na't) of its qualities and an attribute (sifa) of its attributes, and a quality of a thing is dependent on that thing, and what is dependent on a thing cannot be a cause. So it is established that bodyness is not the cause of that determination, nor is that determination the cause of bodyness. Since these two possibilities are invalid, it is necessary that the conjunction of these two conditions is the effect of a separate cause. And if so, it is necessary that every determined body is an effect of a separate cause, and everything like that is contingent in itself. And know that this proof is based on the fact that determination is a positive concept (thubuti), and the discussion of that has preceded in this chapter.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the contingency of bodies. It begins by observing that bodies share the common attribute of 'bodyness' (الجسمية) but differ in their individual 'determination' (التعين). Since sharing and differing are due to different aspects, each body is composed of these two aspects. The argument then examines three possibilities regarding the relationship between bodyness and determination: (1) bodyness entails determination, (2) determination entails bodyness, or (3) neither entails the other. The first is rejected because if bodyness entailed a specific determination, then all bodies would have that same determination, which is false. The second is rejected because determination is an attribute of a body, and an attribute depends on its subject; therefore, determination cannot be the cause of bodyness. Therefore, the combination of bodyness and determination in a particular body must be caused by an external cause. Hence each body is contingent (ممكن الوجود), depending on a cause outside itself. The author adds a note that this proof assumes determination is a positive concept, which was discussed earlier.

Key Terms
الجسمية
التعين
المشاركة
الممايزة
مركب
معلول
علة
ممكن
ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument might be misunderstood as denying that bodyness itself is caused; rather it shows that the particular composition is caused.
Correction: The proof does not claim that the attribute of bodyness is contingent, but that the combination of bodyness and determination in each individual body requires an external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that determination is the cause of bodyness?
A) Because determination is a quality of a body and depends on it, so it cannot be a cause
B) Because all bodies share the same determination
C) Because bodyness is prior to determination
D) Because determination is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
Bodies are contingent because the combination of bodyness and determination in each body requires an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a composite of bodyness and your unique determination. If both are contingent and require an external cause, what does that mean for your own existence? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or are you acknowledging your dependence on the Creator every moment?
?
The proof shows that the very composition of your body—its shared nature and its individuality—cannot come from itself. How does this truth affect the way you view your body and your life? Is there any part of your daily routine that reflects this deep contingency?
?
If every determined body needs a cause, and that cause is ultimately the Necessary Being, then how should this shape your relationship with your own body and with the One who created it? Are you using your body in a way that honors its purpose as a sign of Allah's power and creativity?

The Fifth Proof: From Divisibility to Contingency
Author presents a syllogism on body contingency via divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the fifth proof for the contingency of bodies based on divisibility.

Core Idea
Everything extended is divisible; divisible implies composite; composite implies contingent.

In this passage, the author presents a concise syllogism that establishes the contingency of all bodies. The argument moves from the nature of extended things to their divisibility, then to composition, and finally to contingency. Each premise rests on deeper proofs discussed elsewhere in kalam, highlighting the interconnectedness of theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three premises
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the proof is structured as a chain: every extended thing is divisible, every divisible thing is composite, every composite thing is contingent.
The conclusion is that every body is contingent.
2
Examine the support for each premise
The author indicates that the first premise is proven by arguments against the indivisible part. The second premise is definitional (divisible means having parts). The third premise relies on a previously established proof.
Each premise has deep investigations referenced.
3
Understand the logical flow
Trace the reasoning: extendedness leads to divisibility, which leads to composition, which leads to contingency. This necessary chain shows that bodies cannot be necessary in themselves.
This proof is one of several establishing the contingency of the physical world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في إثبات أن الأجسام ممكنة لذواتها : أن نقول : إن كل متحيز منقسم بالدلائل المذكورة في مسألة نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، وكل منقسم فهو مركب ، وكل مركب فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته بالدليل الذي سبق ذكره مرارا ، ينتج أن كل جسم ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وفي كل واحد من هذه المقدمات الثلاثة مباحث عميقة .
The fifth proof for establishing that bodies are contingent in themselves: We say: Every extended thing (mutaḥayyiz) is divisible by the proofs mentioned in the issue of negating the indivisible part. Every divisible thing is composite. Every composite thing is contingent in itself by the proof that has been mentioned repeatedly. It follows that every body is contingent in itself. And in each of these three premises there are deep investigations.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents a concise syllogism. The major premise: every extended thing (mutaḥayyiz) is divisible, proven by arguments against atomism (the indivisible part). The minor premise: every divisible thing is composite (composed of parts). The conclusion: every composite thing is contingent, which was previously established. Therefore, every body (which is extended) is contingent. The author notes that each of these premises has deep discussions elsewhere.

Key Terms
متحيز
منقسم
جزء لا يتجزأ
مركب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that the divisibility of extended things is self-evident and requires no proof.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the first premise is proven by arguments against the indivisible part, indicating that kalam requires rigorous justification even for seemingly obvious claims.

Checkpoint

A) Every body is composite
B) Every extended thing is divisible
C) Every composite is contingent
D) Every body is contingent

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof establishes that bodies are contingent by showing they are extended, thus divisible, thus composite, and compositeness entails contingency.

The Sixth Proof: From the Principle of the One to Contingency of Bodies
Author presents the sixth proof for contingency based on the principle of the one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle 'from the one only one proceeds' leads to the conclusion that bodies are contingent.

Core Idea
If an essence necessitated two effects, it would not be one — so bodies cannot be necessary.

This proof introduces a subtle argument based on the philosophical principle of the one. It examines whether a single essence can necessitate both receptivity and necessary existence. The answer leads to a clear conclusion about contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the logical progression.
2
Identify the key premises
The proof rests on three key points: (1) essences necessitate receptivity, (2) if they also necessitated existence, they would necessitate two things, (3) this violates the principle that from one only one proceeds. Find these in the text.
The principle of the one is central.
3
Understand the impossibility
Why is it impossible for one essence to necessitate two distinct things? Reflect on the principle that a simple cause produces a simple effect. If the essence were necessary, it would cause both receptivity and existence, which are two different attributes.
This is a standard argument against the necessity of composite beings.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the essence cannot necessitate both, and it does necessitate receptivity, it cannot necessitate existence. Therefore, existence is not from the essence itself, so the body is contingent.
5
Reflect on the basis
The proof depends on viewing receptivity and necessity as positive attributes. Discuss why this matters for the argument.
If receptivity were not a positive attribute, the argument would collapse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة في إثبات أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها : أن نقول : لا شك أن ماهيات الأجسام قابلة للصفات والأعراض ، فهذه الماهيات مقتضية لهذا القبول ، فلو كانت مقتضية لوجوب الوجود أيضاً ، لزم كون تلك الماهيات مقتضية لأمرين : أحدهما : قبول الصفات . والثاني : وجوب الوجود . وهذا محال لما ثبت أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، وهذا الدليل مبني على أن القبول صفة وجودية وعلى أن الوجوب صفة وجودية وعلى أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد .
The sixth proof for establishing that bodies are contingent in themselves: We say: There is no doubt that the essences (mahiyyat) of bodies are receptive to attributes and accidents. So these essences necessitate this receptivity. If they also necessitated necessary existence, then those essences would necessitate two things: one is the receptivity of attributes, and the second is necessary existence. This is impossible because it has been established that from the one only one proceeds. And this proof is based on the fact that receptivity is a positive attribute, that necessity (al-wujud) is a positive attribute, and that from the one only one proceeds.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This proof argues that bodies have essences that are receptive to accidents. This receptivity is a property of the essence. If the essence were also necessary (i.e., requiring existence by itself), then the essence would necessitate two effects: receptivity and necessary existence. But the principle that 'from the one only one proceeds' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد) is a philosophical axiom, especially in the context of emanation. Since the essence would then produce two distinct things, it cannot be a simple one. Therefore, the essence cannot necessitate both; thus it cannot necessitate necessary existence, so it must be contingent. The proof relies on understanding receptivity and necessity as positive attributes and the principle of unity of effect.

Key Terms
ماهيات
قابلة
صفات
أعراض
مقتضية
وجوب الوجود
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle 'from the one only one proceeds' is about Allah's unity and direct creation.
Correction: This principle is a general philosophical axiom used here to argue about the nature of essences, not directly about divine creation. It is about the causal simplicity.

Checkpoint
What is the impossibility that the proof points to?
A) That bodies have essences
B) That one essence would necessitate two different things
C) That accidents exist
D) That receptivity is positive

Key Takeaway
The principle 'from the one only one proceeds' shows that a single essence cannot be the source of two distinct necessary effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you ever feel as though you are self-sufficient—that your being is necessary? How does this proof that bodies are contingent challenge that assumption, and what does it demand you acknowledge about your relationship with the Creator?
?
The principle 'from the one only one proceeds' reveals that a simple essence cannot produce two distinct effects. Look at your own heart: are you torn between multiple loyalties, desires, and fears? How does this principle call you to simplify your life to a single devotion to Allah?
?
If your essence is not self-necessary but contingent, then every moment of your existence is a gift. What would it mean for you to live today fully aware that your next breath is not owed to you but is a direct act of divine grace?

Seventh Proof: Accidents Depend on External Causes
Author presents the seventh proof for contingency using inevitable accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof for the contingency of bodies based on their inevitable accidents and their dependence on an external cause.

Core Idea
Bodies' inevitable accidents prove they need an external cause, hence they are contingent.

This lesson presents the seventh proof from Imam al-Razi's Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The argument focuses on the fact that every body is always accompanied by certain accidents, such as magnitude and location. Since these accidents cannot be caused by the body's essence itself, they must come from an external cause, demonstrating the body's contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's reasoning unfold.
Notice the logical sequence: premises leading to conclusion.
2
Identify the key premises
The passage states two main premises: (1) every body is never free from certain accidents (magnitudes and locations); (2) these accidents are not caused by bodyness itself.
The second premise is argued by absurdity: if bodyness caused them, all bodies would have the same accidents.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Given that bodies are never free from accidents that require an external cause, bodies themselves depend on an external cause for their existence.
This leads to the conclusion that bodies are contingent (mumkin li dhatihi).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this proof is one of several ways a philosopher can establish the contingency of bodies. It relies on the observed variability of accidents.
The conclusion is that bodies are not self-sufficient; they require a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة في بيان أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها : أن تقول : كل جسم فإنه يمتنع خلوه عن نقص في الأعراض ، وهي المقادير ، والحصول في الأحياز، والمقتضى الحصول هذه الأعراض ليست هي الجسمية ، وإلا لزم مساواة الجسمية للأجسام في هذه الصفات ، فالمقتضى لها غير ذواتها . فنقول : الجسم ممتنع الخلو عن هذه الصفات ، وحصول هذه الصفات متوقف على الغير، فالجسم ممتنع الخلو عما يفتقر إلى السبب المنفصل ، وما كان كذلك كان مفتقرا إلى سبب منفصل ، فيكون ممكنا لذاته . فهذا مجموع الطرق التي يقدر الفيلسوف على التمسك بها في إثبات أن الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها .
The seventh proof for establishing that bodies are contingent in themselves: You say: Every body is necessarily never free from some accidents, such as magnitudes (al-maqadir) and occupying spaces (al-husul fi al-ahiyaz). The cause of obtaining these accidents is not bodyness itself, otherwise bodyness would equal bodies in these attributes. So that which causes them is other than their essences. We say: The body is necessarily never free from these attributes, and the attainment of these attributes depends on something else. So the body is necessarily never free from what depends on a separate cause. And whatever is like that is dependent on a separate cause, so it is contingent in itself. So this is the totality of the ways that the philosopher can hold to in establishing that bodies are contingent in themselves.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This proof argues that bodies are always accompanied by certain accidents like magnitude and location. These accidents cannot be caused by bodyness itself, because bodyness is common to all bodies, yet the specific magnitudes and locations differ. Therefore, the cause of these accidents must be external to the body's essence. Since a body inevitably has such accidents, and these accidents require an external cause, the body itself depends on an external cause for its existence. Hence, it is contingent. The author concludes that these are the various arguments a philosopher can use to prove the contingency of bodies.

Key Terms
أعراض
مقادير
أحياز
مقتضى
سببا منفصلا
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The accidents themselves prove contingency simply because they are present.
Correction: The argument does not rely on the mere presence of accidents but on the fact that they require an external cause, which then makes the body dependent on that cause.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, why can't bodyness be the cause of the accidents?
A) Because bodyness is not real
B) Because bodyness is common to all bodies while accidents differ
C) Because accidents are eternal
D) Because bodyness is made of accidents

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that because bodies inevitably have accidents that require an external cause, bodies themselves are contingent.

?
The proof shows that your body’s very existence depends on an external cause. Does that realization change how you see your daily acts of worship? How can you actively commemorate your contingency in your next prayer or action?
?
Consider the accidents that are inseparable from you—your shape, your location. They are not from your essence. What does that say about your control over your own life? In what area of your life do you need to admit your dependence on God more deeply, and what specific step will you take today to act on that admission?

Four False Explanations for Bodies' Qualities
The author refutes four false explanations for the diverse qualities of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four false views on the cause of bodies' qualities and why they are rejected, leading to the correct view that qualities are caused by a separate non-corporeal existent.

Core Idea
The cause of a body's qualities cannot be internal or another body—only a separate non-corporeal existent suffices.

The author refutes four possible explanations for why each body has specific qualities. After demonstrating the impossibility of each, he concludes that the true cause must be a separate, non-corporeal being. This passage is a classic example of theological argumentation eliminating alternatives to establish the correct view.

Guided Path
1
First false view: powers in the body
Read the refutation of the view that qualities are caused by powers (quwā) subsisting within the bodies. Understand why this leads to infinite regress.
2
Second false view: materials and receptacles
Examine the rejection of the view that materials and receptacles cause qualities. Note the categorical proof that a body cannot be a locus in a receptacle.
3
Third false view: another separate body
Consider the argument against the view that another body causes the qualities. Grasp why this leads back to the same problem of specification.
4
Fourth false view and conclusion
See the refutation of the view that other qualities in other bodies cause these qualities. Then note the conclusion: the correct cause is a separate non-corporeal existent.
The text says 'second division' but context shows it's a new view, not among the four false ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جسمانيا ، فهذه بأسرها سوى القسم الأخير باطلة . فبقي أن يكون الحق هو هذا القسم.
وأما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : المقتضى ) الحصول (۱) هذه الصفات قوى حالة في هذه الأجسام : فنقول : هذا محال . لأن الأجسام كما أنها بعد تماثلها في ماهياتها قد اختلفت في الأعراض ، فكذلك قد اختلفت في القوى الموجبة لتلك الأعراض وذلك يوجب أن تكون تلك القوى ( موجبة ((۲) لقوى أخرى إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال .
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الموجب : لهذه الصفات المختلفة مواد الأجسام وقوابلها ، فهذا أيضا محال. لأنا أقمنا البرهان اليقيني على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون حالا في محل ، وحاصلا في قابل
•
والقسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : المقتضى لهذه الصفات جسم آخر مباین . فهذا أيضا محال. لأن هذا الجسم يكون لا محالة مخصوصا بالصفات التي لأجلها صار مؤثرا في سائر الأجسام ومديرا لها ، والتقسيم الأول يعود في سبب اختصاص ذلك الجسم بتلك الصفات
والقسم الرابع : وهو أن يقال : المقتضى الحصول هذه الصفات في هذه الأجسام صفات أخرى حالة في الأجسام الأخر (٣) ، فهذا : أيضا باطل لعين الدليل المذكور ، ولما بطل القول بهذه الأقام، ثبت أن الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن اختصاص كل جسم بصفته المعينة، إنما كان لأجل موجود مباين، ليس بجسم ولا جسماني .
Corporeally, so these entirely except the last division are false. So the truth remains this division. As for the first division: that it is said: the requirer of (obtaining) these qualities is powers subsisting in these bodies. So we say: this is impossible. Because bodies, just as after their similarity in their essences they have differed in accidents, so too have they differed in the powers necessitating those accidents, and that necessitates that those powers necessitate other powers ad infinitum. And that is impossible. As for the second division: that it is said: the necessitator of these different qualities is the materials of bodies and their receptacles. So this also is impossible. For we have established the certain proof that it is impossible for a body to be a locus in a place and to be subsisting in a receptacle. And the third division: that it is said: the requirer of these qualities is another separate body. So this also is impossible. Because this body would necessarily be specified with the qualities due to which it became effective in other bodies and manager of them, and the first division returns concerning the cause of the specification of that body with those qualities. And the fourth division: that it is said: the requirer of obtaining these qualities in these bodies is other qualities subsisting in other bodies. So this also is false due to the very same proof mentioned. And when the statement with these divisions became false, it was established that the truth is the second division, which is that the specification of each body with its particular quality is only due to a separate existent, not a body nor corporeal.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage refutes four false explanations for why each body has its specific qualities (like heat, cold, etc.). The author presents four divisions and rejects each. The first division claims the qualities are caused by powers inherent in the bodies. The objection is that since bodies are similar in essence but different in accidents, the powers would also differ, leading to an infinite regress of powers. The second division claims the materials and receptacles of bodies cause the qualities. This is rejected because a body cannot be a substrate (ḥall) in a receptacle; it is a categorical impossibility. The third division claims another separate body causes these qualities. This is rejected because that other body would itself be specified by qualities, leading to the same regress as before. The fourth division claims that other qualities in other bodies cause these qualities. This is rejected by the same proof. After all four are refuted, the author concludes that the correct view is that each body's specific quality is due to a separate, non-corporeal existent (mawjūd mubāyin), neither a body nor corporeal. Note: The text says 'the second division' but from the context it should refer to the correct view, which is not one of the four; this appears to be a textual error.

Key Terms
قوى (quwā)
مواد (mawādd)
قوابل (qawābil)
مباين (mubāyin)
حال (ḥall)
محل (maḥall)
تماثل (tamāthul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth view (qualities in other bodies) is the same as the first (powers in the body).
Correction: The first view attributes causation to powers within the same body, while the fourth attributes it to qualities in other bodies. The refutation is similar (infinite regress) but the views are distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the first false view refuted?
A) Qualities are due to powers in the bodies
B) Qualities are due to materials
C) Qualities are due to another body
D) Qualities are due to other qualities in other bodies

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that each body's specific quality is caused by a separate, non-corporeal existent (mawjūd mubāyin), not by internal powers, materials, another body, or other qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute the characteristics of your body – your health, strength, or even your intelligence – purely to physical causes, forgetting that the ultimate cause is a non-corporeal Creator who specifies each body? How would your daily life change if you genuinely recognized that every quality is a direct gift from Him?
?
The author argues that internal powers cannot be the ultimate cause because it leads to infinite regress. In your life, do you rely on your own abilities as if they are self-sufficient, ignoring the One who grants those powers? What does this lesson teach you about where true causation lies?

When you face a situation where you feel limited by your physical nature – illness, weakness, or inability – do you seek help from the non-corporeal source, or do you despair in the material means? Reflect on a time when you overlooked the role of the unseen cause and what you should have done instead.

Fifth Counter-Argument: Bodily Origination and Capacity
Fifth refutation: bodies are contingent at origination but not at continuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth refutation against the argument for a creator, which shows that bodies are capable of being acted upon only at origination, not at continuance, due to the impossibility of actualizing what is already actual.

Core Idea
What already exists cannot be made to exist again — that would be impossible.

In this final refutation, the author presents a logical puzzle: if bodies are equal in being bodies, why are they subject to different rulings regarding divine capacity? The key insight is the principle 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (actualizing what is actual) is impossible. This counter-argument must be answered for the original proof to hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the argument has three parts: claim, reason, and concluding remark.
2
Identify the claim about bodies
The passage claims that bodies at origination are maqdūra, but at continuance they are not. Why? Because you cannot bring into existence what already exists.
3
Understand the reason: Taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil
This key term means 'actualizing what is already actual.' It is logically impossible because it would require re-creating something that already exists.
This is a fundamental principle in kalam: the act of creation applies only to bringing into existence, not maintaining it.
4
Connect to the broader debate
The author concludes that these five refutations must be answered for the opponent's proof to stand. Reflect on why this specific refutation is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : وهو أن الأجسام حال حدوثها مقدورة وحال بقائها غير مقدورة لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال مع أن الجسم الحادث مساو للجسم الباقي . فهذه نقوض لا بد من الجواب عنها ليتم دليلكم.
Fifth: That bodies at the time of their origination are capable of being acted upon (maqdūra), while at the time of their continuance they are not capable of being acted upon, because to actualize what is already actual (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) is impossible. Yet the originated body is equal to the permanent body. So these are refutations that must be answered in order for your proof to be complete.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This is the fifth and final refutation in the series. The author points out that bodies, when they first come into existence, are capable of being created (maqdūra) by an agent, because they are not yet present. But after they exist and continue, they are no longer capable of being brought into existence again, because that would entail re-actualizing what is already actual (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. Yet the originated body and the permanent body are equal in being bodies (i.e., they share the generic attribute). Despite this equality, they have different rulings regarding being acted upon. This shows that generic equality does not force equality in all rulings. The author concludes that these five refutations must be answered for the opponent's proof to stand.

Key Terms
جسم
حدوث
بقاء
مقدورة
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument denies God's power over existing things.
Correction: The argument only refers to the act of bringing into existence (taḥṣīl), not to other types of divine action like sustaining or annihilating.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth refutation, why are bodies not capable of being acted upon during their continuance?
A) Because they are eternal.
B) Because it is impossible to actualize what is already actual.
C) Because God does not have power over them.
D) Because they are equal to originated bodies.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are only capable of being brought into existence at the moment of origination, not after they exist, because actualizing what is already actual is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defending Infinite Regress: Three Examples
Third objection: specificity by nature leads to infinite regress; three arguments showing regress is not impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of infinite regress against causal specificity is countered using examples from mathematics, relational attributes, and divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is not automatically impossible; examples show it can be acceptable.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology: whether attributing specific properties to a nature leads to an infinite regress, and whether such regress is inherently impossible. The author provides three counterexamples to argue that infinite regress may be permissible, challenging the opponent's assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The opponent asks: why can't we say each body's specific accident is caused by a power and nature within it? This seems intuitive, but the opponent points out it leads to infinite regress.
Notice the structure: objection is posed, then a response, then evidence.
2
Understand the counter-response
The author does not deny the regress; instead, he asks why regress is considered impossible. This shifts the burden of proof.
The three examples that follow are meant to show that regress is not intrinsically absurd.
3
Examine the first example: numerical relations
The number one is half of two, third of three, quarter of four, and so on infinitely. This is a coherent infinite regress in mathematics.
4
Examine the second example: relational attributes
Relational attributes (like 'father') are real accidents; their occurrence in a locus involves further relations, leading to regress. Yet such regress is accepted.
5
Examine the third example: divine knowledge
God knows things, knows that He knows them, knows that He knows that He knows, and so on to infinity. This regress is actual and not impossible.
This example shows that infinite regress can be real and acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل جسم إنما اختص بالصفة المعينة لأنه حصل في ذلك الجسم قوة وطبيعة ، أوجبت ذلك العرض . قوله : و السؤال عائد في اختصاص ذلك الجسم بتلك الطبيعة فيلزم التسلسل ، قلنا : يلزم التسلسل . فلم قلتم : إنه محال ؟ والذي يدل على جوازه وجوه : الأول : إن الواحد نصف الاثنين ، وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة ، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية . الثاني : إن الصفات الاضافية ، قد ثبت بالدليل أنها موجودات حاصلة في الأعيان ، ثم إنها من قبيل الأعراض ، وكون حصولها في محالها إضافات مغايرة لذواتها ، وذلك يوجب التسلسل . الثالث : إنا بينا أنه تعالى عالم بالمعلومات ، وعالم بكونه عالما بها ، وكذلك في الدرجة الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وذلك غير التسلسل.
Third question: Why is it not permissible to say: Every body is distinguished by its specific attribute because in that body there occurred a power (quwwa) and nature (ṭabī‘a) that necessitated that accident? His statement: But the question returns to the distinction of that body by that nature, so an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. We said: Yes, an infinite regress follows. So why do you say it is impossible? And what indicates its permissibility are several aspects: First: The one is half of two, and a third of three, and a quarter of four, and so on to infinity. Second: Relational attributes (al-ṣifāt al-iḍāfiyya) have been proven by evidence to be existents occurring in reals. Then, they are of the category of accidents, and the fact that their occurrence in their loci is additional relations different from the loci themselves necessitates an infinite regress. Third: We have shown that God, exalted, is knower of known things, and knower that He is knower of them, and similarly in the third and fourth degree to infinity, and that is not an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (السؤال الثالث) from an opponent. The opponent asks: why can't we say that each body's specific accident is caused by a power and nature within that body? The response (likely from the author's perspective) is that this leads to an infinite regress because the nature itself would need to be explained. But then the author counters: why do you consider infinite regress impossible? He lists three arguments showing that infinite regress is not intrinsically impossible. First example: numerical relations – one is half of two, a third of three, a quarter of four, and so on infinitely. Second: relational attributes (al-ṣifāt al-iḍāfiyya) are real accidents, and their occurrence in loci involves additional relations, leading to infinite regress. Third: God's knowledge – God knows things, and knows that He knows them, etc., to infinity, and this is not an impossibility. Thus, the opponent's objection based on infinite regress is not valid.

طبيعة
تسلسل
صفات إضافية
عالم بالمعلومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage asserts that infinite regress actually occurs in the physical world or that it is always acceptable.
Correction: The passage only argues that infinite regress is not intrinsically impossible; it does not claim that all regresses are actual or acceptable.

Checkpoint
Which example does the author give to show that infinite regress is not impossible?
A) The number one is half of two, etc.
B) The body's nature necessitates the accident.
C) God's attributes are eternal.
D) All bodies are composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The author uses three examples to argue that infinite regress is not inherently impossible, thus defending causal specificity without logical contradiction.

?

The Free Agent Faces the Same Objection
Mutakallim turns the objection back on the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mutakallim argues that the objection of preponderance without a reason applies equally to the free agent.

Core Idea
The free agent cannot avoid the specification problem without a reason.

The objector suggests that the cause of qualities might be a necessary agent, avoiding the need for choice. The Mutakallim responds by showing that this very objection—that specification requires a preponderating factor—applies just as forcefully to the free agent. The argument hinges on the equality of bodies in essence. This lesson traces that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the initial response
Read the opening question and the statement 'His statement: The relation of that necessitator...' and the reply 'We said: This same thing...'. Notice how the Mutakallim immediately claims the objection applies to the free agent.
2
Analyze the two possibilities for recipients
The Mutakallim divides the recipients into either equal or unequal in suitability. Follow his reasoning that equality forces arbitrary choice, and inequality contradicts the equality of bodies.
The equality of bodies in essence is a key premise.
3
Reach the conclusion
After dismissing inequality, the only option is equality. Then the free agent's specification is just as arbitrary as the necessary agent's. The objection is thus turned back on the objector.
The phrase 'so it is established' signals the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر في حصول هذه الصفات المعينة في هذه الذوات المعينة موجبا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار ؟ قوله : ( نسبة ذلك الموجب إلى الكل واحدة ، فيمتنع حصول الرجحان ، قلنا : هذا أيضا بعينه لازم في الفاعل المختار ، فإن تلك القوابل إما أن تكون متساوية في قابلية الصفات من غير أن يكون لبعضها رجحان على الباقي في قابلية تلك الصفات ، وإما أن يكون بعضها أولى ببعض تلك الصفات من بعض ، والثاني باطل لأن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية ، وكونها كذلك يمنع من الاختلاف في هذه الأولوية ، وإن جاز ذلك : فلم لا يجوز اختلافها في سائر الصفات مع كونها متساوية في الماهية ؟ ولما بطل هذا القسم ، تعين الأول ، وإذا كان كذلك كانت نسبة الفاعل المختار إلى كل تلك الأجسام بالسوية ونسبة كل تلك الأجسام إليه بالسوية ، وحينئذ يكون تخصيص ذلك الفاعل على بعض تلك الأجسام ببعض تلك الصفات رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن من غير مرجح ، وهو محال . فيثبت أن الذي أوردتموه على الفلاسفة في القول بالموجب ، وارد بعينه على القائلين بالمختار . والجواب : قوله : و لم لا يجوز أن يكون التعين جزءاً من المقتضى في أحد الجانبين ، أو مانعا من القبول في الجانب الثاني؟ ، قلنا : لأن التعين قيد عدمي ، والقيد العدمي لا يكون داخلا في المقتضى . أو نقول : إنه إما أن يكون عدميا أو ثبوتيا ، فإن كان عدميا ، فالسؤال زائل ، وإن كان ثبوتياً عاد البحث في أنه : لم اختص هذه الذات بهذا التعيين ، والذات الثانية بالتعين الثاني ، ولم يحصل الأمر بالعكس منه ؟ وأما النقوض فمدفوعة . وأما الحدوث
The fifth question: Why is it not permissible that the effecter in the occurrence of these particular qualities in these particular essences be a necessitator by essence, not an agent by choice? His statement: 'The relation of that necessitator to all is one, so the occurrence of preponderance is impossible.' We said: This same thing is necessarily required for the free agent. For those recipients are either equal in receptivity of the qualities, with none having a preponderance over the rest in receptivity of those qualities, or some are more appropriate for some of those qualities than others. The second is false, because bodies are equal in complete essence, and their being so prevents difference in this appropriateness. And if it were permissible, then why is it not permissible for them to differ in other qualities while being equal in essence? And when this division is invalid, the first is determined. And if it is so, then the relation of the free agent to all those bodies is equal, and the relation of all those bodies to it is equal; and then the specification by that agent of some of those bodies with some of those qualities would be a preponderance of one of the two possibilities without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. So it is established that what you have advanced against the philosophers in the statement of the necessitator is equally advanced against those who affirm the free agent. And the answer: His statement: 'And why is it not permissible that determinacy be a part of the requisite in one of the two sides, or a preventer of acceptance in the second side?' We said: Because determinacy is a negative restriction, and a negative restriction cannot be included in the requisite. Or we say: It is either negative or positive. If it is negative, then the question disappears; and if it is positive, the inquiry returns to: Why was this essence specified with this determinacy, and the second essence with the second determinacy, and the opposite did not occur? As for the counterexamples, they are refuted. And as for origination
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is the fifth question, a long objection and answer about whether the cause of specification is a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat) or a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). The objector suggests that perhaps the cause is a necessary one, not a free chooser. The opponent (the Mutakallim) replies that the same problem of preponderance without a preponderating factor (rajhan bi la murajjih) applies to the free agent as well. He argues that the recipients (bodies) are either equal in receptivity (so the agent must choose arbitrarily) or some are more suitable, but since bodies are equal in essence, they cannot differ in suitability. Therefore, the free agent would also be forced to choose without a reason, which is impossible—so the objection against the philosophers applies equally to the free agent. Then 'the answer' (al-jawab) provides a resolution: if determinacy (al-ta'ayyun) is a negative condition (qayd 'adami), it does not enter into the requisite; if it is positive, the same question of specification arises again. The passage concludes that the counterexamples are refuted, and then mentions 'origination' (al-huduth) which is cut off, but indicates a continuation. The teacher should note that this passage is dense and shows how the Mutakallim turns the objection back onto the objector.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
رجحان بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection against the necessary agent is unique and does not affect the free agent.
Correction: The Mutakallim shows that the free agent faces the same problem of arbitrary choice when recipients are equal.

Checkpoint
Why does the Mutakallim say the second possibility (inequality of recipients) is false?
A) Because bodies are created in time.
B) Because bodies are equal in essence, so they cannot differ in suitability.
C) Because the necessary agent cannot choose.
D) Because inequality is impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
The free agent faces the same specification problem as the necessary agent unless a reason for choice is given.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a free agent's choice would be arbitrary without a reason. When you reflect on your own choices, do you always have a reason? Or are there moments when you act without being able to articulate why? How does that challenge or confirm your trust in God's perfect wisdom, knowing that every divine act has a reason even if hidden from you? Commit to seeking the wisdom behind one of God's decrees in your life this week, even if you cannot fully grasp it.
?
The Mutakallim's argument places both the necessary agent and the free agent under the same logical scrutiny. Does this make you uncomfortable, as if God's actions are being judged by human logic? Or does it strengthen your faith that truth requires consistency? Honestly examine your heart: are you willing to submit your intellect to divine mysteries, or do you insist on full rational comprehension? Pledge to accept one aspect of God's actions that you do not understand, without demanding an explanation.
?
If the free agent's specification without a preponderating factor is impossible, then there must be a reason for everything God does. But what if you are going through a trial that seems pointless? Does this lesson give you hope that there is a hidden reason, or does it challenge you to search for it? Take a moment to write down one trial you are facing and ask God to show you the wisdom behind it, trusting that His choice is not arbitrary.

Resolving the Specification Problem through Negative Restriction
Mutakallim resolves the specification problem via negative condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the answer that determinacy (ta'ayyun) is a negative condition, which resolves the problem of specification without a positive reason.

Core Idea
Negative restrictions explain specification without needing a positive reason.

After the tu quoque argument, the objector asks: why not say that determinacy is built into the requisite or acts as a preventer? The Mutakallim's answer is that determinacy (ta'ayyun) is a negative restriction (qayd 'adami), which cannot be part of the positive requisite. This resolves the problem because negative conditions do not require a separate preponderating factor. This lesson unpacks that subtle but crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's proposal
The objector suggests determinacy could be part of the requisite or a preventer. Read this part carefully.
Determinacy here means 'this specific body gets this specific quality'
2
Understand the Mutakallim's first answer
The first answer: determinacy is a negative condition, so it cannot be part of the positive requisite. Understand what a negative condition means.
A negative condition is like an absence or lack, not a positive factor.
3
Analyze the second answer
The second answer: if determinacy is negative, the problem disappears; if positive, the same question recurs. This shows the only consistent way is to treat it as negative.
This forces the objector to accept the negative interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, you often assume that to change a situation you need to add something positive (effort, knowledge, people). But this passage teaches that specification often comes from removing a negative condition. What is the one negative condition you have been ignoring — a sin, a bad habit, a lack of tawakkul — that, if removed, would allow Allah to specify good in your life without you striving harder? When will you remove it?
?
The objector insisted on a positive cause for specification; the Mutakallim showed that a negative condition is sufficient. Are you, in your relationship with Allah, constantly asking 'why did this happen to me?' or 'why does Allah not give me X?', assuming a positive reason for His decree? Could it be that the answer is simply the removal of an obstacle — and that obstacle is your own self? Can you surrender to the 'negative' explanation and trust that Allah's wisdom is sufficient?
?
The passage resolves an intellectual problem by distinguishing positive from negative. But this distinction is not just theoretical: your spiritual elevation often requires subtracting vices (negative removal) rather than adding virtues (positive acquisition). What is one vice in your heart — pride, envy, laziness — that you have been trying to 'fight' with positive actions, when the real solution is to simply let it go through tawbah and humility? Will you name it right now and commit to removing it?

Fifth Objection: Necessary Cause vs Free Agent
Presents the fifth objection and rebuts it by showing the same problem applies to the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the free agent by showing that the objector's argument against the necessary cause applies equally to the free agent.

Core Idea
The objection against necessary causes also refutes free agents.

This passage presents the fifth objection in a series. The objector claims that a necessary cause cannot produce specific attributes in specific bodies. The author surprises us by arguing that the same problem undermines the free agent position. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Understand the objector's challenge: why can't the cause be a necessitator?
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector says the necessitator's ratio to all things is one, so no preponderance (tarjīḥ) can occur. This means a necessary cause cannot prefer one effect over another.
This is the core of the objection.
3
Follow the author's division
The author divides the recipients (qawābil) into two possibilities: either they are equal in receptivity, or some are more deserving. He then argues why the second is impossible.
The second option is invalid because bodies are equal in essence (māhiyya).
4
See the problem for the free agent
Since all recipients are equal in receptivity, the free agent's relation to them is also equal. Any specification would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible.
Thus the same objection applies to the free agent.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the objection against the philosophers (necessitators) applies exactly to those who affirm a free agent. The argument is consistent.
This is a powerful counter-argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس: لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر في حصول هذه الصفات المعينة في هذه الذوات المعينة موجبا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار؟ قوله: نسبة ذلك الموجب إلى الكل واحدة، فيمتنع حصول الرجحان. قلنا: هذا أيضا بعينه لازم في الفاعل المختار، فإن تلك القوابل إما أن تكون متساوية في قابلية الصفات من غير أن يكون لبعضها رجحان على الباقي في قابلية تلك الصفات، وإما أن يكون بعضها أولى ببعض تلك الصفات من بعض، والثاني باطل لأن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية، وكونها كذلك يمنع من الاختلاف في هذه الأولوية، وإن جاز ذلك: فلم لا يجوز اختلافها في سائر الصفات كونها متساوية في الماهية؟ ولما بطل هذا القسم، تعين الأول، وإذا كان كذلك كانت نسبة الفاعل المختار إلى كل تلك الأجسام بالسوية ونسبة كل تلك الأجسام إليه بالسوية، وحينئذ يكون تخصيص ذلك الفاعل على بعض تلك الأجسام ببعض تلك الصفات رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن من غير مرجح، وهو محال. فيثبت أن الذي أوردتموه على الفلاسفة في القول بالموجب، وارد بعينه على القائلين بالمختار.
The fifth question: Why is it not permissible that the effecter in the occurrence of these specific attributes in these specific essences be a necessitator (mūjib) by essence not an agent by choice (fā‘il bi’l-ikhtiyār)? [The objector] says: The ratio of that necessitator to the whole is one, so the occurrence of preponderance (tarjīḥ) is impossible. We said: This too necessarily applies exactly to the free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār). For those recipients are either equal in their receptivity of the attributes without any of them having a preponderance over the rest in receptivity of those attributes, or some of them are more deserving of some attributes than others. The second is invalid because bodies are equal in the totality of essence (tamām al-māhiyya), and their being so prevents difference in this priority. And if that is allowed, then why is it not allowed that they differ in the rest of the attributes while being equal in essence? And when this division became false, the first is determined. And if that is so, then the ratio of the free agent to all those bodies is equal, and the ratio of all those bodies to him is equal. Then the specification of that agent to some of those bodies with some of those attributes would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator (murjīḥ), which is impossible. So it is established that what you have brought against the philosophers concerning the doctrine of the necessitator, applies exactly to those who affirm the free agent.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection in a longer argument. The objector asks why the cause (mu’aththir) of specific attributes in specific essences cannot be a necessary cause (mūjib bi’l-dhāt) rather than a free agent (fā‘il bi’l-ikhtiyār). The objector's statement: 'the ratio of that necessitator to the whole is one, so preponderance is impossible' means that if the cause is necessary and uniform, it cannot prefer one effect over another. The author responds that this very problem applies to the free agent as well. He argues by dividing the recipients (qawābil) into two possibilities: either they are equal in receptivity, or some are more deserving. The second is false because bodies are equal in essence (māhiyya) and thus cannot differ in priority. If they could, why not differ in other attributes? So the first possibility is necessary: all bodies are equally receptive. Then the free agent's relation to all bodies is equal, and any specification would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible. Therefore, the objection against necessitators also refutes free agents. This is a powerful counter-argument showing consistency.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
رجحان
قوابل
ماهية
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming a free agent avoids the problem of preponderance.
Correction: The author shows that the same problem arises for the free agent if recipients are equal in receptivity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence if all recipients (bodies) are equal in receptivity?
A) The free agent can specify attributes freely.
B) The free agent's specification would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible.
C) The bodies must differ in priority.
D) The necessitator can cause different effects.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a necessary cause cannot specify attributes applies equally to a free agent when recipients are equal in receptivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice between two equally good options, do you ever feel a 'preponderator' within you—something that tips the scale? What is that, and is it truly from Allah or from your own desires?
?
This argument shows that even a free agent cannot arbitrarily specify without a reason. In your life, where do you assume your free will alone justifies your preferences? Are you willing to submit your choices to the same logical scrutiny?
?
If God is the ultimate free agent, how does this argument affect your understanding of why He created one person with faith and another without? Does it make you more humble about your own state of belief?

Specification Cannot Be Part of the Requirement
Author refutes the objection about specification being part of the requirement or a preventer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that specification could be part of the requirement or a preventer by analyzing its nature as negative or positive.

Core Idea
Specification as a negative condition cannot be part of the requirement.

In this passage, the author continues the defense of the need for a preponderator by addressing a clever objection: Could specification simply be part of the requirement itself? The author argues that specification is a negative condition and therefore cannot be included, and if it is positive, the same problem arises again. This lesson unpacks that dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's challenge: why cannot specification be part of the requirement (muqtaḍā) on one side or a preventer (māni‘) on the other?
This sets up the need for the author's response.
2
Understand the first answer
The author's first answer: specification is a negative condition (qayd ‘adami), and such a condition cannot be part of the requirement.
Negative conditions indicate absence, not a causal component.
3
Follow the dichotomous answer
The author offers a second argument: specification is either negative or positive. If negative, the question dissolves. If positive, the original problem of arbitrary assignment returns.
This reframes the objection as a false dilemma.
4
Note the dismissal of counterexamples
The author concludes by rejecting all counterexamples (nuqūḍ) to this reasoning.
The full argument is resilient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: قوله: ولم لا يجوز أن يكون التعين جزءاً من المقتضى في أحد الجانبين، أو مانعا من القبول في الجانب الثاني؟ قلنا: لأن التعين قيد عدمي، والقيد العدمي لا يكون داخلا في المقتضى. أو نقول: إنه إما أن يكون عدميا أو ثبوتيا، فإن كان عدميا، فالسؤال زائل، وإن كان ثبوتيا عاد البحث في أنه: لم اختص هذه الذات بهذا التعيين، والذات الثانية بالتعين الثاني، ولم يحصل الأمر بالعكس منه؟ وأما النقوض فمدفوعة.
And the answer: [The objector] says: And why is it not permissible that the specification (ta‘ayyun) be part of the requirement (al-muqtaḍā) in one of the two sides, or a preventer (māni‘) of acceptance in the second side? We said: Because specification is a negative condition (qayd ‘adami), and the negative condition cannot be included in the requirement. Or we say: It is either negative (adami) or positive (thubūtī). If it is negative, then the question is removed; and if it is positive, the inquiry returns to: why was this essence specified with this specification and the second essence with the second specification, and the opposite did not occur? And as for the counterexamples (nuqūḍ), they are refuted.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is part of the ongoing dialectic. The objector proposes that perhaps the specification (ta‘ayyun) of a particular body to receive a particular attribute could be part of the requirement (muqtaḍā) on one side, or a preventer (māni‘) on the other side, thus allowing for differentiation without a preponderator. The author gives two answers: First, specification is a negative condition (qayd ‘adami), i.e., it indicates the absence of something, and such a condition cannot be part of the requirement. Second, we can say the specification is either negative or positive. If negative, the problem evaporates because no positive cause is needed. If positive, then we ask why this essence was given this particular positive specification and not the opposite, thus leading back to the same problem of arbitrary assignment. The author concludes that all counterexamples (nuqūḍ) to this argument are refuted.

Key Terms
تعين
مقتضى
مانع
قيد عدمي
عدمي
ثبوتي
نقوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Specification can be part of the requirement for causation.
Correction: Specification is a negative condition and cannot be included in the requirement (muqtaḍā).

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about specification if it were a negative condition?
A) It can be part of the requirement.
B) The question is removed.
C) It must be positive.
D) It leads to a regress.

Key Takeaway
Specification is a negative condition and thus cannot be part of the requirement; if positive, it only pushes the problem back.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence—your specific body, abilities, and circumstances—do you ever feel a deep need to acknowledge a Preponderator who chose these for you? Or do you treat them as mere facts without a cause? How would your life change if you truly believed every detail of your specification was intentionally determined?
?
The lesson shows that even specification itself cannot be considered part of the requirement. In your daily life, do you ever attribute events to 'just the way things are' without seeking the deeper cause? What would it mean for your relationship with the Creator to realize that nothing is random, and every specific detail demands an explanation?
?
If a negative condition cannot be a cause, then your own existence is not explained by the mere absence of obstacles. Are you willing to face the implication that a positive, intentional cause must have chosen you over all other possibilities? Does that thought bring you gratitude or discomfort?
