Using the Premise of Possibility of Attributes for Celestial Bodies
The author enumerates two ways of applying the premise of possibility of attributes to celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the premise of possibility of attributes is applied to celestial bodies, specifically through the first two ways: the specificity of number and thickness, and composition from parts.

Core Idea
Even celestial bodies are contingent in their properties and need a determining cause.

In this chapter, the author shows that the rational premise of possibility of attributes—that any possible attribute requires a determiner—applies not only to elemental bodies but also to celestial spheres. The passage presents the first two ways of demonstrating this: the fixedness of a specific number and thickness for each sphere, and the composition of spheres from parts each of which could have been otherwise. Understanding these arguments strengthens the case for a necessary Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening paragraph
Read the first few lines where the chapter's aim is stated and the two divisions (celestial and elemental) are introduced.
The author says the premise is used for both celestial and elemental bodies.
2
Examine the first way
Focus on the first way: each sphere has a specific number and thickness, while alternatives (larger or smaller) are possible. This implies that its specification needs a cause.
The example given is Mars's sphere being thicker than the Sun's, and the outermost sphere's thickness unknown.
3
Examine the second way
Study the second way: each sphere is composed of parts, based on the earlier premise about imaginary division. The inner part could have been external, so its position is merely possible.
This argument uses the atom theory that anything divisible must have parts before division.
4
Consider the third way
Note that the third way is cut off but begins with motion and rest. Reflect on how the argument might continue.
The complete set of ways would strengthen the contingency case.

The Fourth Argument: Contingency of Speed
Fourth argument: speed of celestial motions is contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the variability of celestial motion speeds demonstrates contingency.

Core Idea
Speed could have been different, so it is contingent.

The fourth argument in this passage builds on the observation that any motion could have occurred faster or slower than it actually did. The specific speed we observe in celestial spheres is not a necessary outcome but a contingent fact. The author illustrates this by comparing the vastly different periods of rotation among the spheres, showing that the actual speeds are not determined by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument
Read the Arabic passage for the fourth argument and its translation. Notice the structure: a general statement followed by illustrative examples.
The argument is built on the principle that if something could have been different, it is contingent.
2
Understand the logic
The argument states: every motion could have been faster or slower, therefore its actual speed is contingent. The examples (fixed stars vs. sun) highlight the vast differences in speed.
Think about why speed variation matters for contingency.
3
Connect to the larger purpose
This argument shows that the heavens are not necessary in their attributes; they point to a free choice, thus requiring a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كل حركة قائمة يمكن وقوعها أسرع مما وقع وأبطأ مما وقع فذلك الحد المعين من السرعة والبطء من الجائزات وإنه إن تتحرك كرة الثوابت مرة واحدة تتحرك كرة الشمس ستة وثلاثين ألف مرة. وأعجب من هذا: أن الفلك الأعظم أعظم من تلك الثوابت بما لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى ثم إن الفلك الأعظم على غاية عظمته يتحرك كل يوم مرة واحدة وتلك الثوابت لا تتمم دورية إلا في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة. الخامس : كل حركة قد وقعت متوجهة إلى جهة معينة دون سائر الجهات فتكون من الجائزات . السادس : كل فلك يعرض فإنه يوجد فلك آخر إما أعلى منه أو أسفل منه وذلك في الحيز من الجائزات
The fourth: Every motion that exists could have occurred faster than it occurred and slower than it occurred, so that specific limit of speed and slowness is among the contingent things. And if the sphere of the fixed stars moves once, the sphere of the sun moves thirty-six thousand times. And more astonishing than this: that the greatest sphere is greater than those fixed stars by what only Allah knows, then the greatest sphere, despite its extreme greatness, moves once every day, and those fixed stars do not complete their revolution except in thirty-six thousand years. The fifth: Every motion that occurs is directed towards a specific direction, to the exclusion of other directions, so it is among the contingent things. The sixth: Every sphere that appears, there exists another sphere, either above it or below it, and that in space is among the contingent things.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments for the contingency (al-jawaz) of celestial phenomena. The author, in a typical kalam style, lists proofs that the specific configurations of the heavens are not necessary but possible. The fourth argument focuses on speed: any motion could have been faster or slower, so its actual speed is contingent. He illustrates with the vastly different periods of the fixed stars, the sun, and the greatest sphere. The fifth argument points out that any motion is directed towards a specific direction, whereas other directions are possible, so it is contingent. The sixth argument notes that any sphere implies the existence of other spheres above or below it, so its specific position in space is contingent. Each of these arguments aims to show that the celestial realm exhibits marks of contingency, which then points to the need for a Creator.

?
The passage highlights that the greatest sphere, despite its immense size, rotates daily while smaller fixed stars take millennia. ?
Your heartbeat, breathing, and every motion of your body could be different—they are contingent. How does this knowledge change your attitude towards your health and daily activities? Will you thank the One who chose these specific speeds for you, and will you use them in His obedience?

The Fifth Argument: Contingency of Direction
Fifth argument: direction of celestial motion is contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific direction of celestial motion demonstrates contingency.

Core Idea
Motion could have been in any direction, so its actual direction is contingent.

The fifth argument shifts focus from speed to direction. Every celestial motion we observe is directed toward a specific direction, but other directions are equally possible. This shows that the chosen direction is not necessary but contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth argument

2
Understand the logic

Consider why direction is a matter of choice, not necessity.
3

The Sixth Argument: Contingency of Sphere Existence
Sixth argument: contingency of sphere positions in space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of other spheres above or below a given sphere shows contingency of position.

Core Idea
A sphere's position relative to others is contingent because other positions are possible.

The sixth argument considers the spatial arrangement of celestial spheres. Whenever a sphere exists, there are other spheres either above or below it. This indicates that the specific position of a sphere in space is not necessary but contingent, as other placements are conceivable.

Arguments 7 and 8: Contingency of Celestial Forms and Differences
Two arguments from contingency of forms and differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments for contingency based on the specific forms and differences of celestial bodies, reinforcing the need for a necessary cause.

Core Idea
The specific forms and differences of heavenly bodies are not necessary but contingent.

Sanusi continues his series of arguments for contingency with two more observations about the celestial realm. He points out that the attribution of celestial form to celestial bodies and elemental form to elemental bodies is a contingent fact—it could have been otherwise. Furthermore, the differences between the spheres and the planets are also contingent. These arguments reinforce the idea that everything in the heavens depends on a necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style of the two arguments.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find argument 7 (attribution of forms) and argument 8 (difference between spheres and planets). Understand what each claims is contingent.
Both arguments use the phrase 'من الجائزات' (among the contingent things).
3
Understand why contingency matters
Ask yourself: why does pointing out that these features are contingent support the need for a cause? The commentary will help connect the dots.
4
Reflect on a potential misconception
Some might think the heavens are perfect and self-necessary. Consider how these arguments show they are not.
This guards against thinking the celestial realm is self-sufficient.

Both arguments highlight that the specific characteristics of heavenly bodies are not necessary but possible.

Three Arguments on the Contingency of Planets and Signs
Three arguments on contingency of specific celestial features

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific positions, natures, and characteristics of planets and zodiac signs are contingent and not necessary.

Core Idea
Every detail of the heavens is contingent, not necessary.

After establishing broader arguments for contingency, the author now focuses on specific features of the celestial bodies: their positions, natures, and the properties of zodiac signs. These three arguments reinforce that none of these details are necessary in themselves; they all point to a contingent universe dependent on a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full set of arguments. Notice their parallel structure.
Each begins with an ordinal number (التاسع, العاشر, الحادي عشر) and ends with 'من الجائزات'.
2
Understand 'contingent'
Reflect on what 'contingent' means here: something that could have been otherwise and depends on an external cause.
This is a key concept in kalam for establishing a Creator.
3
Identify the pattern
Notice that each argument takes a specific fact about the heavens and asserts it is contingent. The pattern is: X is contingent (من الجائزات).
X can be a position, a nature, or a characteristic.
4
Reflect on the cumulative case
Consider how these three examples together show that many specific details of the celestial realm are not self-necessary.
This prepares the ground for requiring an external cause for the entire system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : حصول كل واحد من الكواكب في نقطة متعينة من الفلك يكون من الجائزات . العاشر : اختصاص كل كوكب يكون معين وطبيعة معينة من الجائزات . الحادي عشر : اختصاص كل برج بطبيعة معينة وأمر معين من الجائزات
The ninth: The occurrence of each one of the planets in a specific point of the sphere is among the contingent things. The tenth: The specification of each planet with a specific location and specific nature is among the contingent things. The eleventh: The specification of each zodiac sign with a specific nature and specific matter is among the contingent things.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
These three arguments focus on the specific placements and natures of celestial bodies. The ninth argument: each planet occurs at a specific point in its sphere; this is contingent because it could have been at a different point. The tenth: each planet is specified with a particular location and nature; again contingent. The eleventh: each zodiac sign is specified with a particular nature and matter; also contingent. These arguments continue to build the case that every detail of the heavens is contingent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The positions, natures, and characteristics of celestial bodies are necessary or eternal.
Correction: The passage argues that all such specifics are contingent—they could have been otherwise—and therefore require a cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the ninth argument?
A) All planets are the same
B) The specific point where a planet appears is contingent
C) Planets do not move
D) The spheres are made of the same substance

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the stars and their precise positions, do you ever stop to think that each of those details could have been different and was chosen by God? How often do you take the order of the universe for granted without seeing the hand of the Chooser behind every detail? Commit this week to spend one night reflecting on a specific star or planet and thank God for placing it exactly where it is.
?
Your own personality, your strengths, your weaknesses—are they not also like the planets' natures? They were specified by God, not randomly assigned. When you feel pride in a talent or despair over a flaw, are you forgetting that both are contingent and placed in you by the All-Wise? Pause now and ask God: what do You want me to do with this specific nature You gave me?
?
The zodiac signs have specific natures and effects that we observe. But do you ever act as if certain times or astrological influences have power over you, independent of God? This lesson reminds you that even those associations are contingent and determined by God. Is there any area of your life—your mood, your luck, your plans—where you are unconsciously attributing necessity to worldly cycles? Repent and reaffirm: la havla wa la quwwata illa billah.

The Void and the World's Contingent Location
Short argument on contingency via infinite void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of an infinite void demonstrates the contingency of the world's location.

Core Idea
The world could have been anywhere in the infinite void, so its actual location is not necessary.

This lesson presents a concise argument from classical kalam that uses the concept of infinite void to establish the contingency of the world's location. Even if the void itself is not directly observable, the reasoning shows that the world's specific placement is not self-explanatory. The argument is the twelfth in a series, relying on a prior proof for the void's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is a single sentence, so pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
Break the argument into its parts: the premise (existence of infinite void), the fact (world is in a specific part), and the conclusion (this is contingent).
The word 'فوقوع' signals the result of the premise.
3
Reflect on the necessity of contingency
Ask yourself: Why does the infinite void make the location contingent? If the void were finite, would the argument still work?
Contingency here means the world could have been elsewhere, so its actual place depends on a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : ثبت بالدليل وجود خلاء لا نهاية له خارج العالم فوقوع كرة العالم في الجزء المعين من ذلك الخلاء دون سائر الأجزاء من الجائزات
The twelfth: It is established by proof the existence of infinite void outside the world, so the occurrence of the sphere of the world in the specific part of that void, to the exclusion of other parts, is among the contingent things.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This argument uses the existence of an infinite void outside the world (as proven by some earlier proof) to show that the world's specific location in that void is contingent. Since the void is infinite, the world could have been placed anywhere, so its actual position is not necessary but contingent.

The world's location in the infinite void is contingent, not necessary, because it could have been elsewhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Simplicity of Spheres Entails Contingency: Four Arguments
A detailed argument that the simplicity of spheres entails contingency in four ways

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the simplicity of celestial spheres leads to their contingency through four distinct arguments.

Core Idea
Simplicity does not save spheres from being contingent.

This lesson presents the thirteenth argument against the eternity of spheres. The author argues that even if spheres are simple, they exhibit features that are contingent. He enumerates four ways in which this contingency becomes evident, from the distribution of cavities to the possibility of fragmentation and the structure of epicycles.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its four parts
Read the passage carefully, noting the enumeration of four ways. Then read the translation to grasp the full meaning.
The passage is incomplete, but the provided portion covers the first three ways fully.
2
First way: equality of parts leads to contingency of cavity location
Understand that because all parts of a sphere are equal in nature, the specific location of a cavity (where a planet resides) is contingent—it could have been elsewhere.
3
Second way: simplicity vs. thickness differences
Despite the spheres being simple, two completers separated from an inclined sphere have opposite properties (thin vs. thick), indicating contingency.
The term 'completers' (المتهمين) may be a scribal error for 'المتممين'.
4
Third way: symmetry of surfaces leads to possibility of tearing
Since upper and lower surfaces have the same nature, if one sphere can touch another with its concave surface, it must also be possible with its convex surface, implying the possibility of tearing and fragmentation.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if spheres are simple and interchangeable, then destruction is possible.
5
Fourth way: epicycle embedding (incomplete)
The passage cuts off while discussing the epicycle embedded in another sphere. Reflect on how this structure also points to contingency.
Even incomplete, this shows that complexity (embedding) arises from simple spheres, which is contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : زعموا أن كل كرة من كرات الأفلاك فهو بسيط وهذا يوجب عليهم الإمكان من وجوه كثيرة : أحدها: أنه لما كان جميع أجزاء تلك الكرة متساوية في الطبيعة والماهية كان حصول النقرة التي حصل فيها الكوكب في جانب معين من ذلك الفلك دون سائر الجوانب من الجائزات وثانيها : لما كانت طبائع الكرات بسائط كانت طبيعة كل واحد من المتهمين اللذين ينفصلان عن الفلك المميل بسبب انفصال الفلك الخارج المركب عنه : طبيعة بسيطة مع أن أحد الجانبين اختص بالرقة والجانب الثاني اختص بالثخن فيكون ذلك من الجائزات وثالثها : إن طبيعة السطح الأعلى من كل فلك تكون مساوية لطبيعة السطح الأسفل منه وكل ما جاز على الشيء جاز على مثله إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كما جاز على كرة عطارد أن تماس كرة القمر بمقعرها وجب أن يجوز عليها أن تماسها بمحدبها ومتى ثبت هذا الجواز كان جواز الخرق والتفرق والتمزق لازما ورابعها : زعموا أن فلك التدوير مركوز في ثخن الفلك الخارج عن
The thirteenth: They claimed that every sphere among the spheres is simple, and this necessitates contingency for them from many aspects: First: Since all parts of that sphere are equal in nature and essence, the occurrence of the cavity in which the planet occurs in a specific side of that sphere to the exclusion of other sides is among the contingent things. Second: Since the natures of the spheres are simple, the nature of each one of the two completers that separate from the inclined sphere due to the separation of the outer composite sphere from it is a simple nature, despite that one of the two sides is characterized by thinness and the second side is characterized by thickness, so that is among the contingent things. Third: The nature of the upper surface of each sphere is equal to the nature of the lower surface of it, and whatever is possible for a thing is possible for its like. If this is established, we say: Just as it is possible for the sphere of Mercury to touch the sphere of the Moon with its concave [surface], it must be possible for it to touch it with its convex [surface], and when this possibility is established, the possibility of tearing, splitting, and fragmentation becomes necessary. Fourth: They claimed that the epicycle is embedded in the thickness of the sphere that is outside... [incomplete].
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth and most elaborate argument, which addresses the claim that each sphere is simple (basyit). The author argues that even if spheres are simple, they exhibit features that would be contingent. He gives four aspects: First, if all parts are equal, then the specific location of a cavity or planet in a particular side is contingent. Second, despite the spheres being simple, the two parts that separate from the inclined sphere due to the outer composite sphere are themselves simple, yet one is thin and the other thick, which is contingent. Third, if the upper and lower surfaces have the same nature, then whatever is possible for one is possible for the other; this leads to the possibility of a sphere touching another with its convex side, which would imply the possibility of tearing and fragmentation—which are contingent. Fourth, the epicycle is embedded in the thickness of another sphere, which again points to contingency. The argument is cut off but clearly builds to show that the assumed simplicity does not preclude contingency.

Key Terms
كرة
بسيط
إمكان
خرق
تفريق
تمزق
فلك التدوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is simple, it must be necessary and cannot be contingent.
Correction: This argument shows that simplicity does not rule out contingency; indeed, simplicity leads to multiple contingent aspects.

Checkpoint
In the thirteenth argument, what does the author argue about the spheres being simple?
A) It proves they are eternal
B) It necessitates contingency from many aspects
C) It proves they are necessary
D) It shows they are made of different substances

Key Takeaway
The simplicity of celestial spheres does not entail necessity; rather, it gives rise to multiple contingent features, undermining claims of their eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that the simplicity of spheres does not make them necessary. Where in your own life have you assumed that something being 'simple' means it is 'inevitable' or 'fixed'? Are there beliefs or habits you consider unchangeable simply because they seem basic? What would it take for you to see their contingency?
?
The third argument uses the principle 'whatever is possible for a thing is possible for its like.' When you consider your own capabilities, do you allow for the same possibilities in others that you assume for yourself? How might you be limiting God's creativity by denying similar possibilities to others?
?
The arguments in this passage rely on careful reasoning about the nature of spheres. Do you tend to accept claims about the universe without examining their logical implications? What is one deeply held belief about the world that you have not subjected to this kind of rigorous analysis, and are you willing to examine it now?

Democritus on the Arrangement of Elements
Explains Democritus's elemental arrangement from sphere motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Democritus's explanation of how the motion of the celestial sphere causes the arrangement of the four elements: fire, air, water, and earth.

Core Idea
Heat from motion arranges the elements: fire nearest the sphere, earth at the center.

In this passage, the author presents the atomist view of Democritus to later refute it. Democritus argues that the sphere's rotation causes heat, which affects nearby bodies and creates a gradient from fire to earth. This materialist explanation aims to account for the order of the cosmos without invoking a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Democritus's argument for elemental arrangement.
Notice how each element's position is determined by distance from the sphere.
2
Identify the four elements and their positions
List the elements in order from the sphere outward: fire, air, water, earth. Note the properties (heat, cold, subtlety, density).
3
Understand the causal mechanism
The sphere's motion generates heat; bodies closer become hotter and subtler, those farther become colder and denser.
4
Reflect on the purpose
This explanation is offered as a naturalistic alternative to divine creation, setting up a challenge to theistic arguments.
The author will later refute this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذكور واستدار. كان باطنه مملوءاً من الأجسام، والحركة موجبة للسخونة، والفلك لأجل حركته القوية يوجب غاية السخونة واللطافة، فيما بلاصقه من الأجسام، ويوجب غاية البرودة والكثافة في الأجسام التي تكون في غاية البعد منه، وهي الأجسام الحاصلة عند القرب من المركز فلهذا السبب صار الجسم العنصري المماس لجرم الفلك في غاية اللطافة والحرارة، وهو النار، وصار الجسم العنصري الذي يكون في غاية البعد من الفلك في غاية الكثافة والبرودة، وهو الأرض، والجسم المتصل بالنار، وهو الهواء لكونه شبيها بالنار مع أنه أقل لطافة من النار، والجسم المتصل بالأرض، هو الماء لكونه شبيها بالأرض في الكثافة والبرودة، مع أنه أقل كثافة وبردا من الأرض، فهذا هو السبب في ترتيب هذه العناصر، ثم لما استدارت الأفلاك بحركاتها المختلفة على هذه العناصر المختلفة، اقتضت حركات الأفلاك تمزيج بعض أجزاء هذه العناصر ببعض، فتولدت المواليد الثلاثة من المعادن والنباتات والحيوان فهذا تفصيل مذهب ديمقراطيس في هذا الباب قالوا: وما لم تبطلوا بالدليل فساد هذا الاحتمال لم يحصل مقصودكم البتة من الاستدلال بأحوال الأفلاك والعناصر، على وجود الصانع الحكيم.
The mentioned and rotated. Its interior was filled with bodies, and motion causes heat, and the sphere due to its strong motion causes extreme heat and subtlety in the bodies adjacent to it, and causes extreme cold and density in the bodies that are at the greatest distance from it, which are the bodies obtained near the center. For this reason, the elemental body touching the celestial body became in extreme subtlety and heat, which is fire, and the elemental body that is at the greatest distance from the sphere became in extreme density and cold, which is earth, and the body connected to fire, which is air, because it resembles fire while being less subtle than fire, and the body connected to earth, which is water, because it resembles earth in density and cold, while being less dense and cold than earth. This is the reason for the arrangement of these elements. Then when the spheres rotated with their various motions upon these different elements, the motions of the spheres necessitated the mixing of some parts of these elements with others, so the three productions—minerals, plants, and animals—were generated. This is the detailed doctrine of Democritus in this subject. They said: Unless you refute by proof the corruption of this possibility, your purpose will never be achieved from inferring by the states of the spheres and elements to the existence of the Wise Maker.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the atomist cosmology of Democritus and the challenge it poses to theists. It begins by describing how the sphere's rotation causes heat and subtlety in adjacent bodies, leading to the arrangement of the four elements: fire (hottest/most subtle), air (less subtle), water (colder/denser), and earth (coldest/densest). Then it explains that the motion of the spheres mixes these elements, generating three types of beings: minerals, plants, and animals. Finally, the atomists (presumably followers of Democritus) issue a challenge: unless the theist can prove the impossibility of this alternate explanation, their argument from the order of the heavens and elements for a wise creator is not conclusive. The passage sets up a key cosmological objection that the rest of the section will refute.

Key Terms
ديمقراطيس
عناصر
فلك
حركة
نار
هواء
ماء
أرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is the author's own view on creation.
Correction: This is a summary of Democritus's materialist cosmology, presented to be refuted.

Checkpoint
According to Democritus, what determines whether an elemental body becomes fire or earth?
A) The inherent nature of the body
B) Its distance from the rotating sphere
C) The will of the gods
D) Random chance

Key Takeaway
Democritus explains the order of elements as a natural outcome of motion and distance, not divine design.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read Democritus's explanation again. He attributes the arrangement of fire, air, water, and earth solely to the sphere's motion and distance. Does this explanation satisfy your heart, or do you sense something missing? What is that missing piece, and how does your soul respond to that absence?
?
If the entire world order is the result of physical forces as Democritus claims, then what is the purpose of your existence? This passage presents a view of the universe without divine intention. Sit with that thought for a moment. Does it make you appreciate the Islamic belief in a purposeful Creator more? How?
?
This passage is a summary of an opponent's view. Before the author's refutation, examine your own conviction: Is there any part of you that finds Democritus's explanation plausible? If so, what is that part, and what does it say about your faith? What step will you take today to reinforce your belief that the order of the world is a sign of God's wisdom?

The Generation of Beings and the Atomist Challenge
Explains generation of beings and atomist challenge to theistic arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomist account of how the mixing of elements generates minerals, plants, and animals, and the challenge this poses to theistic arguments.

Core Idea
The motion of spheres mixes elements to produce all beings, challenging design arguments.

This passage continues Democritus's cosmology, explaining how the motions of various spheres mix the four elements, giving rise to the three kingdoms of nature. It then presents the atomists' challenge: unless the theist can prove this explanation impossible, their argument from order to a wise maker is not conclusive. This sets up the critical objection that the author will refute in subsequent sections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the generative process
The motions of the spheres mix the elements, producing minerals, plants, and animals.
Notice that no divine intervention is needed.
2
Recognize the challenge
Atomists argue that if this natural explanation is possible, the design argument for a wise creator becomes inconclusive.
3
Consider the theistic response
The author will later refute this challenge by showing why Democritus's scenario is impossible or insufficient.
This passage sets the stage for that refutation.

The atomists present a plausible alternative to theistic design. Are there areas in your own life where you rely on 'natural' explanations for blessings or trials, rather than seeing them as direct from Allah? Will you commit to consciously attributing every event to His wisdom and will?
?
If a skeptic asked you to refute the atomist possibility with proof, would you be able to do so? What does that say about your intellectual preparation to defend your faith? What step will you take today to strengthen your understanding of the arguments for necessary existence?

Aristotle's Four Arguments Against Atomism
Aristotle refutes Democritus using four arguments: self-motion, void, indivisible parts, and symmetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's four-part refutation of Democritus's atomism and why the fourth argument is particularly decisive.

Core Idea
Even if atoms exist, their similarity would make motion impossible.

This passage presents Aristotle's critique of Democritus's atomic theory. After summarizing three fundamental objections already proven in natural science, Aristotle adds a powerful fourth argument that shows atomism fails on its own assumptions. Let's explore each argument and see how they combine to dismantle the atomist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let Aristotle's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the first three arguments are referenced as already established, while the fourth is explained in detail.
2
Identify the four arguments
The passage presents four distinct refutations. Identify each one and note how Aristotle groups the first three together before presenting the fourth as a separate, conditional argument.
The first three are based on principles proven false; the fourth assumes those principles and still shows contradiction.
3
Focus on the fourth argument's logic
The fourth argument is a conditional: even if atoms exist, their similarity means no direction is preferred, so motion is impossible. Understand why this is a strong internal critique.
This argument works even if one were to accept self-moving atoms, void, and indivisibility.
4
Consider the theological implication
Atomism was used by some to argue against divine causation. Aristotle's refutation shows that the material world cannot explain its own motion, pointing to a need for an external mover.
This connects to the larger proof of God's existence based on motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: أن أرسطاطاليس قد أبطل هذا القول من وجوه: أحدها: إنه بين أن كون الجسم متحركا لذاته محال، وقد سبقت هذه على سبيل الاستقصاء في العلم الطبيعي وثانيها: إن هذا بناء على إثبات الخلاء خارج العالم وقد سبقت هذه أيضا على سبيل الاستقصاء، ثالثهما: إن هذا بناء على أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وقد سبقت هذه المسألة أيضا على سبيل الاستقصاء. ولما كانت هذه القواعد الثلاثة باطلة عند أرسطاطاليس، لا جرم حكم بفساد هذا المذهب. ورابعها: إن بتقدير صحة هذه الأصول الثلاثة، فإنه يجب أن لا تتحرك تلك الأجزاء البتة. لأن الأجزاء المعترضة في الخلاء، إذا كانت متشابهة، لم يكن بأن تقتضي طبيعة ذلك الجزء بأن تنتقل إلى حيز، أولى من أن تنتقل إلى حيز آخر. ولما تساوت الانتقالات وتعارضت وتدافعت، وجب في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، أن يبقى في حيزه المعين.
The answer: Aristotle refuted this saying in several ways: First: He demonstrated that a body's being self-moving is impossible, and this has already been covered exhaustively in natural science. Second: It is based on affirming the void outside the world, and this too has already been covered exhaustively. Third: It is based on the claim that these sensible bodies are composed of indivisible parts, and this issue has also been covered exhaustively. Since these three principles are false according to Aristotle, he necessarily judged this doctrine as false. Fourth: Even assuming the validity of these three principles, it would be necessary that those parts never move at all. Because the parts existing in the void, if they are similar, it is not the case that the nature of that part requires moving to a location rather than moving to another location. Since the movements would be equal, opposed, and mutually canceling, it would be necessary for each of those parts to remain in its specific location.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's refutation of Democritus's atomist cosmology. It begins by stating that Aristotle refuted this view in multiple ways. The first three arguments are referenced as already established in natural science: (1) a body cannot move by itself, (2) there is no void outside the world, and (3) bodies are not composed of indivisible parts. Since these three principles (self-motion, void, atomism) are false, the entire Democritean system is invalid. Then Aristotle adds a fourth, independent argument: even if we grant those three principles, the atoms in the void would all be similar, so no atom would have a reason to move in one direction rather than another; they would remain stationary due to equal and opposite tendencies. Thus, Democritus's scheme fails even on its own terms. This is a complete objection-and-answer unit: the atomist challenge is met with a multi-point refutation.

Key Terms
أرسطاطاليس
متحرك لذاته
خلاء
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
تشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the fourth argument is just a repetition or weaker than the first three.
Correction: The fourth argument is actually the strongest: it shows that even if we accept the atomist principles, atomism still fails internally because identical atoms have no reason to move.

Checkpoint
According to Aristotle's fourth argument, why would atoms in the void not move even if they existed?
A) Because they are infinitely dense
B) Because they are attached to each other
C) Because they are all similar and have no preference for any direction
D) Because the void is actually full of air

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's refutation of atomism uses four arguments, the most powerful being that even if atoms existed, their similarity would make motion impossible, pointing to the need for an external mover.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The fourth argument shows that even if we grant the atomist's assumptions, motion requires an external chooser. In which areas of your life are you assuming that things just 'happen' without a divine choice behind them?
?
The lesson teaches that self-motion is impossible. Are there parts of your character or habits you think you can change entirely by your own will, without turning to Allah? What would it mean to admit that transformation is entirely from Him?

Two Additional Refutations of Democritus' Atomism
Two additional refutations of Democritus: nobility and smoothness/heat transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments that later scholars used to refute Democritus' atomistic theory.

Core Idea
The heavens are more noble than earth, and smooth surfaces don't transfer motion.

The refutations of Democritus continue. Beyond the earlier arguments, later scholars advanced two further critiques. The first appeals to common sense about nobility: if the heavens arose by accident, they would be less perfect than earthly things, which is false. The second explores the physical interaction of smooth surfaces and heat transfer, though the argument remains incomplete. This lesson presents these two additional refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that there are two distinct refutations presented.
The second refutation is incomplete; the text cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Understand the first refutation
The first refutation argues from the nobility of beings: if Democritus were right, the heavens would be less noble than earthly things, which contradicts sensory evidence.
3
Examine the second refutation
The second refutation discusses smooth surfaces and heat transfer, but the argument is incomplete. Identify the key physical ideas mentioned.
This refutation is a work in progress or truncated in the source.
4
Reflect on scholarly refinement
Consider why later scholars continued to develop new arguments against atomism, even though earlier ones existed.
This shows the depth of engagement with the theory.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن العلماء أبطلوا ذلك القول من وجوه أخرى: الأول: إن على تقدير الذي ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس، يكون حدوث الأفلاك لأسباب اتفاقية غريبة نادرة، وأما حدوث المواليد الثلاثة من المعادن والنبات والحيوان فهو لأجل أشياء طبيعية أصلية جوهرية، ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لوجب أن تكون تركيبات الأفلاك والكواكب، أقل شرفا وأدون كمالا، من تركيبات المواليد الثلاثة، ولما دلّ الحس على فساد هذا القول، ثبت فساد ذلك القول. الثاني: إن مقعر فلك القمر، ومحدب كرة النار، أيضاً: سطح أملس وإذا انزلق الأملس على الأملس لم يلزم من حركة أحديها حركة الآخر، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إما أن يقال: إن الفلك لأجل حركته يسخن جدا، فإذا تسخن في ذاته، صارت سخونته سبباً لسخونة جوهر الجرم الملاصق له، ولهذا السبب تحصل السخونة في جوهر النار. وإما أن يقال: الفلك إذا استدار لزم
Then the scholars refuted that saying in other ways: First: On the assumption of what Democritus held, the origination of the spheres would be due to rare accidental causes, whereas the origination of the three productions—minerals, plants, and animals—would be due to essential natural things. If that were the case, then the compositions of the spheres and planets would have to be less noble and inferior in perfection to the compositions of the three productions. Since sense perception indicates the falsity of this, the falsity of that saying is established. Second: The concave surface of the sphere of the moon and the convex surface of the sphere of fire are also smooth surfaces. If a smooth surface slides on a smooth surface, the motion of one does not necessitate the motion of the other. If this is established, we say: Either it is said that the sphere becomes very hot due to its motion, and when it becomes hot in itself, its heat becomes a cause for the heat of the substance of the body adjacent to it, and for this reason heat occurs in the substance of fire; or it is said that when the sphere rotates, it necessitates ...
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents two additional refutations of Democritus by later scholars (presumably post-Aristotelian). The first refutation focuses on the nobility of beings: if Democritus were right, the heavens (spheres and planets) would arise from rare accidental causes, while earthly beings (minerals, plants, animals) arise from essential natural causes. This would imply that the heavens are less perfect than earthly things, which is contradicted by empirical observation—the heavens are clearly more noble. The second refutation (incomplete) argues that the surfaces of the moon's sphere and the fire sphere are smooth. If smooth surfaces slide against each other, motion is not necessarily transferred. The author then begins to discuss alternatives: either the sphere's motion causes heat in itself and then heats the adjacent fire, or the sphere's rotation necessitates something. The text cuts off, so the full argument is missing. This lesson shows that scholars continued to refine arguments against atomism.

The First Refutation: Celestial Bodies vs. Generated Beings
Al-Razi's first argument: the absurd consequence of Democritus' view on the nobility of celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the argument from nobility and sensory evidence to refute Democritus' atomism.

Core Idea
If Democritus were right, the heavens would be less perfect than earthly things—an absurdity.

Al-Razi begins his first refutation by comparing the origins of celestial spheres and earthly beings according to Democritus. He argues that if atoms caused the heavens accidentally, they would be less noble than natural beings—contradicting what we observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises: one about accidental causes for spheres, the other about natural causes for generated beings.
3
Understand the role of sense perception
Al-Razi appeals to sense perception to show the conclusion is false, thereby refuting the premises. Why is sense perception authoritative?
This connects to broader epistemological principles in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن العلماء أبطلوا ذلك القول من وجوه أخرى:
الأول: إن على تقدير الذي ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس، يكون حدوث الأفلاك لأجل أسباب اتفاقية غريبة نادرة، وأما حدوث المواليد الثلاثة من المعادن والنبات والحيوان فهو لأجل أشياء طبيعية أصلية جوهرية، ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لوجب أن تكون تركيبات الأفلاك والكواكب، أقل شرفا وأدون كمالا، من تركيبات المواليد الثلاثة، ولما دل الحس على فساد هذا القول، ثبت فساد ذلك القول.
الثاني: إن مقعر فلك القمر، ومحدب كرة النار، أيضاً سطح أملس. وإذا انزلق الأملس على الأملس لم يلزم من حركة أحديها حركة الآخر. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إما أن يقال: إن الفلك لأجل حركته يسخن جدا، فإذا تسخن في ذاته، صارت سخونته سببا لسخونة جوهر الجرم الملاصق له، ولهذا السبب تحصل السخونة في جوهر النار. وإما أن يقال: الفلك إذا استدار لزم من استدارته حركة استدارة كرة النار وحركتها، فلأجل شدة حركة كرة النار تسخن وتصير نارا. والأول باطل، لما ثبت أن الأفلاك لا حارة ولا باردة. والثاني أيضا باطل لما ثبت أنه لا يلزم من حركة كرة الفلك حركة النار التي في جوف الفلك.
الثالث: أن نقول: لو لزم من حركة الفلك أن ينقلب الجسم الملاصق له نارا، لزم أيضا أن ينقلب الجسم الملاصق للنار نارا، فعلى هذا التقدير يلزم من كون النار ملاصقا للهواء أن يصير الهواء نارا، ثم يلزم من كون الملاصق للهواء أن يصير الماء نارا على هذا التقدير، فيلزم عند تمادي الدهور والأعصار أن تنقلب كل العناصر نارا. ومعلوم أنه باطل. فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب.
Then the scholars invalidated that statement from other aspects: First: On the assumption of what Democritus held, the occurrence of the celestial spheres would be due to rare, strange accidental causes, while the occurrence of the three beings (metals, plants, and animals) would be due to natural, original, essential things. And if it were so, then the compositions of the celestial spheres and planets would have to be less noble and less perfect than the compositions of the three beings. And since sense perception indicates the falsity of this statement, the falsity of that statement is established. Second: The concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of the sphere of fire are also smooth surfaces. And if a smooth surface slides on a smooth surface, the motion of one does not necessitate the motion of the other. And if this is established, we say: Either it is said that the sphere becomes very hot due to its motion, and when it becomes hot in itself, its heat becomes a cause for the heat of the substance of the adjacent body, and for this reason heat occurs in the substance of fire. Or it is said: that when the sphere rotates, its rotation necessitates the rotational motion of the sphere of fire and its motion, and due to the intensity of the motion of the sphere of fire, it becomes hot and becomes fire. The first is invalid because it is established that the celestial spheres are neither hot nor cold. The second is also invalid because it is established that the motion of the celestial sphere does not necessitate the motion of the fire that is within the sphere. Third: We say: If the motion of the sphere necessitated that the adjacent body turns into fire, then it would also necessitate that the body adjacent to fire turns into fire. On this assumption, fire being adjacent to air would necessitate that air becomes fire, then the adjacent to air would necessitate that water becomes fire on this assumption. Thus, over the course of ages and centuries, all elements would turn into fire. And it is known that this is false. This is the sum of the discussion on this topic.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents three additional refutations by the scholars (theologians) of Democritus' atomistic theory. The passage begins by stating that the scholars invalidated that theory from other aspects (wujuh), then enumerates them.

The first refutation: If Democritus were correct, the origin of celestial spheres would be due to rare, accidental causes, while the origin of three kinds of generated beings (minerals, plants, animals) would be due to natural, essential causes. This would imply that the celestial bodies are less noble and less perfect than earthly generated beings—which is contrary to sensory evidence. Since sense perception shows the falsity of this implication, the original theory is also false.

The second refutation concerns the generation of fire from the motion of spheres. The argument notes that the concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of the sphere of fire are smooth. Smooth surfaces sliding on each other do not transfer motion from one to the other. Two possibilities are examined: (1) The sphere's motion makes it very hot, and that heat transfers to the adjacent body, causing fire. (2) The sphere's rotation causes the fire sphere to rotate, and that rapid rotation generates heat, producing fire. Both are rejected: the first because celestial spheres are known to be neither hot nor cold; the second because the motion of the celestial sphere does not necessitate motion of the interior fire. Hence, the theory cannot explain the origin of fire.

The third refutation reduces the theory to an infinite regress: If motion of the sphere turns adjacent body into fire, then that fire would turn its adjacent body (air) into fire, and so on successively until all elements become fire. This absurd consequence disproves the theory.

The passage concludes by stating that this summarizes the discussion on this topic.

Key Terms
ديمقراطيس
الأفلاك
المواليد الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Al-Razi is only making an aesthetic argument.
Correction: The argument is based on causality and the nature of perfection; it is a logical reduction ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi argue would be the consequence if Democritus were right?
A) The heavens would be more perfect.
B) The heavens would be less perfect.
C) The heavens would not exist.
D) The generated beings would be accidental.

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that Democritus' theory leads to an absurd conclusion contradicted by sensory evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the vast, ordered heavens—the sun, moon, and stars—do you see them as more perfect than the things you interact with daily on earth? If so, does your observation convincingly refute any doubts you have about a purposeful Creator? What will you do this week to let the order of creation deepen your certainty?
?
Al-Razi appeals to 'sense perception' as a decisive proof. In what area of your life—beliefs, habits, relationships—are you ignoring what your senses clearly tell you about God's handiwork? Name one specific change you will make to align your conviction with what you can observe.
?
If you were to treat the regularity of your own life—your health, provision, and daily circumstances—as mere accidents, how would that affect your trust in God's care? What commitment can you make today to recognize His intentional order in both the heavens and your own life?

The Second Refutation: Smooth Surfaces and Heat Generation
Al-Razi's second argument: the impossibility of fire generation from sphere motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that Democritus cannot explain how the motion of celestial spheres produces fire.

Core Idea
Smooth surfaces don't transfer motion, and celestial spheres are neither hot nor cold.

Al-Razi's second refutation targets Democritus' explanation of the origin of fire. He examines the contact between the lunar sphere and the sphere of fire, arguing that smooth surfaces cannot transmit motion, and that the spheres lack the properties needed to generate heat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities
Al-Razi presents two possible ways the sphere's motion could cause fire. Find them in the text.
2
Understand the rejection of the first possibility
Why does Al-Razi reject the idea that the sphere itself becomes hot?
Key principle: celestial spheres are neither hot nor cold.
3
Understand the rejection of the second possibility
Why does he reject the idea that the sphere's rotation causes the fire sphere to rotate and heat up?
Because smooth surfaces do not necessitate motion transfer.

Then the scholars invalidated that statement from other aspects: First: On the assumption of what Democritus held, the occurrence of the celestial spheres would be due to rare, strange accidental causes, while the occurrence of the three beings (metals, plants, and animals) would be due to natural, original, essential things. And if it were so, then the compositions of the celestial spheres and planets would have to be less noble and less perfect than the compositions of the three beings. And since sense perception indicates the falsity of this statement, the falsity of that statement is established. Second: The concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of the sphere of fire are also smooth surfaces. And if a smooth surface slides on a smooth surface, the motion of one does not necessitate the motion of the other. And if this is established, we say: Either it is said that the sphere becomes very hot due to its motion, and when it becomes hot in itself, its heat becomes a cause for the heat of the substance of the adjacent body, and for this reason heat occurs in the substance of fire. Or it is said: that when the sphere rotates, its rotation necessitates the rotational motion of the sphere of fire and its motion, and due to the intensity of the motion of the sphere of fire, it becomes hot and becomes fire. The first is invalid because it is established that the celestial spheres are neither hot nor cold. The second is also invalid because it is established that the motion of the celestial sphere does not necessitate the motion of the fire that is within the sphere. Third: We say: If the motion of the sphere necessitated that the adjacent body turns into fire, then it would also necessitate that the body adjacent to fire turns into fire. On this assumption, fire being adjacent to air would necessitate that air becomes fire, then the adjacent to air would necessitate that water becomes fire on this assumption. Thus, over the course of ages and centuries, all elements would turn into fire. And it is known that this is false. This is the sum of the discussion on this topic.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents three additional refutations by the scholars (theologians) of Democritus' atomistic theory. The passage begins by stating that the scholars invalidated that theory from other aspects (wujuh), then enumerates them.

The first refutation: If Democritus were correct, the origin of celestial spheres would be due to rare, accidental causes, while the origin of three kinds of generated beings (minerals, plants, animals) would be due to natural, essential causes. This would imply that the celestial bodies are less noble and less perfect than earthly generated beings—which is contrary to sensory evidence. Since sense perception shows the falsity of this implication, the original theory is also false.

The second refutation concerns the generation of fire from the motion of spheres. The argument notes that the concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of the sphere of fire are smooth. Smooth surfaces sliding on each other do not transfer motion from one to the other. Two possibilities are examined: (1) The sphere's motion makes it very hot, and that heat transfers to the adjacent body, causing fire. (2) The sphere's rotation causes the fire sphere to rotate, and that rapid rotation generates heat, producing fire. Both are rejected: the first because celestial spheres are known to be neither hot nor cold; the second because the motion of the celestial sphere does not necessitate motion of the interior fire. Hence, the theory cannot explain the origin of fire.

The third refutation reduces the theory to an infinite regress: If motion of the sphere turns adjacent body into fire, then that fire would turn its adjacent body (air) into fire, and so on successively until all elements become fire. This absurd consequence disproves the theory.

The passage concludes by stating that this summarizes the discussion on this topic.

Key Terms
مقعر فلك القمر
محدب كرة النار
سطح أملس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument depends on empirical physics of smooth surfaces.
Correction: Al-Razi is using a logical principle: if smooth surfaces do not necessitate motion, then the causal link is broken.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi reject the idea that the sphere's motion causes heat?
A) Because the sphere is too far away.
B) Because the sphere is smooth.
C) Because celestial spheres are neither hot nor cold.
D) Because fire is already hot.

Key Takeaway
The second refutation shows that Democritus' theory cannot explain fire generation due to the properties of smooth surfaces and celestial spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an explanation of the world that seems plausible, do you examine its assumptions as carefully as Al-Razi examined the two options? What assumptions in your own life might be based on 'smooth surfaces'—ideas that seem to fit but have no real connection to truth?
?
Al-Razi refuted these theories by showing they contradict established facts. Have you ever held a belief that contradicted what you already knew to be true? What would it take for you to let go of that belief today?
?
This passage reminds us that not all plausible explanations are valid. Are there areas in your life where you have accepted a 'smooth' explanation about God, your purpose, or morality without rigorously checking it against revelation? What step will you take this week to test one such assumption?

The Third Refutation: Infinite Regress of Fire
Al-Razi's third argument: the infinite regress of fire leading to universal conflagration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that Democritus' theory leads to an infinite regress where all elements turn into fire.

Core Idea
If motion turns adjacent bodies into fire, eventually everything becomes fire.

Al-Razi's third refutation exposes an absurd consequence of Democritus' theory: if the sphere's motion converts the adjacent body into fire, then by the same logic, fire would convert its adjacent air into fire, and so on, until all elements become fire. This is clearly false, so the theory is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and trace the chain
Follow Al-Razi's chain: sphere motion → fire → air → water → all elements become fire.
2
Identify the underlying assumption
What assumption does Al-Razi draw from Democritus to start the regress?
That motion of the sphere turns the adjacent body into fire.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Why is the conclusion that all elements become fire absurd? Why does this refute Democritus?
Because we observe that elements persist and do not all turn to fire.

ثم إن العلماء أبطلوا ذلك القول من وجوه أخرى:
الأول: إن على تقدير الذي ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس، يكون حدوث الأفلاك لأجل أسباب اتفاقية غريبة نادرة، وأما حدوث المواليد الثلاثة من المعادن والنبات والحيوان فهو لأجل أشياء طبيعية أصلية جوهرية، ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لوجب أن تكون تركيبات الأفلاك والكواكب، أقل شرفا وأدون كمالا، من تركيبات المواليد الثلاثة، ولما دل الحس على فساد هذا القول، ثبت فساد ذلك القول.
الثاني: إن مقعر فلك القمر، ومحدب كرة النار، أيضاً سطح أملس. وإذا انزلق الأملس على الأملس لم يلزم من حركة أحديها حركة الآخر. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إما أن يقال: إن الفلك لأجل حركته يسخن جدا، فإذا تسخن في ذاته، صارت سخونته سببا لسخونة جوهر الجرم الملاصق له، ولهذا السبب تحصل السخونة في جوهر النار. وإما أن يقال: الفلك إذا استدار لزم من استدارته حركة استدارة كرة النار وحركتها، فلأجل شدة حركة كرة النار تسخن وتصير نارا. والأول باطل، لما ثبت أن الأفلاك لا حارة ولا باردة. والثاني أيضا باطل لما ثبت أنه لا يلزم من حركة كرة الفلك حركة النار التي في جوف الفلك.
الثالث: أن نقول: لو لزم من حركة الفلك أن ينقلب الجسم الملاصق له نارا، لزم أيضا أن ينقلب الجسم الملاصق للنار نارا، فعلى هذا التقدير يلزم من كون النار ملاصقا للهواء أن يصير الهواء نارا، ثم يلزم من كون الملاصق للهواء أن يصير الماء نارا على هذا التقدير، فيلزم عند تمادي الدهور والأعصار أن تنقلب كل العناصر نارا. ومعلوم أنه باطل. فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب.
Then the scholars invalidated that statement from other aspects: First: On the assumption of what Democritus held, the occurrence of the celestial spheres would be due to rare, strange accidental causes, while the occurrence of the three beings (metals, plants, and animals) would be due to natural, original, essential things. And if it were so, then the compositions of the celestial spheres and planets would have to be less noble and less perfect than the compositions of the three beings. And since sense perception indicates the falsity of this statement, the falsity of that statement is established. Second: The concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of the sphere of fire are also smooth surfaces. And if a smooth surface slides on a smooth surface, the motion of one does not necessitate the motion of the other. And if this is established, we say: Either it is said that the sphere becomes very hot due to its motion, and when it becomes hot in itself, its heat becomes a cause for the heat of the substance of the adjacent body, and for this reason heat occurs in the substance of fire. Or it is said: that when the sphere rotates, its rotation necessitates the rotational motion of the sphere of fire and its motion, and due to the intensity of the motion of the sphere of fire, it becomes hot and becomes fire. The first is invalid because it is established that the celestial spheres are neither hot nor cold. The second is also invalid because it is established that the motion of the celestial sphere does not necessitate the motion of the fire that is within the sphere. Third: We say: If the motion of the sphere necessitated that the adjacent body turns into fire, then it would also necessitate that the body adjacent to fire turns into fire. Thus, over the course of ages and centuries, all elements would turn into fire. And it is known that this is false. This is the sum of the discussion on this topic.

The Third Objection: Reductio ad Absurdum of Universal Fire
Third objection showing absurdity of the claim via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to refute the claim that motion necessarily turns adjacent bodies into fire.

Core Idea
If motion turns adjacent bodies into fire, then all elements would eventually become fire—an absurdity.

This passage presents a third objection against the claim that the movement of the celestial sphere transforms the adjacent body into fire. The author employs a logical chain to show that such a claim leads to an absurd conclusion: that over time, all elements would turn into fire. By demonstrating this impossibility, the original claim is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's premise
The objection starts from the opponent's claim: motion of the sphere turns adjacent body into fire. This is the assumption we will test.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the author's reasoning: if that premise holds, then fire adjacent to air would turn air into fire, and so on. See how the chain extends through all elements.
3
Recognize the absurd conclusion
The conclusion is that over ages, all elements become fire. This is known to be false, so the premise must be false.
4
Reflect on the method
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assume the claim, derive a contradiction, reject the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن نقول : لو لزم من حركة الفلك أن ينقلب الجسم الملاصق له نارا ، لزم أيضا أن ينقلب الجسم الملاصق للنار نارا ، فعلى هذا التقدير يلزم من كون النار ملاصقا للهواء أن يصير الهواء نارا ، ثم يلزم من كون ( الملاصق للهواء أن يصير ) الماء نارا على هذا التقدير ، فيلزم عند تمادي الدهور والأعصار ، أن تنقلب كل العناصر نارا . ومعلوم أنه باطل . فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم بحقائق الأمور.
The third: That we say: If it follows from the movement of the sphere that the body adjacent to it turns into fire, then it would also follow that the body adjacent to fire turns into fire. On this assumption, it follows from fire being adjacent to air that the air becomes fire, then it follows from what is adjacent to air becoming water on this assumption, so over the course of ages and centuries, it would follow that all elements turn into fire. And it is known that this is false. So this is the sum of the discourse on this topic. And God knows best the realities of things.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection in a dialectical argument. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum. He argues that if the opponent's claim that the motion of the celestial sphere causes the adjacent body to become fire were true, then by the same logic, fire adjacent to air would turn air into fire, and then the air adjacent to water would turn water into fire. This would lead, over time, to all elements becoming fire, which is absurd. Therefore, the original claim is false. The author closes by saying this concludes the discussion, acknowledging that God knows the true realities.

Correction: The author is using reductio ad absurdum; he does not believe fire transforms adjacent things, but shows the opponent's claim leads to absurdity.

Objection and Answer on Ancient Hatred Causing Recent Harm
The author presents an objection about ancient hatred causing recent harm and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the principle that every effect requires a recent cause, using the example of ancient hatred.

Core Idea
An ancient cause alone is not enough; a recent condition is always needed.

This passage presents a clever objection against the necessity of a recent cause for every effect. The objector uses the example of an ancient hatred leading to a recent harm, arguing that no recent cause is needed. The author's answer clarifies that the ancient hatred requires a recent moment of ability and opportunity—thus preserving the causal principle. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note the objector's example: a person hates another from ancient times and then causes harm recently. The objector claims this shows a recent cause is not necessary.
The objector's reasoning is: the ancient hatred is sufficient, so no new cause is needed.
2
Identify the author's response
The author responds by distinguishing between the cause of the harm's connection (ancient hatred) and the cause of its actual occurrence (recent ability and opportunity).
The author forces the objector to admit that a recent condition is required.
3
Understand the concept of 'ability and opportunity'
The author introduces 'qudra' (power) and 'mukna' (opportunity) as the recent conditions that allow the ancient hatred to become effective now.
This preserves the principle that every event needs a recent cause.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument upholds the need for a recent cause even when a cause appears ancient. The objector's example actually proves the opposite of what they intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أليس أن إنساناً مبغضاً لإنسان آخر من قديم [ الدهر ] ثم إنه يوقعه في بلاء ومحنة. فإنه يقال : إن وقوعه في هذه المحنة ، ليس لهذا السبب الحادث، بل إنما كان ، لأجل البغض القديم؟ فنقول : لابد وأن يقولوا : إن البغض القديم كان موجب اتصال الأفة إليه. أما وقت القدرة والمكنة ، فإنما حصل في هذه الساعة . فقد اعترفوا ههنا بحدوث هذا الأثر لهما
If they say: 'Is it not that a person who hates another person from ancient times then causes him to fall into calamity and trial? So it is said: His falling into this trial is not due to a recent cause, but rather it was due to the ancient hatred?' We say: They must say that the ancient hatred was the cause of the connection of harm to him. As for the time of ability and opportunity, it only occurred at this hour. So they have acknowledged here that this effect occurred for them (i.e., for the two).
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that an ancient hatred can cause a recent harm, so a recent cause is not always necessary. The answer explains that the ancient hatred was indeed the cause of the harm's connection, but the actual occurrence of the harm required a new condition—the time of ability and opportunity (قدرة ومكنة). This new condition is a recent cause. The objectors thus implicitly admit that the effect has a recent aspect, namely the newly available ability and opportunity. Therefore, the objection does not refute the need for a recent cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That the objection disproves the need for a recent cause entirely.
Correction: The answer shows that a recent condition (ability and opportunity) is still required.

Checkpoint

C) Building a house without materials.
D) The creation of the world from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Even if a cause is ancient, its effect requires a recent condition to occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a trial, do you instinctively search for a single ancient cause—'It's because of what happened ten years ago'—rather than looking at the recent conditions you allowed to persist? What recent ability or opportunity have you ignored that keeps the harm alive?
?
If Allah holds you accountable for your actions, not just your past, but also for the moments when you had the ability to change but did not—what will you do today with the ability you have right now to break the cycle of ancient bitterness?

The Dilemma of Recent Causation
Sanusi presents a dilemma on the causation of recent events and its rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two horns of the dilemma regarding causal dependence of recent events and why neither is acceptable to al-Ka‘bī or common reason.

Core Idea
Recent events cannot depend on recent causes without infinite regress, nor on an unchanging cause without contradiction.

The author presents a powerful dilemma for the view that every recent judgment requires a recent cause. If we accept that, we get infinite regress; if we reject it and say the cause is eternal and unchanging, then how can a recent effect arise from it? This dilemma challenges the very basis of causal reasoning, and the author shows that neither option is rationally acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two conditional statements introduced by 'إن قلنا' and 'إن حكمنا'.
Al-Ka‘bī rejects the first option; rational people reject the second.
2
Identify the two horns
The first horn: every recent thing depends on another recent thing (infinite regress). The second horn: every recent thing depends on an eternal effecter without any change (improbable).
3
Understand each rejection
Al-Ka‘bī rejects the first horn because he denies infinite regress. Rational people reject the second because an unchanging cause cannot produce a new effect without changing.
The author implies that the only consistent position is to admit a recent cause that changes, which al-Ka‘bī denies.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The author concludes that what most rational people judge (need for a recent cause) is rejected by al-Ka‘bī, and what al-Ka‘bī wants (intuitive knowledge without inference) is not rationally judged.
This shows that al-Ka‘bī’s position is not supported by reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمة . فنقول : إنا إن قلنا بافتقار الحكم الحادث إلى سبب حادث ، لزمنا الاعتراف باستناد كل حادث إلى [ حادث ] آخر قبله . والكعبي لا يرضى بهذا القول البتة . [ وإن حكمنا بأن استناد كل حادث إلى مؤثر ، كان موجوداً قبله، من غير أن يحصل لذلك المؤثر ، تغير حال وتبدل البتة ] فهذا مما يستبعده كل العقلاء. فثبت : أن الذي يحكم به صريح عقل جمهور العقلاء ، فالكعبي لا يرتضيه ولا يقول به ، والذي يريده الكعبي ، ويقول به ، فالعقل لا يحكم به
والسؤال الثاني : إن العقل كما يستبعد الحدوث من غير فاعل ، فكذلك يستبعد الحدوث من غير سبق مادة ومدة . فإنه كما أن حدوث البناء من غير الباني مستبعد ، فكذلك حدوث البناء من غير تقديم الخشب والحجر واللبن مستبعد . وليس أحد الأستبعادين أقوى من الثاني. وأيضاً : حدوث الحادث لا
And if you know this premise, we say: If we say that the recent judgment depends on a recent cause, we are forced to acknowledge that every recent thing depends on [another] recent thing before it. And al-Ka‘bī does not accept this statement at all. And if we judge that every recent thing depends on an effecter that existed before it, without any change or alteration occurring to that effecter at all, this is something every rational person deems improbable. So it is established that what is judged by the explicit intellect of the majority of rational people, al-Ka‘bī neither approves nor says; and what al-Ka‘bī wants and says, the intellect does not judge. The second question: Indeed, just as the intellect deems improbable occurrence without an agent, so too it deems improbable occurrence without prior matter and time. For just as the occurrence of a building without a builder is improbable, so the occurrence of a building without prior provision of wood, stone, and bricks is improbable. And one of the two improbabilities is not stronger than the other. Also, the occurrence of the event does not...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage first presents a dilemma for the position that the need for a cause is intuitive. The author argues that if we say every recent thing (ḥukm ḥādith) depends on a recent cause, we get an infinite regress, which al-Ka‘bī rejects. Alternatively, if we say every recent thing depends on an eternal effecter without any change, the intellect finds that improbable. Thus, neither option is acceptable. The author concludes that what the majority rational people judge (i.e., the need for a recent cause) is not accepted by al-Ka‘bī, and what al-Ka‘bī wants (the intuitive knowledge without inference) is not judged by reason. Then he raises a second objection: just as reason deems occurrence without an agent improbable, it also deems occurrence without prior matter and time improbable. The example of a building requires both a builder and materials; neither improbability is stronger. This challenges the sufficiency of mere ḥudūth as proof for an agent without also proving pre-existing matter. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: both agent and matter seem equally necessary to reason.

Key Terms
حكم حادث (ḥukm ḥādith)
استناد (istinād)
مؤثر (mu’aththir)
مؤثر (mu’aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dilemma leaves a valid third option.
Correction: The author shows that only two options are presented and both are rejected; no third option is offered.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma?
A) Infinite regress of causes
B) Dependence on an unchanging eternal cause
C) Both A and B
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that the rational requirement of a recent cause leads to absurdity, while the alternative of an unchanging cause is also rationally improbable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage states that every rational person deems the idea of an unchanging cause producing a new effect as improbable. Yet, you believe Allah creates without change. How do you reconcile this apparent improbability with your faith? Does this dilemma push you to deeper trust in divine power beyond human reasoning?

The Second Objection: Matter and Time as Necessary for Occurrence
Sanusi argues that reason requires matter and time as well as an agent for occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that reason deems occurrence without prior matter and time just as improbable as occurrence without an agent.

Core Idea
Occurrence requires both agent and matter – reason rejects both absences equally.

The author raises a second objection to the claim that the intellect only requires an agent for occurrence. He argues that the intellect also requires pre-existing matter and time, using the analogy of a building needing both a builder and materials. This challenges the sufficiency of the argument from contingency (ḥudūth) for proving a creator without also proving pre-existing matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the parallel structure: 'كما يستبعد ... فكذلك يستبعد'.
2
Understand the analogy
The example of a building needing both a builder and materials (wood, stone) shows that both are equally necessary to reason.
The author says 'ليس أحد الاستبعادين أقوى من الثاني' (one improbability is not stronger than the other).
3
Reflect on the implication
If reason equally requires matter and time, then the mere fact of occurrence does not prove an agent alone; it also proves pre-existing matter, which could lead to a form of materialism that theists reject.
This weakens the standard kalām argument that temporal origination proves a creator without also proving matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمة . فنقول : إنا إن قلنا بافتقار الحكم الحادث إلى سبب حادث ، لزمنا الاعتراف باستناد كل حادث إلى [ حادث ] آخر قبله . والكعبي لا يرضى بهذا القول البتة .
