والسؤال الثاني : إن العقل كما يستبعد الحدوث من غير فاعل ، فكذلك يستبعد الحدوث من غير سبق مادة ومدة . فإنه كما أن حدوث البناء من غير الباني مستبعد ، فكذلك حدوث البناء من غير تقديم الخشب والحجر واللبن مستبعد . وليس أحد الأستبعادين أقوى من الثاني. وأيضاً : حدوث الحادث لا
And if you know this premise, we say: If we say that the recent judgment depends on a recent cause, we are forced to acknowledge that every recent thing depends on [another] recent thing before it. And al-Ka‘bī does not accept this statement at all. And if we judge that every recent thing depends on an effecter that existed before it, without any change or alteration occurring to that effecter at all, this is something every rational person deems improbable. So it is established that what is judged by the explicit intellect of the majority of rational people, al-Ka‘bī neither approves nor says; and what al-Ka‘bī wants and says, the intellect does not judge. The second question: Indeed, just as the intellect deems improbable occurrence without an agent, so too it deems improbable occurrence without prior matter and time. For just as the occurrence of a building without a builder is improbable, so the occurrence of a building without prior provision of wood, stone, and bricks is improbable. And one of the two improbabilities is not stronger than the other. Also, the occurrence of the event does not...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage first presents a dilemma for the position that the need for a cause is intuitive. The author argues that if we say every recent thing (ḥukm ḥādith) depends on a recent cause, we get an infinite regress, which al-Ka‘bī rejects. Alternatively, if we say every recent thing depends on an eternal effecter without any change, the intellect finds that improbable. Thus, neither option is acceptable. The author concludes that what the majority rational people judge (i.e., the need for a recent cause) is not accepted by al-Ka‘bī, and what al-Ka‘bī wants (the intuitive knowledge without inference) is not judged by reason. Then he raises a second objection: just as reason deems occurrence without an agent improbable, it also deems occurrence without prior matter and time improbable. The example of a building requires both a builder and materials; neither improbability is stronger. This challenges the sufficiency of mere ḥudūth as proof for an agent without also proving pre-existing matter. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: both agent and matter seem equally necessary to reason.

مادة (mādda)
مدة (mudda)
استبعاد (istib‘ād)
باني (bānī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second objection is an independent argument unrelated to the first.
Correction: It builds on the first by showing that even if we accept an agent, we still need to account for matter, challenging the sufficiency of the ḥudūth argument.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, what does the intellect deem improbable?
A) Occurrence without an agent
B) Occurrence without matter and time
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that reason equally requires both an agent and pre-existing matter for any occurrence, challenging the sufficiency of the ḥudūth argument.

?

?
The objection suggests that reason requires both an agent and matter. Have you ever considered that your own argument for God might be incomplete if it doesn't account for the origin of matter? How does that make you feel about the strength of your faith?
?
Imagine you are confronted by someone who rejects God because of this very objection—that matter must be eternal. Can you honestly say you have a response that addresses the heart of their doubt, or would you rely on a rote answer? What would it take for you to truly understand this challenge?

Every New Event Requires a New Cause
The author argues for a new cause for each new event and refutes al-Ka'bi's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every new event requires a new cause and how al-Ka'bi's opposing view is rationally untenable.

Core Idea
Every new event demands a new cause—eternal conditions alone cannot produce a novel effect.

The author presents a fundamental principle of causality: each new event must have a new cause. He illustrates with a vivid example and then addresses a potential objection involving ancient hatred, showing that even then a new condition (ability/opportunity) is necessary. Finally, he refutes al-Ka'bi's position by showing it leads to either an infinite regress or an irrational denial of change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the flow from example to objection to conclusion.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the argument.
2
Analyze the initial example
Identify why the sky above and earth below cannot cause a sound at a specific moment. The author argues they existed before the sound, so they are not new causes.
This establishes the need for a new cause for each new event.
3
Examine the objection and response
Consider the objection that ancient hatred causes a later affliction. The author responds that even in that case, a new condition (ability/opportunity) is necessary for the effect to occur at that time.
This shows that opponents implicitly accept the requirement of a new condition.
4
Understand the dilemma for al-Ka'bi
The author presents two options: either every event depends on a previous event (leading to infinite regress, which al-Ka'bi rejects) or an eternal cause produces the new event without any change (which is rationally improbable).
Al-Ka'bi's view is caught between two unacceptable consequences.
5
Conclude the rational position
The majority of rational people accept that new events require new causes. Al-Ka'bi's refusal to accept this contradicts clear reason.
This lesson establishes a key principle of kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسبباً . فلو قيل : السبب في حدوثه : كون السماء فوقنا ، والأرض تحتنا ، حكم صريح العقل بفساد هذا التعليل . وكل عاقل يقول : إن كون السماء فوقنا ، والأرض تحتنا : كان حاصلا قبل هذا الوقت . فكيف يمكن جعله سبباً لحدوث الصوت في هذه الساعة . بل لابد لهذا الصوت الحادث من سبب حادث؟
فإن قالوا : أليس أن إنساناً مبغضاً لإنسان آخر من قديم الدهر ثم إنه يوقعه في بلاء ومحنة. فإنه يقال : إن وقوعه في هذه المحنة ، ليس لهذا السبب الحادث، بل إنما كان ، لأجل البغض القديم؟ فنقول : لابد وأن يقولوا : إن البغض القديم كان موجب اتصال الأفة إليه. أما وقت القدرة والمكنة ، فإنما حصل في هذه الساعة . فقد اعترفوا ههنا بحدوث هذا الأثر لهما ، وكان لأن شرائط التأثير إنما حصلت في هذا الوقت فقط.
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمة . فنقول : إنا إن قلنا بافتقار الحكم الحادث إلى سبب حادث ، لزمنا الاعتراف باستناد كل حادث إلى حادث آخر قبله . والكعبي لا يرضى بهذا القول البتة . وإن حكمنا بأن استناد كل حادث إلى مؤثر ، كان موجوداً قبله، من غير أن يحصل لذلك المؤثر ، تغير حال وتبدل البتة فهذا مما يستبعده كل العقلاء. فثبت : أن الذي يحكم به صريح عقل جمهور العقلاء ، فالكعبي لا يرتضيه ولا يقول به ، والذي يريده والكعبي ، ويقول به ، فالعقل لا يحكم به.
And a cause. If one says: the cause of its occurrence is that the sky is above us and the earth below us, the clear intellect judges the corruption of this explanation. Every rational person says: that the sky is above us and the earth below us existed before this time. So how can it be made a cause for the occurrence of the sound at this hour? Rather, this sound, [it being] an event, must have a new cause.
If they say: Is it not that a person who harbors hatred for another person from ancient times then causes him to fall into affliction and trial? It is said: his falling into this trial is not due to this new cause, but rather it was due to the ancient hatred? We say: They must say that the ancient hatred was a cause of the connection of harm to him. As for the time of ability and opportunity, that happened only at this hour. They thus admitted here the occurrence of this effect from both, because the conditions of influence only occurred at this time alone.
Once you understand this premise, we say: If we judge that the new judgment requires a new cause, we are obliged to admit that every event depends on another event before it. Al-Ka'bi is not at all satisfied with this statement. And if we judge that every event depends on an effect that existed before it, without any change of state or alteration occurring in that effect, this is something that all rational people deem improbable. Thus it is established: what the clear intellect of the majority of rational people judges, al-Ka'bi does not accept nor say; and what al-Ka'bi wants and says, the intellect does not judge.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a new cause for every new event, along with a refutation of al-Ka'bi's position. The author begins by giving an example: if someone claims that the cause of a sound's occurrence is that the sky is above and the earth below, the clear intellect immediately rejects this because these conditions existed before the sound. Thus the sound must have a new cause. Next, the author considers an objection: an ancient hatred can cause a later affliction. He responds by explaining that even in that case, the ancient hatred alone is not sufficient; the new element is the ability and opportunity (الوقت القدرة والمكنة) that arose at that moment. The opponents themselves admit that the effect is new, so they implicitly accept the need for a new condition. Then the author summarizes: if we affirm that every new judgment (new event) requires a new cause, we must admit an infinite regress of causes — but al-Ka'bi rejects this. If we instead hold that an eternal cause (a pre-existing effect) without any change produces the new event, that is deemed improbable by all rational people. Therefore, the judgment of the majority of rational people is that a new cause is needed, and al-Ka'bi's contrary view is against reason.

Key Terms
سبب حادث
حادث
بغض قديم
شرائط التأثير
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal cause (like ancient hatred) can produce a new effect without any new condition.
Correction: Even in that case, a new condition (ability/opportunity) is necessary for the effect to occur at a specific time.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the explanation that the cause of a sound is that the sky is above and the earth below?
A) Because these are not real causes of sound.
B) Because these conditions existed before the sound, so they cannot be a new cause.
C) Because the intellect cannot perceive such causes.
D) Because al-Ka'bi denied such causation.

Key Takeaway
Every new event requires a new cause; otherwise, we face an infinite regress or accept an unchanging cause producing a new effect, both of which are rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there 'ancient hatreds' or old excuses you still hold onto to justify your current actions, even though the real conditions have changed? What new 'time of ability and opportunity' is actually at work in your life right now that you have ignored?
?
When you delay repentance or a good deed, do you assume that your old intentions (like 'I will do it later') are enough to bring about the action? How does this lesson expose the emptiness of that assumption?
?
What new cause—a specific, fresh commitment—will you set into motion today to break a pattern you have attributed only to past circumstances? Do not let the intellect you claim to have be like al-Ka'bi's, rejecting clear truth.

The Intellect's Similar Judgment on Matter and Time as on a Cause
The author argues that the intellect also requires matter and time for an event, and refutes the objection that the need for a cause is stronger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the intellect requires matter and time for an event just as it requires a cause, and why the theologians' distinction is arbitrary.

Core Idea
The intellect finds it equally improbable for an event to lack matter and time as to lack a cause.

This passage presents a second objection in a theological debate about causality. The author argues that rational judgment demands both a cause and prior matter/time for any event. He then refutes the objection that the need for a cause is stronger, accusing theologians of inconsistency and arbitrariness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a building analogy to illustrate his point.
2
Identify the two improbabilities and the analogy
Find where the author states that the intellect deems it improbable for an event to lack matter/time just as it lacks a cause. Note the building example: a building without a builder is as improbable as a building without wood and stone.
3
Understand the refutation of the theologians' objection
The author considers the objection that the need for a cause is stronger. He refutes it by pointing out that the majority of rational people judge both equally, and the theologians' denial is mere dialectical wrangling.
The key term 'بديهيات' (self-evident truths) appears here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسؤال الثاني : إن العقل كما يستبعد الحدوث من غير فاعل ، فكذلك يستبعد الحدوث من غير سبق مادة ومدة . فإنه كما أن حدوث البناء من غير الباني مستبعد ، فكذلك حدوث البناء من غير تقديم الخشب والحجر واللبن مستبعد . وليس أحد الأستبعادين أقوى من الثاني. وأيضاً : حدوث الحادث لا في وقت ولا في زمان مخصوص : مستبعد أيضاً. فإن كان الاستبعاد في أحد البابين حجة ، فليكن في جميع المواضع حجة وإن جاز الإعراض عنه في بعض الصور ، فكذلك في سائر الصور .
فإن قالوا : إن حكم العقل بأن المحدث لابد له من فاعل ، أقوى من حكمه بأن المحدث لابد له من مادة ومدة . قلنا : نحن نرى الجمهور الأعظم من العقلاء ، لا يختلف حكم عقولهم في هذه الصورة . أما المتكلمون فإنهم يصححون ذلك الحكم في الحاجة إلى الفاعل، ويبطلونه في الحاجة إلى المادة والمدة . فنقول : أما أقاويل أرباب الجدل والتعنت فغير معتبرة . فإنهم قد تعودوا إنكار البديهيات . إذا احتاجوا إلى إنكارها ، وتعودوا ادعاء البديهة في غير موضعها ، إذا احتاجوا اليها . فثبت : أنه لا عبرة في هذا الباب بقولهم ، وإنما العبرة بالأحكام الجازمة المذكورة في عقول أهل السلامة . وترى بحكمهم في هذه الأبواب على السوية . فكان الفرق محض التحكم .
فهذا ما في هذا المقام من البحث .
The second question: The intellect, just as it deems improbable the occurrence of an event without an agent, similarly deems improbable the occurrence of an event without prior matter and time. For just as the occurrence of a building without a builder is improbable, so too the occurrence of a building without the prior provision of wood, stone, and brick is improbable. Neither of the two improbabilities is stronger than the other. Also, the occurrence of an event at no specific time or at no particular moment is also improbable. If improbability in one of the two categories is considered a proof, then let it be a proof in all cases; and if it is permissible to disregard it in some instances, then similarly in all other instances.
If they say: The intellect's judgment that an event must have an agent is stronger than its judgment that an event must have matter and time. We say: We see that the vast majority of rational people do not differ in the judgment of their intellects in this matter. As for the theologians (متكلمون), they affirm that judgment regarding the need for an agent, but invalidate it regarding the need for matter and time. We say: The sayings of the people of dialectical wrangling and obstinacy are not credible. For they have become accustomed to denying self-evident truths when they need to deny them, and they have become accustomed to claiming self-evidence where it does not belong when they need to. Thus it is established: there is no regard in this matter for their sayings; regard is only for the decisive judgments present in the intellects of sound people. And you see that their judgments in these matters are on equal footing. So the distinction is pure arbitrariness.
This is what is in this subject of discussion.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a second objection (or second argument) concerning whether an event requires matter and time in addition to a cause. The author begins by stating the second question: just as the intellect finds it improbable that an event occurs without an agent (فاعل), it similarly finds it improbable that an event occurs without prior matter (مادة) and time (مدة). He illustrates with the example of a building: it is equally improbable for a building to exist without a builder as it is for it to exist without the prior existence of wood, stone, and brick. Therefore, neither improbability is stronger than the other. Additionally, he notes that an event occurring without a specific time or moment is also deemed improbable by the intellect.
The author then considers a potential objection: one might argue that the judgment that an event needs an agent is stronger than the judgment that it needs matter and time. He refutes this by pointing out that the vast majority of rational people judge both equally. The theologians (متكلمون), however, affirm the need for an agent but deny the need for matter and time. The author dismisses their position as mere dialectical wrangling (جدل) and obstinacy (تعنت), accusing them of denying self-evident truths when convenient and claiming self-evidence where it does not belong. Ultimately, he concludes that the only reliable judgments are the decisive ones present in the intellects of sound people, and in these matters their judgments are equal. Hence, the distinction made by the theologians is pure arbitrariness (محض التحكم).

Key Terms
فاعل
مادة
مدة
الاستبعاد
بديهيات
أهل السلامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only the need for an agent is rationally necessary, while matter and time are merely conventional.
Correction: The author argues that the intellect judges both equally; the need for matter and time is as self-evident as the need for a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does the intellect's judgment about the need for matter and time compare to its judgment about the need for a cause?
A) The need for a cause is stronger.
B) The need for matter and time is stronger.
C) Both are equally improbable if missing.
D) Only the need for a cause is a rational judgment.

Key Takeaway
The intellect's requirement for matter and time is as essential as the requirement for a cause; dismissing it is arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been guilty of denying an obvious truth simply because it was inconvenient for your position, like the theologians who deny self-evident truths when needed? How can you guard against this in your faith and daily judgments?
?
The author says sound people judge equally on the need for agent, matter, and time. Is there an area in your life where you make arbitrary distinctions, treating one necessity as stronger while ignoring another? What step will you take to align your intellect with truth?
?
What self-evident truth about your Creator, your own existence, or your accountability have you been avoiding, knowing it demands a radical change in how you live? When will you stop pretending and start acting on it?

Proof That Actions Are from the Self
Author proves that actions are from ourselves via necessary connection of motives and deterrents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that our actions are necessarily from ourselves based on the necessary connection between pure motives and deterrents and the occurrence of actions.

Core Idea
Our actions are from us because they necessarily follow our pure motives and deterrents.

In this passage, the author presents a logical proof that actions originate from the self. He uses two concrete examples—thirst driving a person to drink and knowledge of fire's harm preventing entry—to establish that when motives are pure and deterrents absent, action is necessary; when deterrents are pure, action is impossible. From this necessary connection, he concludes that actions must be from us, as otherwise the link would break.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two examples and the logical structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the proof for necessary occurrence from pure motives and the proof for impossibility from pure deterrents. Understand each example.
The first uses a fasting person drinking; the second uses entering fire.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
Note that the author establishes necessary connections: pure motives → action must occur; pure deterrents → action cannot occur. This is the foundation.
The author uses 'impossible' and 'necessary' in a rational sense.
4
See how the conclusion follows
The author argues that if actions were not from us, they could occur against our will or fail to occur despite our will, contradicting the necessary connections. Thus, actions are from us.
This is a proof by contradiction.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider: This proof establishes that we are the agents of our actions, which is crucial for moral responsibility and later arguments about the need for a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوعها عند دواعينا الخالصة، ويجب انتفاؤها عند قيام الصوارف . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب القطع بكونها واقعة بنا لا بغيرنا . والدليل على وجوب وقوعها عند حصول الدواعي القوية : أن الصائم في الصيف إذا اشتدت شهوته لشرب الماء والشارع يأمره بالشرب ، والطبيب يأمره بالشرب ، وعلم هذا الإنسان أنه لا مضرة في هذا الشرب، لا في دينه ولا في دنياه : فإنه يحصل له عند هذا الشرب أعظم اللذات . فإن عند حضور هذه الاعتقادات في قلبه : يستحيل أن لا يشرب الماء . فثبت : أن الفعل عند توفر الدواعي الخالصة عن الصوارف واجب الوقوع . والدليل على أن الفعل ممتنع الوقوع عند حصول الصوارف الخالصة : أن من علم ما في دخول النار من الضرر العظيم، وعلم أنه لا منفعة له في دخولها البتة . لا في الحال ولا في الاستقبال ، ولا يتوسل بذلك الدخول إلى دفع ضرر أعظم ضررا منه فإن عند حضور هذه الاعتقادات في قلبه ، يمتنع منه دخول النار . فيثبت بهذا البيان : أن عند خلوص الدواعي ، يجب حصول الأفعال ، وعند خلوص الصوارف ، يمتنع حصولها . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : وجب القطع بأن هذه الأفعال واقعة بنا لا بغيرنا . لأنه لو لم يكن وقوعها بنا ، لكان وقوعها إما أن يكون لا لمؤثر ، أو لمؤثر غيرنا . وعلى التقديرين فكان لا يمتنع تقع وإن كرهناها ، ولا أن لا تقع وإن أردناها . كما أن فعل زيد ، لما لم يكن واقعا بعمرو ، لا جرم ربما وقع وإن كرهه عمرو، وربما لا يقع وإن أراده عمرو . وذلك يقدح فيما بيناه من أن تلك الأفعال واجبة الوقوع عند توفر الدواعي ، وممتنعة الوقوع عند توفر الصوارف. فيثبت بهذا البيان الذي ذكرناه : أن أفعالنا واقعة بنا . وهذا هو إثبات أصل القياس المذكور .
Their occurrence upon our pure motives, and necessary non-occurrence when deterrents are present. And since the matter is so, it is necessary to be certain that they occur by us and not by others. The proof for the necessity of their occurrence upon obtaining strong motives: that the fasting person in summer, when his desire for drinking water intensifies and the legislator commands him to drink, and the doctor commands him to drink, and this person knows that there is no harm in this drinking, neither in his religion nor in his worldly affairs—then at this drinking he experiences the greatest pleasures. For when these beliefs are present in his heart, it is impossible that he does not drink water. So it is established: that the action, when pure motives free of deterrents are present, is necessarily occurring. And the proof that the action is impossible of occurrence when pure deterrents are present: that a person knows the great harm in entering the Fire, and knows that there is no benefit for him in entering it at all, neither in the present nor in the future, and he does not use that entry to repel a greater harm—then when these beliefs are present in his heart, entering the Fire is impossible for him. So by this exposition it is established: that when motives are pure, actions necessarily occur, and when deterrents are pure, they are impossible of occurrence. And when this is established, we say: it is necessary to be certain that these actions occur by us and not by others. Because if their occurrence were not by us, then their occurrence would either be without an effectant, or by an effectant other than us. On both suppositions, it would not be impossible that they occur even if we dislike them, nor that they do not occur even if we desire them. Just as the action of Zayd, since it does not occur by 'Amr, indeed sometimes it occurs even if 'Amr dislikes it, and sometimes it does not occur even if 'Amr desires it. That contradicts what we have established: that those actions are necessarily occurring when motives are present, and impossible of occurrence when deterrents are present. So by this exposition that we have mentioned, it is established: that our actions occur by us. And this is the establishment of the foundation of the mentioned syllogism.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a proof that our actions are from ourselves. The author argues using two examples: one where pure motives necessitate the action (the thirsty person who knows drinking is beneficial and permissible) and one where pure deterrents prevent the action (the person who knows the harm of fire and has no benefit). From these necessary connections, he concludes that actions are determined by our motives and deterrents. He then argues that if actions were not from us, they could occur against our will or fail to occur despite our will, which would contradict the observed necessary connection. This proves that actions are from us, establishing the foundation for the subsequent syllogism about the need for a creator.

Key Terms
دواعي (dawā‘ī)
صوارف (ṣawārif)
فعل (fi‘l)
مؤثر (mu’aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that necessary occurrence implies coercion from an external source.
Correction: The author's point is that the necessity arises from internal motives and knowledge, not external compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the author's proof, what is established by the example of the person who knows the harm of fire and has no benefit from entering it?
A) That he will necessarily enter the fire if he desires it.
B) That he will necessarily avoid the fire due to pure deterrents.
C) That the action of entering is impossible because of external force.
D) That his action is not from himself.

Key Takeaway
Actions are proven to be from ourselves because their occurrence and non-occurrence are necessarily tied to our pure motives and deterrents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a pure motive to do good, do you act on it immediately, or do you hesitate despite knowing there is no harm? What is holding you back?
?
Think of a time you deliberately ignored a clear deterrent because of a fleeting desire. What does that reveal about the purity of your deterrents? Will you let that happen again?
?
If you truly believe that your actions are from you and follow your motives and deterrents, how will you change the way you respond to the call of prayer or the temptation of sin starting today?

Establishing Temporal Origination as the Cause of Need for a Creator
The author argues that need for a doer is due to temporal origination (ḥudūth).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn prove that the need of an action for an agent is due to its temporal origination (ḥudūth) by eliminating other possible causes.

Core Idea
An action's need for an agent is not due to its eternity or its persistence, but solely because it begins to exist in time.

In this passage, the author sets out to prove the foundational causal principle that the need for a creator is rooted in the temporal origination of created things. He does this by a logical division (taqsīm) of all possible reasons an action might need an agent, and then systematically refuting those alternatives. This argument is the hinge on which the entire proof for God's existence turns, for if the cause of need is indeed ḥudūth, then everything that begins to exist requires a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read and mark the logical divisions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. As you read, identify the three possible causes listed for an action's need for an agent.
Notice the author uses 'إما... أو...' to set up the division.
2
Understand the refutation of eternity as cause
The first possibility is prior eternity (qidam). Why does the author reject it? Because prior non-existence has no relation to the agent.
Non-existence cannot be a cause of need.
3
Understand the two refutations of subsistence as cause
The third possibility is subsistence (baqā'). The author gives two reasons: 1) It would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. 2) Examples like a building remaining after its builder show subsistence doesn't require ongoing agency.
Focus on the logic of the first reason and the intuitive force of the second.
4
Conclude the cause and see its application
With the first and third eliminated, temporal origination (ḥudūth) remains as the only possible cause. The author then applies this to bodies: since bodies are temporally originated, they need an agent.
This proof assumes prior establishment that bodies are temporally originated.
5
Reflect on the proof's structure
Consider why this method of division and refutation is effective. It systematically eliminates alternatives, leaving only one valid conclusion.
This is a classic kalam argument pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات العلة: فهو أنا ندعي أن احتياج أفعالنا إلينا، إنما كان لأجل حدوثها. والدليل عليه: أن احتياج أفعالنا إلينا إما أن يكون لقدمها السابق، أو لحدوثها، أو لبقائها. والأول باطل لأن العدم السابق لا تعلق له بالفاعل. والثالث أيضاً باطل لوجهين: الأول: إنه لو احتاج الباقي إلى الفاعل، لكان هذا تحصيلا للحاصل وهو محال. والثاني: إن البناء قد يبقى بعد فناء الباني، والنقش قد يبقى بعد فناء النقاش. ولما بطل هذان القسمان، علمنا أن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث. فثبت أن أفعالنا محتاجة إلينا، لأجل حدوثها، وثبت أن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث. ولما ثبت أن الأجسام محدثة، كانت علة الحاجة إلى الفاعل حاصلة فيها، فوجب القطع بافتقارها إلى الفاعل. هذا تمام تقرير هذا الدليل.
And as for establishing the cause: it is that we claim that the need of our actions for us is only because of their temporal origination (ḥudūth). The proof of it: that the need of our actions for us is either due to their prior eternity (qidam), or due to their temporal origination, or due to their subsistence (baqā'). The first is invalid because prior non-existence has no relation to the agent. The third is also invalid for two reasons: first, if the subsisting thing needed the agent, this would be a obtaining of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. Second, the building may remain after the builder perishes, and the engraving may remain after the engraver perishes. When these two divisions are invalidated, we know that the cause of need is temporal origination. So it is established that our actions need us because of their temporal origination, and it is established that the cause of need is temporal origination. And when it is established that bodies are temporally originated (muḥdatha), the cause of need for an agent is present in them, so it is necessary to decisively conclude their dependence on an agent. This is the complete exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing that the cause (ʿilla) for why an action needs an agent is its temporal origination (ḥudūth). The author first states the claim: our actions need us only because they occur in time. He then divides the possible reasons for needing an agent into three: the action's pre-eternity (qidam), its temporal origination, or its continued subsistence (baqā'). He refutes the first because pre-eternal non-existence has no connection to an agent – non-existence does not need anything. He then refutes the third with two arguments: first, if a subsisting thing needed an agent, that would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is absurd; second, examples like a building remaining after its builder or an engraving after its engraver show that subsistence does not require continued agency. With the first and third eliminated, the only remaining cause is temporal origination. The author then extends this: since it is already established that bodies are temporally originated, they necessarily possess the cause of needing an agent, so it is certain that they depend on an agent. The proof follows a classic kalam method of division (taqsīm) and refutation of alternatives.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
حدوث
قدم
بقاء
تحصيل الحاصل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that bodies are temporally originated without justification.
Correction: The author explicitly says 'when it is established that bodies are temporally originated' – this proof relies on a prior independent proof (e.g., the argument from accidents). It does not prove ḥudūth here but uses it as a premise.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the cause of an action's need for an agent?
A) Its prior eternity
B) Its subsistence
C) Its temporal origination
D) Its non-existence

Key Takeaway
The need of an action for an agent is due solely to its temporal origination, as established by eliminating the alternatives of pre-eternity and subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence began in time and therefore depends entirely on God, what does that mean for your sense of self-sufficiency? Do you live as if you are self-originating, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence on Him?
?
The proof shows that subsistence does not require the agent—the building remains after the builder perishes. Yet do you treat your own existence as if it continues independently of God? How would your daily attitude change if you truly believed that every moment you exist is only due to God's constant creative act?
?
When you consider that your actions need you only because they begin in time, does that humble you? Do you realize that even your ability to act is a gift from the One who brought you into existence? How can you cultivate a deeper reliance on Him in your decisions today?

Refuting the Objection from Will and Action
The author refutes an objection by posing a dilemma of regress or determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that human actions are not from us by exposing a dilemma of infinite regress or divine determinism.

Core Idea
The link between will and action either leads to an infinite regress or makes God the true agent.

This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position that human actions are created by God. The objector claims that actions necessarily follow our willings, proving they are from us. But the author turns this argument around, showing that if willings themselves are from us, we need an infinite chain; if from God, then actions are not independent. This clever refutation exposes the flaw in the Mu'tazilī reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the beginning of the passage where the objector says actions occur when motives are present and are absent when deterrents are present, concluding actions are from us.
The objector assumes a necessary connection between will and action.
2
Identify the author's reply
Notice that the author says this reasoning does not hold for the Mu'tazila because of what follows.
The reply targets the Mu'tazilī position that humans create their own actions.
3
Follow the dilemma's first horn
Trace the argument: if the servant creates his own willings, then each willing requires another willing, leading to an infinite regress.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible in Islamic theology.
4
Follow the dilemma's second horn
If God creates the willings, then actions become necessary from the servant, negating independence.
This contradicts the Mu'tazilī doctrine that humans are independent agents.
5
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that the objection fails and actually undermines the Mu'tazilī position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجوه :
الأول : لا نسلم وقوع أفعالنا بنا . قوله : و الدليل عليه : أنه يجب وقوعها عند دواعينا ، ويجب بقاؤها على العدم عند صوارفنا . وذلك يقتضي وقوعها بنا ، قلنا : القول بأن أفعالنا يجب وقوعها عند دواعينا ، ويمتنع وقوعها عند عدم دواعينا : لا يستقيم على قول المعتزلة . والدليل عليه : أن تلك الأفعال إذا كانت واجبة الوقوع عند حصول هذه الإرادات ، وكانت ممتنعة الحصول عند حصول الكراهات ، فحصول تلك الإرادات والكراهات ، إن كان من العبد ، افتقر العبد في إحداثها إلى إرادات أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال . وإن لم يكن حدوثها من العبد ، بل من الله تعالى ، فعندما يحدث الله تلك الدواعي في العبد : كان الفعل واجب الصدور عنه وعندما لا يحدثها فيه ، كان الفعل ممتنع الصدور عنه . وحينئذ لا يكون مستقلا بفعل نفسه . وذلك ضد مذهب المعتزلة .
And the objection against it is from several aspects:
The first: We do not concede that our actions happen by us. He says: The proof of it is that they must occur when our motives (dawāʿīnā) are present, and must remain non-existent when our deterrents (ṣawārifunā) are present. That entails that they happen by us. We say: The statement that our actions must occur when our motives are present and cannot occur when our motives are absent is not sound according to the Muʿtazila. The proof of it: that if those actions are necessarily occurring when these willings (irādāt) occur, and are impossible when these aversions (karāhāt) occur, then the occurrence of those willings and aversions: if it is from the servant, then the servant would need other willings to produce them, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. And if their occurrence is not from the servant, but from God Most High, then when God creates those motives in the servant, the action is necessarily emitted from him, and when He does not create them in him, the action is impossible to emit from him. At that point, he is not independent in his own action. That contradicts the doctrine of the Muʿtazila.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is the first objection and its answer within a larger proof for a creator. The objector denies that human actions are performed by us. He presents his proof: actions necessarily occur when we will them and necessarily do not occur when we dislike them, which indicates they are from us. The author responds by arguing that this very reasoning is inconsistent with Muʿtazilī doctrine (libre will). He points out that the necessitation of actions by willings and aversions leads to a dilemma. If the willings and aversions themselves are produced by the servant, then producing them would require prior willings, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). If they are produced by God, then the servant's actions become necessitated by God's creation of motives, making the servant not an independent agent. Thus, the objection fails either way, and it actually undermines the Muʿtazilī position that humans create their own actions. The answer shows that the objector's assumption of necessary connection between will and action does not support human agency but rather leads to determinism or infinite regress.

Key Terms
دواعي
صوارف
إرادة
كراهة
تسلسل
استقلال
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If actions always follow our willings, that proves we are the independent agents of those actions.
Correction: The author shows that the willings themselves are either created by us (leading to regress) or by God (making us not independent). So the correlation does not prove independence.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the servant himself creates his own willings?
A) The actions would be from God
B) The servant would be an independent agent
C) It would lead to an infinite regress
D) The actions would not occur

Key Takeaway
The objection that actions follow our will does not prove human agency; it either leads to infinite regress or divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to thank Him for every good choice, recognizing His creation of your desire? What is one specific action today you can consciously attribute to His giving you the motive?

Do you truly believe that without God's constant creation of your intentions, you could not even form a desire? How will this change your prayer and reliance on Him?
?
The Muʿtazilites thought that the correlation between will and action proved human agency. But the author shows this logic collapses. Are there areas in your life where you assume your own independence? What would it mean to live as if your every action is sustained by God's will? What one habit can you change today to reflect that reliance?

Third and Fourth Objections on Hudduth and Need for an Agent
Two objections on causation are raised and the fourth is refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third and fourth objections challenge the argument from origination and how the fourth objection is refuted by showing circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning undoes the fourth objection, but the third objection stands unresolved.

This passage presents two critical objections to the argument that origination (ḥudūth) implies a need for an agent. The third objection questions whether origination itself is the cause of that need, while the fourth objection argues that the origination of our actions is restricted and does not entail the unrestricted origination of the world. The author then refutes the fourth objection by exposing a circularity in its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the dialectical structure. Note that the third objection is a brief challenge, while the fourth is more detailed and includes a response.
Pay attention to the phrases 'السؤال الثالث' and 'السؤال الرابع'.
2
Identify the two objections
Locate the third objection: it disputes that origination is the cause of need. Then locate the fourth objection: it distinguishes restricted origination from unrestricted origination.
The third objection does not provide a full argument; it promises future proofs.
3
Analyze the refutation of the fourth objection
Notice how the author responds by considering a possible counter-argument: that 'origination as such' is the cause. The author shows this leads to either self-evidence (making the proof unnecessary) or circularity (if it requires proof).
The key concept is 'الدور' (circularity).
4
Reflect on the outcome
Ask yourself: Why does the third objection remain unanswered? What does this tell us about the strength of the original argument?
The fourth objection is refuted, but the third still poses a challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على سبيل الاتفاق ، إلا أنه اتفق في هذه الصورة الواحدة من الاتفاقيات أنها بقيت دائمة على نسق واحد ؟ وذلك لأن بتقدير أن لا يكون الحدوث على سبيل الاتفاق (١) ممتنعا في أول العقل [ لم (٣) ] يكن كون بعض الاتفاقيات دائما على نسق ( واحد ) (٣) ممتنعا أيضاً في أول العقل . وحينئذ يسقط هذا الدليل . وهذا السؤال واقع على هذا الدليل .
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أن أفعالنا واقعة بنا . فلم قلتم : إن علة هذه الحاجة هي الحدوث ؟ وتقريره : إنا سنقيم الدلائل الكثيرة في مسألة الحدوث والقدم ، على أن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر ، ولا جزءاً من هذه العلة ، ولا شرطا لها .
السؤال الرابع : سلمنا أن حدوث أفعالنا علة لاحتياج أفعالنا إلينا . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم منه أن يكون حدوث العالم علة لاحتياج العالم إلى الفاعل ؟ وتقريره : إن حدوث أفعالنا عبارة عن حدوث مقيد ، بقيد كونه حاصلا في أفعالنا . ولا يلزم من كون هذا المجموع علة للحاجة ، كون الحدوث فقط علة للحاجة .
فإن قلتم : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن ذلك الحدوث ما كان علة لتلك الحاجة لكون ذلك الحدوث الخاص ، بل لمجرد كونه حدوثا . فنقول : فهذا إنما يتم لو ثبت لكم : أن الحدوث من حيث هو حدوث ، علة للحاجة . وإن كان ذلك معلوما بالبديهة ، فقد ضاع سعيكم في تقرير هذا الدليل الطويل . وإن كان ذلك محتاجاً إلى الحجة . فأنتم ما ذكرتم إلا هذا القياس . وقد ظهر في هذا السؤال : أن هذا القياس لا يصح ، إلا إذا ثبت أن الحدوث من حيث هو حدوث ، علة للحاجة . وحينئذ يتوقف الدليل على المدلول ، والمدلول على الدليل . فيلزم الدور .
وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الموضع . وبالله التوفيق
By way of coincidence? Rather it happened in this one particular case of coincidences that they remained perpetual in one manner? That is because given that origination by way of coincidence is not impossible in the beginning of the intellect, it would not be impossible also in the beginning of the intellect for some coincidences to be perpetual in one manner. Then this proof collapses. And this question is directed at this proof.
The third question: We grant that our actions occur by us. But why do you say that the cause of this need is origination? And its explanation: We will present many proofs in the issue of origination and eternity, that origination cannot be a cause for the need of an effect, nor a part of this cause, nor a condition for it.
The fourth question: We grant that the origination of our actions is a cause for the need of our actions for us. But why do you say that it necessarily follows that the origination of the world is a cause for the need of the world for an agent? Its explanation: The origination of our actions is an expression of a restricted origination, with the restriction of its occurring in our actions. It does not follow from this composite being a cause for need that origination alone is a cause for need.
If you say: We know necessarily that that origination was not a cause for that need because of that particular origination, but merely because it is origination. We say: This is only valid if it is established for you that origination as such is a cause for need. If that is known by intuition, then your effort in presenting this lengthy proof has been wasted. If it requires proof, you have only mentioned this syllogism. And it has become apparent in this question that this syllogism is not valid unless it is established that origination as such is a cause for need. Then the proof depends on the conclusion, and the conclusion on the proof, resulting in circularity.
This completes the discussion in this place. And success is from God.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange containing two objections (the third and fourth) against a previous proof, along with a response to the fourth objection. The first objection (third) challenges the claim that 'origination' (ḥudūth) is the cause of the need for an agent, asserting that origination cannot be a cause, part of a cause, or condition for need. The second objection (fourth) argues that the origination of our actions is a restricted origination (muqayyad) tied to our actions, so it does not follow that the unrestricted origination of the world would require an agent. The author then responds to the fourth objection by considering a possible counterargument: one might claim that the cause of need is simply 'origination as such' (al-ḥudūth min ḥaythu huwa ḥudūth), not the particular restricted instance. The author points out that if this is known by intuition, then the lengthy proof is unnecessary; if it requires proof, then the objection's syllogism depends on establishing that origination as such is a cause, which is exactly what needs to be proved, leading to circular reasoning (dawr). Thus the fourth objection is refuted, and the third remains as a challenge. The passage concludes with 'This completes the discussion in this place'.

Key Terms
الحدوث
علة الحاجة
الدور
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the fourth objection is successfully refuted by simply noting the distinction between restricted and unrestricted origination.
Correction: The author shows that even that distinction fails if one tries to base the cause on 'origination as such', because it leads to circularity.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth objection claim about the relationship between restricted and unrestricted origination?
A) That restricted origination implies unrestricted origination as a cause for need.
B) That unrestricted origination is the cause for need.
C) That the origination of actions is restricted, so it does not entail that the origination of the world is a cause for need.
D) That both restricted and unrestricted origination are causes for need.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection is refuted by exposing circular reasoning, but the third objection remains unresolved, leaving the argument from origination partially challenged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that the author leaves the third objection unanswered. This means a rational argument for God's existence faces a challenge that is not resolved here. How does this affect your confidence in proofs for God? Do you rely on reason alone, or do you have other grounds for certainty? What will you do to address this intellectual doubt—ignore it, seek further reading, or deepen your trust beyond arguments? Commit today to honestly confronting unresolved questions about your faith.
?
Imagine you are in a debate and someone raises the third objection: 'Why is origination itself the cause of need?' You don't have an immediate answer. How do you respond? Does this unsettle you? Reflect on your own understanding: have you ever truly grasped why the contingency of the world points to a Creator? If not, what steps will you take to move from intellectual assent to heartfelt conviction? Do not let this question slip away—let it drive you to seek deeper knowledge.
?
The fourth objection is refuted by exposing circular reasoning, but the third remains. Are you comfortable with incomplete answers in your faith? Think of a time when you faced a doubt about God's existence that was not fully resolved. Did you push it aside, or did it lead you to a stronger foundation? Today, pick one unresolved question about your belief—perhaps from this lesson or elsewhere—and decide to research it sincerely, not to win an argument but to find truth for your own soul. Write it down and set a time this week to explore it.

Objections to the Design Argument and Refutation of the Generative Power
Raising two objections about organ formation and refuting the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the design argument from organ formation and the detailed refutation of the second objection involving the generative power.

Core Idea
Blind natural powers cannot produce ordered structures; only an intelligent creator can.

The design argument from the human body is one of the most powerful proofs for God's existence. Critics have raised objections suggesting that natural mechanisms within the body, such as properties of the womb or a generative power in the sperm, could explain the formation of organs. In this passage, the author presents two such objections and then systematically refutes the second, showing that a power without consciousness would only produce random aggregates, not the intricate order we observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the full English translation. Let the author's own argument flow through before you analyze.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two objections
Locate the first objection (womb properties) and the second objection (generative power). Notice how the second objection is introduced with 'وهي السؤال الثاني'.
3
Follow the refutation's division
The author splits the generative power into two possibilities: one with knowledge and power, one devoid of consciousness. Trace this division.
The refutation begins at 'فإن قالوا: هذا محال'.
4
Understand why each possibility fails
The first possibility (conscious power) is false because the soul at its peak cannot create the body. The second (blind power) is false because a blind power in uniform matter would only produce random, disordered results like spheres.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum argument.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Realize that since both possibilities are false, the generative power cannot explain the ordered body, thus the design argument stands. Reflect on the necessity of an intelligent creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القرآن العظيم ، في أكثر من ثمانين موضعا (۱) فإن قيل : السؤال عليه من وجوه : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل واحد من أجزاء الرحم مخصوص بخاصية لأجلها ، توجب حدوث الكيفية المخصوصة في الجزء الذي يتصل به من المني ، فيكون تولد الأعضاء المختلفة لهذا السبب ؟ وهي السؤال الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : حل في جسم المني قوة مخصوصة ، المسماة بالقوة المولدة ، وتلك القوة المولدة هي التي أفادت هذه الآثار المختلفة وفعلت هذه الآثار العجيبة ؟ فإن قالوا : هذا محال لأن هذه القوة الحالة في المني ، المسماة بالقوة المولدة، إما أن يكون لها علم بوجود المنافع والمصالح وقدرة على تحصيل الوجه الأصلح الأصوب من التركيبات الموافقة وإما أن يقال : هذه القوة قوة لا شعور لها [ بشيء ] ولا قدرة لها على تحصيل شيء ، بل هي قوة خالية عن الشعور والإدراك أما القسم الأول : فهو باطل . إذ لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكانت تلك القوة عالمة بوجود المنافع والمصالح ، والمضار ، والمفاسد ، وقادرة على تحصيل المنافع ، ودفع المضار ، ولكانت هي التي خلقت هذا البدن ، وركبت هذه البنية على هذا التركيب العجيب ، والتأليف الغريب ، لكنا نعلم بالضرورة أنه ليس الأمر كذلك لأن القوى البدنية ، والنفس الإنسانية [ في حال كمال العقل ، وفي حال كمال البدن أكمل حالا ما كانت في أول الأمر حال ما كان البدن نطفة ، فلما لم تقدر النفس الإنسانية ] حال كمالها على شيء من هذه الأحوال ، ففي الحالة التي كانت في غاية النقصان والتصور. كيف يعقل أن يقال : إنها قدرت على هذه الأفعال [ العجيبة ] ؟ وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : القوة المولدة قوة خالية عن الشعور ، والإدراك ، والفهم . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير تكون قوة موجبة لذاتها ، أثرا من الآثار من غير شعور ، ولا إدراك ، ولا قصد ، والمادة بسيطة متشابهة ، وحينئذ ترجع الإلزامات المذكورة من كون ذلك الإنسان كرة واحدة ، أو كرات مضموم بعضها إلى بعض ، من غير أن يكون وضع الأعضاء وترتيبها معتبرا ، بل يكون متعسر الترتيب ، واقعا كما تقع الأشياء الاتفاقية وكل ذلك باطل .
The Great Quran, in more than eighty places. If it is said: The objection to it is from [several] aspects: First: Why is it not permissible to say that each part of the womb is particularized with a specific property for its sake, which necessitates the occurrence of the specific quality in the part of the sperm that connects to it, so the generation of the different organs occurs for this reason? The second question: Why is it not permissible to say that a specific power, called the generative power, settled in the substance of the sperm, and that generative power is the one that produced these different effects and performed these wondrous acts? If they say: This is impossible because this power settled in the sperm, called the generative power, either has knowledge of the existence of benefits and interests and power to attain the most correct and most proper aspect among the compatible compositions, or it is said: This power is a power having no consciousness of anything and no power to attain anything, rather it is a power devoid of sensation and perception. As for the first division: it is false. For if the matter were so, that power would be knowing of the existence of benefits and interests, harms and corruptions, and able to attain benefits and repel harms, and it would be the one that created this body and composed this structure in this wondrous composition and strange arrangement; but we know necessarily that it is not so because the bodily powers and the human soul [in the state of perfect intellect, and in the state of perfect body, their most perfect state was not in the first state when the body was a sperm, so when the human soul] in its perfect state could not manage any of these states, then in the state that was in extreme deficiency and conceiving, how can it be said that it had power over these wondrous acts? As for the second division: that it is said: The generative power is a power devoid of sensation, perception, and understanding. We say: On this assumption, it would be a power necessitating by itself an effect from effects without sensation, perception, or intention, and the matter is simple and similar, and then the previously mentioned implications return: that that human would be one sphere, or spheres attached to each other, without consideration of the placement and arrangement of organs, but rather the arrangement would be difficult, occurring as accidental things occur. And all that is false.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the teleological argument for God's existence (specifically from the development of the human body) and then refutes the second objection in detail. The author begins by noting that the Quran itself mentions creation in many places. Then the objector raises two questions: First, why cannot each part of the womb have a specific property that causes the appropriate quality in the connecting sperm, thus generating different organs? Second, why cannot a special generative power residing in the sperm produce the different organs and effects? The author's response ('If they say: This is impossible...') focuses on the second objection. He splits the generative power into two possibilities: either it has knowledge and power (like a conscious agent), or it is devoid of consciousness. The first possibility is false: if the power had knowledge and power, it would be the creator of the body, but we know necessarily that the soul at its peak cannot even manage such things, let alone the sperm's power in its initial deficient state. The second possibility is also false: if the power is blind and without intention, then the matter (the sperm) is simple and uniform, so the only possible results would be random aggregates (like spheres), not the orderly arrangement of organs we observe. Thus both possibilities are false, so the generative power cannot explain the structured body. The entire objection is thereby refuted.

Key Terms
القوة المولدة
قوة خالية عن الشعور
الوجه الأصلح الأصوب
التركيبات الموافقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A blind natural power can produce ordered structures through trial and error.
Correction: Without knowledge and intention, only random results (like spheres) are possible, not complex organs.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of the second possibility (generative power without consciousness), what does the author conclude would be the result?
A) The organs would be perfectly arranged anyway.
B) The human would be one sphere or balls attached without order.
C) The power would learn over time.
D) The organs would be formed by accident but still function.

Key Takeaway
A power without intelligence and intention cannot produce the ordered complexity of the human body; therefore, the design argument remains sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own body, do you ever reduce its complexity to mere biological processes? After reading this refutation, how does the argument that a blind power cannot produce such order challenge that reductionism? Will you pause today to thank the Creator for His deliberate design?
?
The author points out that even the human soul in its most perfect state cannot create a body. If you are honest, where in your life do you take credit for abilities that ultimately depend on a body you did not create? How can you cultivate humility before God in light of this dependency?
?
This lesson argues that without consciousness and intention, only random outcomes occur. Think of an area in your life where you have expected order to emerge from random choices or habits. What specific step can you take today to align your actions with purposeful intention under God's guidance?

The Objection: Why Can’t Nature Produce Perfect Acts Without Knowledge?
Objector's rejoinder and challenge to the author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's challenge to the author's earlier refutation of natural causality, and the conceptual space it opens for a deeper proof.

Core Idea
The objector concedes nature lacks knowledge but asks: why can't perfect acts still come from it?

After the author argued that nature cannot be the source of order without knowledge, the objector pushes back. He cites authorities who claim nature is wise, then concedes the point about knowledge but insists that perfect actions might still proceed from nature. This sets the stage for the author's two proofs that perfect order requires knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's rejoinder
Read the Arabic passage that records the objector's response. Notice how he first appeals to the authority of physicians and philosophers who say nature is wise.
2
Understand the concession
The objector then concedes: even if nature is not described with knowledge and power, why can it not be the source of precise actions? This is a crucial shift in the debate.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: if nature lacks knowledge, can it still produce ordered effects? The objector thinks yes, but the author will show otherwise.
This challenge sets up the need for a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب السائل عنه : إن كثيراً من الأطباء ، والفلاسفة يقولون : إن الطبيعة حكيمة [وإنها ] كاملة الحكمة ، والقدرة ، والمعرفة . وإذا كان هذا قولاً قاله بعض الناس ، فما الدليل على فساده ؟ سلمنا : أنها ليست موصوفة بالعلم والقدرة ، لكن لم لا يجوز صدور الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة عنها ؟ ويدل على أن الذي ذكرناه محتمل وجهين : الأول : إن الأفعال الصناعية ، إذا صارت ملكات راسخة في النفس ، وبلغت إلى حد الكمال والتمام، فإن العقلاء إذا أرادوا وصفها بالتمام والكمال شبهوها بالطبيعة ، فيقولون : إن هذا العمل صار طبيعياً له ، وهذه الحرفة صارت طبيعة له ، ولولا أنه تقرر في عقولهم : أن أفعال الطبيعة أكمل وأقوى وأتم ، لما شبهوا الصناعة بالطبيعة ، حال محاولة وصفها [ بالكمال والتمام ] . والثاني : إن من لم يعلم حرفة الكتابة ، أو حرفة ضرب الطنبور ، فإنه ما دام يحتاج في الإتيان بتلك الأعمال إلى الرؤية في نقش نقش ، ونقر نقر ،
The questioner answered concerning it: Indeed, many of the physicians and philosophers say that nature is wise [and that it is] perfect in wisdom, power, and knowledge. If this is a statement said by some people, what is the proof of its falsehood? We concede: that it is not described with knowledge and power, but why is it not permissible that precise and perfect actions proceed from it? And it indicates that what we mentioned is subject to two aspects: The first: that craft actions, when they become ingrained faculties in the soul and reach the limit of perfection and completion, then the intelligent, when they want to describe them as perfect and complete, liken them to nature, saying: This work has become natural for him, and this craft has become nature for him. And were it not established in their minds that the actions of nature are more perfect, stronger, and more complete, they would not liken craftsmanship to nature when attempting to describe it [as perfect and complete]. The second: that he who does not know the craft of writing or the craft of playing the drum, as long as he needs, in performing those actions, to look at each engraving and each strike,
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage records the objector's rejoinder to the earlier refutation, and then presents the author's two proofs showing that the objection is invalid. The objector first appeals to the authority of many physicians and philosophers who claim that nature is wise, perfect in wisdom, power, and knowledge. He challenges: even if we grant that nature is not described with knowledge and power, why cannot precise actions still proceed from it? The author then gives two proofs (the text is cut off during the second). The first proof: when human crafts become perfected and habitual, people describe them by likening them to nature, saying 'this work has become natural for him.' This shows that the actions of nature are considered more perfect and complete than crafts; otherwise, they would not use nature as the standard of perfection. Thus, if nature were blind and without knowledge, it would not be the benchmark for the highest perfection. The second proof (incomplete in this window) begins an analogy: a person learning a craft (like writing or drumming) initially needs to consciously direct each action, implying that perfected actions become natural only after knowledge and practice—further indicating that nature's actions are not blind but are accompanied by wisdom. The incomplete ending leaves the full second argument unfinished, but the direction is clear.

الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة

The First Proof: Perfected Crafts Are Likened to Nature as a Mark of Superiority
Author's first proof from the likening of crafts to nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's first proof that nature's actions are perceived as more perfect than human crafts, implying that nature must be wise.

Core Idea
People only liken perfected crafts to nature because they consider nature's actions more perfect.

The author now gives his first proof. He observes that when human crafts become perfected and habitual, people describe them by saying 'this work has become natural for him.' This shows that nature's actions are the standard of perfection. If nature were blind, it would not be the benchmark for the highest perfection. Hence, nature must possess wisdom.

3
Draw the conclusion
The author argues: were it not established that nature's actions are more perfect, people would not use nature as a comparison. Therefore, nature's actions are the highest, implying wisdom.
This proof relies on common linguistic practice.

Refutation of Attributing Creation to Nature
Objection and answer: why not attribute actions to nature?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing creation to nature is rationally invalid and how the author defends the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
Nature is not an independent creator; rejecting self-evident truths is false.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: why can't we attribute the design of the human body to nature, since people often attribute good actions to nature? The response is direct: such an appeal ignores clear reasoning (badīhīyāt) and relies on mere words. The author also clarifies that accepting the Creator as the agent of all creation is exactly what we affirm, while deferring the discussion of moral categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim that nature could be the agent of creation because people commonly speak of nature's role in good actions.
2
Analyze the author's reply
Notice how the author dismisses the appeal to common speech as rejecting axiomatic truths. What are these axiomatic truths? (The necessity of a designer for design.)
3
Examine the second objection and its resolution
The objector raises a consequence: if not nature, then all animals must be created by God. The author accepts this and postpones the discussion of good and evil.

The author postpones the detailed discussion of good and evil (presumably related to moral attributes) to later.

Quranic Creation Verse and Refutation of Nature Attribution
Author explains Quranic creation verse and answers two objections about attributing creation to nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author interprets a Quranic verse about creation and refutes two objections that attribute creation to nature, defending the necessity of divine causation.

Core Idea
Attributing creation to unconscious nature denies self-evident truths and is therefore false.

This passage from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah engages with a key Quranic verse (77:20-24) to demonstrate that the intricate process of fetal development can only be the result of divine wisdom and power. The author then anticipates and refutes two objections: one appealing to common speech attributing good actions to nature, and another concerning the implications of attributing creation to God. By the end, the doctrine of exclusive divine causation is firmly established.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse and interpretation
Read the verse (77:20-24) and the author's explanation of the phrase 'to a known term'. Notice how he highlights the divine management that retains the sperm in the womb for a precise period.
The author emphasizes that the word 'term' indicates a measured duration for fetal completion.
2
Understand the argument for divine wisdom
The author explains that the fetus's development, from retention to expulsion, is perfectly timed and requires conscious planning. This points to a wise Creator, not blind nature.
3
Examine the first objection and refutation
Objection: 'Why not attribute this to nature, since people say good things come from nature?' Refutation: This would require denying self-evident truths just to follow the words of some Arabs, which is false.
The author calls it a daf' al-badīhiyyāt (repelling self-evident truths).
4
Examine the second objection and refutation
Objection: 'If creation is from God, then all animals' creation must be from Him.' Refutation: The author accepts this, but postpones discussion of good and evil.
The author agrees with the implication, showing consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجرى إخفاء قرص الشمس يكف من التراب . قال تعالى في الكتاب الإلهي : ألم نخلقكم من ماء مهين ؟ فجعلناه في قرار مكين ، إلى قدر معلوم ، فقدرنا فنعم القادرون ، ويل يومئذ للمكذبين ) فقوله : ( فجعلناه في قرار مكين، إلى قدر معلوم ، معناه : أن طبيعة المني تقتضي النقل والزوال ، إلا إذا دبر خلقه في الرحم تدبيراً عجيباً يقتضي استمساك تلك القطرة في الحريم، إنا دبرنا خلقة ذلك العضو بحيث يقتضي استمساكها إلى حد معين ، وزمان معلوم ، وهو المدة التي يحتاج الجنين فيها إلى الإستكمال والتمام، فإذا تم البدن وكملت الخلقة زال ذلك الاستمساك ، وبطل ذلك الحفظ ، وانقلبت الطبيعة الحافظة الممسكة إلى مرسلة مخرجة ، وعند ذلك ينفصل الولد عن الرحم فقوله : « إلى قدر معلوم ، إشارة إلى أن بقاء ذلك الجسم في الموضع المعلوم ، ممتداً إلى مقدار الحاجة ، فإذا انقضى زمان الحاجة ، لم يبق الاستقرار في ذلك الموضع ، ثم قال : ( ويل يومئذ للمكذبين ) يعني من عرف أن هذه أفعال واقعة على وجه الحكمة ، والرحمة ، وإفاضة النعمة . ثم إن إنساناً لو أضافها إلى طبيعة لا شعور لها ولا إدراك ، ولا قدرة على فعل من الأفعال ، كان ذلك مكذباً بالعلوم البديهية ، و جاحداً للمعارف اليقينية، فيكون مستحقاً للويل والبلاء وأما قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يضاف ذلك إلى الطبيعة ، بسبب أن الناس يضيفون الأفعال الحسنة إلى الطبيعة ) ؟ قلنا : دفع البديهيات لأجل التمسك بكلمات يذكرها بعض أهل العرب باطل . قوله : « يلزم إضافة تخليق جميع الحيوانات إلى خالق العالم، قلنا : وهكذا نقول : وأما ما يتعلق بالحسن والقبح فالكلام فيه سيأتي بالاستقصاء .
The course of concealing the disk of the sun suffices from dust. He (Glorious) said in the Divine Book: 'Did we not create you from despicable water? Then We placed it in a secure resting place for a known term. So We determined, and excellent are the Determiners. Woe that Day to the deniers.' So His saying: 'Then We placed it in a secure resting place for a known term' means that the nature of sperm necessitates movement and passing away, unless its creation in the womb is managed with a wondrous management that necessitates the retention of that drop in the womb. Indeed, we managed the creation of that organ such that it necessitates its retention until a specific limit and a known time, which is the period the fetus needs for completion and perfection. When the body is completed and the creation is perfected, that retention ceases, that preservation is invalidated, and the preserving, retaining nature turns into a releasing, expelling nature, and at that point the child separates from the womb. So His saying: 'for a known term' indicates that the staying of that body in that known place extends to the amount of need. When the time of need expires, stability in that place no longer remains. Then He said: 'Woe that Day to the deniers' meaning one who knows that these are actions occurring with wisdom, mercy, and bestowal of blessing. Then if a person attributes them to a nature that has no consciousness, no perception, and no ability to perform any action, that would be denier of self-evident sciences and a rejecter of certain knowledge, thus deserving woe and affliction. As for his saying: 'Why is it not permissible to attribute that to nature because people attribute good actions to nature?' We say: Repelling self-evident truths for the sake of clinging to words uttered by some of the Arabs is false. His saying: 'It would necessitate attributing the creation of all animals to the Creator of the world.' We say: We say so as well. As for what relates to good and evil, the discussion about it will come later in detail.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a Quranic verse followed by a refutation of two objections. The author begins by interpreting Q. 77:20-24, describing the stages of fetal development from sperm to birth, emphasizing divine wisdom and providence. The words 'to a known term' indicate that the retention of the fetus is precisely for the needed time. The verse's threat of woe for deniers is explained as referring to those who, knowing the evident wisdom, still attribute these actions to an unconscious nature. Then the first objection is presented: why not attribute this to nature, since people often attribute good actions to nature? The author answers that this would require denying self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt) just to cling to the words of some Arabs—a false move. The second objection argues that if creation is attributed to God, then all animals' creation must be attributed to Him, which the author accepts, deferring the discussion of good and evil to a later point.

Correction: The author shows that such attribution is a denial of self-evident truths about causation and wisdom, and is therefore false and deserving of woe.

Refuting the Celestial Bodies as Direct Creators Objection
Author refutes the objection that celestial spheres/planets directly create human embryos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that celestial spheres create human bodies does not weaken the proof for a divine Creator.

Core Idea
The differentiation of celestial bodies itself points to a Wise Creator.

Does the possibility that celestial spheres create human bodies undermine the argument for God's existence? In this passage, the author acknowledges the possibility but shows how it actually reinforces the need for a Creator. Let's see how he turns the objection on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
The author begins by stating the objection directly.
2
Identify the key premise
The author says bodies are similar. Why is this crucial? Think about what it means for the argument.
If bodies are similar, then differences cannot come from themselves.
3
See how the objection is deflected
The author concludes that the specific qualities of spheres must come from God. Therefore, the objection does not harm the overall proof.
This is a powerful rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون المباشر لتخليق هذه الأبدان هو الأفلاك والكواكب ؟ قلنا : لا شك أن هذا الاحتمال قائم ، لكنا لما بينا أن الأجسام متماثلة ، فحينئذ يظهر لنا : أن اختصاص كل فلك ، وكل كوكب بصفته المعينة وخاصيته المعينة إنما كان بتخليق الإله الحكيم ، وعلى هذا التقدير فلا يضرنا هذا الكلام .
His saying: 'Why is it not permissible that the direct agent of the creation of these bodies be the celestial spheres and planets?' We say: There is no doubt that this possibility exists. However, since we have shown that bodies are similar, then it becomes clear to us that the particularization of each sphere and each planet with its specific quality and specific property is only due to the creation of the Wise God. On this basis, this speech does not harm us.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector proposes that celestial spheres or planets could be the direct creators of human bodies. The author acknowledges the logical possibility but responds by appealing to a prior proof: bodies are similar in essence (the author has already established that all bodies are similar in terms of being bodies). Therefore, the specific differentiation of each sphere and planet—their particular qualities and properties—cannot be explained by the bodies themselves but must come from an external, wise Creator. Since the objection's basis (direct creation by spheres) would require explaining these specificities without God, it fails; the argument for divine creation remains unaffected.

Key Terms
أفلاك

صفة معينة
خاصية معينة
إله حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That celestial spheres or planets could directly create human bodies without a creator.
Correction: The specific properties of spheres require a wise Creator, so they cannot be independent creators.

Checkpoint

B) Because bodies are similar and the specific properties of spheres must come from a wise Creator.
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies it.
D) Because spheres are too far away to affect creation.

Key Takeaway
The specific qualities of celestial bodies point to their dependence on a Creator, not to their independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
