Refutation of Intellect or Soul as Creator
Author refutes the objection that an intellect or soul could be the creator and concludes with the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any proposed creator other than God must ultimately lead to the Necessary Being, and how the Quranic verse 'To your Lord is the ultimate end' encapsulates this argument.

Core Idea
Every proposed agent either depends on a cause or is the Necessary Being itself.

In this passage, the author tackles a plausible objection: perhaps an intellect or soul could be the creator of the universe. He shows that this possibility actually reinforces the argument for God's existence. By analyzing whether that actor is possible or necessary, he demonstrates that any chain of causation must terminate in a Necessary Being. This leads to a powerful Quranic conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection starting with 'As for his saying' and the author's initial acknowledgment. Notice that the author concedes the possibility.
2
Follow the two possibilities
The author splits the analysis: the proposed intellect or soul is either a possible being (mumkin al-wujūd) or a necessary being. Trace each branch.
If possible, it needs a cause; if necessary, it is the intended goal.
3
Understand why regress is false
The author states that circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل) are invalid. Reflect on why this forces the chain to end.
Both logical impossibilities are rejected in kalam.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read the citation of Quran 53:42 and the author's reflection. See how it serves as the ultimate conclusion.
The verse 'To your Lord is the ultimate end' perfectly summarizes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون فاعل هذه التركيبات عقل ونفس : ؟ قلنا : هذا الاحتمال قائم . إلا أنا نقول : ذلك العقل أو النفس ، إن كان ممكن الوجود افتقر إلى السبب، والدور والتسلسل باطلان ، فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى واجب الوجود لذاته وإن كان ذلك الشيء واجب الوجود فهو المقصود . واعلم أن الحرف المعدات للحاجات والضرورات ، لا تندفع إلا بالانتهاء ، إلى واجب الوجود لذاته ، فلهذا السبب جاء في الكتاب الإلهي قوله تعالى : ( وأن إلى ربك المنتهى ) ومن تأمل على الوجه الحقيقي عرف أن هذه الكلمة ينبوع الخيرات ومركز المصالح والساعادات وبالله التوفيق
As for his saying: 'Why is it not permissible that the agent of these compositions be an intellect and a soul?' We say: This possibility exists. However, we say: That intellect or soul, if it is a possible being, is in need of a cause, and circularity and infinite regress are false, so there must be an end to the Necessary Being in itself. And if that thing is the Necessary Being, then it is the intended. And know that all preparative measures for needs and necessities are not repelled except by ending at the Necessary Being in itself. For this reason, in the Divine Book He says: 'And that to your Lord is the ultimate end.' And whoever reflects in a true manner knows that this word is the fountain of good things and the center of benefits and happiness. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a concluding reflection. The objector suggests that an intellect or soul could be the agent behind the complex compositions of bodies. The author accepts the possibility but analyzes it: such an intellect or soul is either a possible being (mumkin al-wujūd) or a necessary being. If it is possible, it requires a cause, and since circular reasoning and infinite regress are invalid, the chain must terminate in a Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd). If it is itself the Necessary Being, then it is exactly what the author is arguing for—God. Thus, any posited agent ultimately leads to the Necessary Being. The author then generalizes: every means for fulfilling needs and necessities cannot succeed without ending at the Necessary Being, citing Quran 53:42: 'And that to your Lord is the ultimate end.' The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms

نفس
ممكن الوجود
واجب الوجود
دور
تسلسل
المنتهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an intellect or soul can be an independent creator without needing God.
Correction: The author shows that such an agent either depends on a cause (leading to God) or is itself the Necessary Being (God).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the outcome if the proposed agent (intellect or soul) is a possible being?
A) It can exist without a cause.
B) It must be a celestial sphere.
C) It needs a cause, leading to the Necessary Being.
D) Circularity and infinite regress are acceptable.

Key Takeaway
Any proposed creator must either be dependent (requiring a Necessary Being) or be the Necessary Being itself, proving that God is the ultimate end of all causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a task using your intellect or effort, do you pause to thank the Necessary Being who made that success possible? Or do you quietly attribute it to your own abilities? What would it look like to consciously direct every achievement back to Him as the ultimate end?
?
You rely on countless 'preparative measures' daily—planning, studying, working, healing. Which of these have you been trusting as if they are self-sufficient? How would your mindset change if you saw each one as a need that is only 'repelled' by ending at the Necessary Being?
?
The verse 'And that to your Lord is the ultimate end' exposes whether your heart truly longs for God or stops at intermediate goods. What is one good thing—a relationship, a career, a worldly goal—that you are currently treating as an end in itself? Will you demote it to a means and elevate Him alone as your ultimate end starting today?

Al-Razi's Alternative Proof for Infinite Events
Al-Razi presents an alternative proof based on causality to establish infinite events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's alternative proof for the existence of an infinite series of events based on causality and the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
If an eternal cause produces a temporal effect, there must be an infinite series of causes.

Al-Razi presents a fascinating argument that builds on philosophical principles to show that events must have an infinite chain of causes. This proof relies on the impossibility of a cause producing an effect without a distinguishing condition. Let's explore the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Try to grasp the overall flow of the argument before diving into details.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'قديم' (eternal) and 'حادث' (temporal).
2
Identify the starting point and the two possibilities for the cause
Al-Razi starts from the existence of motions/events. He then states that the cause of any event is either eternal or temporal. Focus on these two branches.
This is a classic dilemma structure in kalam.
3
Analyze the eternal cause branch
If the cause is eternal, consider whether its effect depends on a condition. If not, we get a contradiction because the effect would have existed eternally. If it does depend on a condition, the cause leads to a temporal condition.
The key point is the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.
4
Trace the regress to an infinite series
The temporal condition itself must have a cause, leading to an infinite regress. Al-Razi then considers two forms of regress – simultaneous or sequential – and concludes that the sequential regress without a beginning is the philosophers' view.
This is the conclusion: events without a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المصنف رحمة الله عليه : وعندي أنه يمكن تقريره بطريق آخر بناء على أصول الفلاسفة ، فيقال : لا شك أن ههنا حركات وتغييرات ، فهذه الحوادث لا بد لها من سبب ، وسبب هذا الحادث إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً فإن كان قديماً فإما أن يتوقف إلحاقه لهذا الحادث على شرط ، أو لا يتوقف فإن لم يتوقف فحينئذ قد كان هذا القديم موجوداً من الأزل إلى الآن من غير صدور هذا الحادث عنه ثم صدر هذا الحادث من غير أن يتميز هذا الوقت عن سائر الأوقات ، بما لأجله صار أولى بحدوث هذا الحادث، وهذا يقتضي رجحان الممكن لا لمرجح وهو محال ، فثبت أن بتقدير أن يكون السبب المؤثر في حدوث هذا الحادث موجوداً قديماً ، إلا أنه يجب الاعتراف بأن تأثيره في وجود هذا الحادث مشروط بشرط حادث ، فحينئذ يرجح الأمر إلى القسم الثاني وهو أن الأثر الحادث لابد له من سبب حادث . ثم الكلام في ذلك الحادث كالكلام في الأول فيفضي هذا إلى التسلسل وإما أن يحصل التسلسل في أسباب ومسببات توجد دفعة واحدة وهو محال ، وإما أن لا يحصل بحيث يكون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بآخر لا إلى أول ، وذلك هو الذي تقول به الفلاسفة ، ويذهبون إليه . وعند ظهور هذا المعنى قد ثبت لهم وجود حوادث لا أول لها ، ووجود حركات لا بداية لها .
The author—may God have mercy on him—said: In my view, it can be established by another method based on the principles of the philosophers. It is said: There is no doubt that there are here motions and changes. These events must have a cause. The cause of this event is either eternal or temporal. If it is eternal, then either its bringing about this event depends on a condition or it does not. If it does not depend, then this eternal would have existed from eternity until now without this event emanating from it, then this event emanates without this time being distinguished from other times by a reason for which it became more appropriate for the occurrence of this event. This necessitates the preponderance of a possible without a preponderator, which is impossible. So it is established that if the effective cause of this event is eternal, it must be acknowledged that its effect in the existence of this event is conditional upon a temporal condition. Thus the matter reverts to the second division, which is that the temporal effect must have a temporal cause. Then the discussion about that temporal cause is like the discussion about the first, leading to an infinite regress. Either the infinite regress occurs in causes and effects that exist at once, which is impossible, or it does not occur such that each of them is preceded by another without end, and that is what the philosophers hold and go toward. When this meaning becomes evident, it is established for them that there are events without a beginning and motions without a start.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage contains an alternative proof presented by the author based on the principles of the philosophers. It is a causal argument for the existence of an infinite series of events. The author begins by stating that there are motions and changes (events). These events must have a cause. The cause is either eternal or temporal. If it is eternal, then either its effect depends on a condition or not. If not, then the eternal cause would have existed from eternity without producing this event, and then the event occurs without any distinguishing condition, implying preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible. Therefore, if the cause is eternal, its effect must depend on a temporal condition. Then the discussion turns to that temporal condition, leading to a regress. The author then presents two possibilities: either the series ends at a first cause or it is infinite. The philosophers accept an infinite series where each cause is preceded by another without end. The conclusion is that there are events without a beginning and motions without a start.

Correction: The proof is presented as a method of argumentation based on the philosophers' own principles, not necessarily a position the author holds.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's proof shows that if an eternal cause produces a temporal effect, it must be conditioned by a temporal condition, leading to an infinite regress of causes.

?
This proof shows that if an eternal cause produces a temporal effect without a condition, it leads to absurdity. In your relationship with God, do you sometimes expect Him to act without wisdom or condition—demanding answers or immediate results without trusting His timing? What condition might God be waiting for in your heart before He brings about a change you desire?

The Second Premise: All Events Require an External Agent
Al-Razi states the second premise that events require an agent, based on impossibility of self-motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that events (changes) cannot be caused by the body itself and must have an external agent.

Core Idea
No body moves itself; every event points to an external mover.

This lesson presents the second premise used by philosophers to argue for the existence of a creator. They claim that since bodies cannot cause their own motion, every event or change must be produced by an external agent. Al-Razi explains this premise and grounds it in a principle from natural science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Razi introduces the premise and its basis.
The premise is introduced as 'their statement' — see how it is justified.
2
Identify the key claim and its basis
The premise is that events must have an effect, agent, and maker. The basis is that a body cannot move by itself. Identify these two parts in the text.
The author references natural science for the detailed proof.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Understand how this premise fits into the philosophers' overall proof for a creator: once established that events need an external agent, that agent is the necessary being.
This premise is one of several leading to the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولهم : هذه الحوادث لابد لها من مؤثر وفاعل وموجد فتقرير هذه المقدمة مبني على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يتحرك لنفسه أو لذاته ، والكلام في هذه المسألة مذكور في العلم الطبيعي على سبيل الاستقصاء، وإذا ثبت أنه يمتنع كونها متحركة لذواتها وجب القول بأنه لا بد لها من محرك يحركها ومؤثر يؤثر في وجود حركاتها .
As for the second premise: it is their statement: 'These events must have an effect, an agent, and a maker.' The establishment of this premise is based on the fact that a body cannot move by itself or by its essence. The discussion of this issue is mentioned in natural science extensively. When it is established that it is impossible for them to move by themselves, it must be said that they must have a mover that moves them and an agent that effects the existence of their motions.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise of the philosophers' argument, which is that events require an external agent. The justification for this premise is that a body cannot move by itself. The author notes that this is discussed in natural science. Therefore, since events are motions or changes in bodies, they must have an external mover and agent.

Third Premise: Finite Corporeal Power Cannot Produce Infinite Actions
Al-Razi proves that a corporeal power cannot perform infinite actions due to its divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that a divisible corporeal power must produce finite actions, and why all three possible scenarios for its parts lead to contradiction or finitude.

Core Idea
A divisible power cannot produce infinite actions.

Al-Razi now tackles a crucial premise: why a corporeal power—any power residing in a physical body—cannot produce an infinite number of actions. He builds his proof on the fact that such a power is divisible because its substrate is divisible. Then he systematically examines what a part of that power can do, and shows that every possibility leads either to logical contradiction or to the conclusion that the whole power's action is finite. This argument is foundational for later discussions about divine power versus finite created power.

Guided Path
1
Establish divisibility of corporeal power
Read the opening lines where Al-Razi states that every corporeal power inheres in a divisible substance, and therefore is itself divisible. Understand the link: no indivisible atom (jawhar fard) means every located substance is divisible.
This step uses the rejection of the indivisible atom, a key Kalām position.
2
Enumerate the three possibilities for a part of the power
Identify the three divisions: (1) part has no strength, (2) part has the same strength as the whole, (3) part has less strength than the whole. These exhaust all logical possibilities.
3
Refutation of the first two possibilities
Follow Al-Razi's refutation of case (1) leading to the whole having no strength (contradiction), and case (2) making part equal to whole (absurd).
Both are dismissed quickly as impossible.
4
Analysis of the third possibility
Study the third case in detail: if the part has less strength, then its action is finite (since it can only do a finite amount). The whole's action is a finite multiple of this finite action, hence also finite.
This yields the desired conclusion: the power's action must be finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة : فهي بيان أن القوة الجسمانية لا تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ، وتقرير هذه المقدمة : أن كل قوة جسمانية فهي حالة في محل متحيز . وكل متحيز منقسم ، بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد ، وكل ما كان حالا في محل منقسم . فهو منقسم ، وكل قوة جسمانية ، فهي منقسمة . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول جزء تلك القوة إما أن لا يقوى على شيء أصلا ، أو يقوى عليه الكل ، أو يقوى على شيء انقص مما يقوى عليه الكل ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة. وإنما قلنا : إنه يستحيل أن يقال : إن جزء القوة لا يقوى على شيء اصلاً لأن تلك القوة ليس إلا تلك الأجزاء ولا يقوى على شيء أصلاح فوجب أن يقال : إن مجموع تلك القوة لا تقوى على شيء أصلا ، فيلزم أن يقال : إن القوة على الشيء ، ليست قوة على الشيء . هذا خلف وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إن جزء القوة يقوي على تمام ما تقوى عليه كل القوة فهذا أيضاً محال لأنه يلزم أن يكون الكل مثل الجزء من غير تفاوت أصلاً ، وذلك محال . وأما الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إن جزء القوة يقوى على بعض ما يقوى عليه الكل . فنقول : إذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فإذا فرضنا أن كل تلك القوة وجزءها أخذا في التحريك من مبدأ معين ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يتناهي فعل الجزء ، وإذا كان كذلك ففعل الكل يكون أضعاف فعل الجزء بمرات متناهية ، وأضعاف المتناهي متناهي ، فوجب أن يكون فعل القوة متناهياً فثبت أن القوى الجسمانية يجب أن تكون أفعالها متناهية ، ويمتنع صدور
As for the third premise: it is the demonstration that a corporeal power cannot have the strength for infinite actions. The establishment of this premise: every corporeal power is an accident in a located substance. Every located substance is divisible, based on the negation of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). And everything that is an accident in a divisible substance is divisible. So every corporeal power is divisible. Once this is established, we say: the part of that power either has no strength for anything at all, or it has strength for the whole, or it has strength for something less than what the whole has strength for. And the three divisions are invalid. We say: It is impossible to say that the part of the power has no strength for anything at all, because that power is nothing but those parts, and if each part had no strength, then the sum would have no strength, leading to the conclusion that the power which is supposed to have strength for something is not strength for something. This is a contradiction. As for the second division: that the part of the power has strength for exactly what the whole has strength for—this is also impossible, because it would entail that the whole is like the part with no difference at all, and that is impossible. As for the third: that the part of the power has strength for part of what the whole has strength for. We say: if that is the case, then if we assume that the entire power and its part both begin moving from a given starting point, then the part's action will be finite. If so, the whole's action will be multiples of the part's action by finite times, and multiples of a finite are finite, so the power's action must be finite. Thus it is established that the actions of corporeal powers must be finite, and it is impossible for them to produce
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage provides the third premise, showing that a corporeal power cannot produce infinite actions. The proof begins by noting that every corporeal power resides in a divisible substance (since indivisible atoms are rejected). Therefore the power itself is divisible. Then the author considers three possibilities for the part of that power: it has no strength, has the same strength as the whole, or has lesser strength. The first leads to the whole having no strength, contradiction. The second makes the whole equal to the part, impossible. The third leads to the whole's action being a finite multiple of the part's finite action, so the whole's action is finite. Hence all possibilities result in finite actions, proving that corporeal power cannot produce infinite actions.

محل متحيز
منقسم
جوهر فرد
فعل
متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument assumes infinite power is needed for infinite actions, but it rigorously shows that finite divisible power necessarily yields finite actions.
Correction: The argument does not assume that infinite power is required; it deduces that a divisible power cannot produce infinite actions regardless of the strength of its parts.

Checkpoint

A) The part's action is infinite.
B) The whole's action is a finite multiple of the part's action, hence finite.
C) The part's action is equal to the whole's action.
D) The whole's action is infinite.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument shows that any corporeal power, being divisible, can only produce finite actions.

Reconciling Independence with Inherence
First aspect of the answer, presenting two causal models reconciling independence with inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of existence being an attribute of essence even if existence is independent of essence, through two logical possibilities: existence causing its own inherence or essence necessitating inherence.

Core Idea
Independence does not prevent inherence; cause can be inherent in its effect.

The objection claims that if existence is independent of essence, it cannot be an attribute. The author begins his answer by exploring two possible ways existence could be independent yet still inhere in essence. This first aspect of the answer uses causal reasoning to show that independence and inherence are compatible. As you read, pay attention to the subtle logical moves and the analogy with form and matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if existence is independent (ghaniy) of essence, it would be self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi) and thus could not be an attribute of essence. This is the challenge the author must address.
The objector assumes that being an attribute requires dependence on the subject.
2
Grasp the first possibility
The author's first possibility: existence itself can be the cause ('illa) of its own inherence in the essence. Even though existence is independent in itself, it causes itself to inhere in the essence. Thus, independence does not prevent inherence.
This is a causal model where the cause (existence) brings about its own being an attribute.
3
Grasp the second possibility
The second possibility: the essence itself necessitates that existence inhere in it. Here the essence plays an active role, making existence an attribute despite existence's independence.
Both possibilities preserve the independence of existence while explaining its inherence.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The author draws an analogy with form and matter: form is the cause of matter's existence, yet form inheres in matter. This illustrates that independence and inherence can coexist. Consider how this analogy supports the argument.
This analogy is from Aristotelian physics; it is meant to show the logical possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : : لو كان غنيا عنها لكان قائما بنفسه ، ولم يكن صفة لتلك الماهية ، قلنا : وتقرير الكلام من وجهين : الأول : أن يقال : الوجود وإن كان غنيا في نفسه عن تلك الماهية ، إلا أنه علة لكونه حالا في تلك الماهية ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالوجود مع كونه غنيا عن تلك الماهية ، يكون حالا فيها . أو يقال : تلك الماهية توجب كون [ذلك] الوجود حالا في تلك الماهية ، فهذان الاحتمالان قائمان ، وكل واحد منهما لا يقدح في كون الوجود حالا في الماهية مع كون الوجود غنيا عن الماهية . وبهذين الطريقين جوزنا كون الصورة علة لوجود المادة ، مع كون الصورة حالة في المادة .
He said: 'If it were independent of it, it would be self-subsisting and would not be an attribute of that essence.' We said: The exposition of the speech is from two aspects. The first: that it is said: Existence, even if it is in itself independent of that essence, is nevertheless a cause for its being inherent in that essence. On this assumption, existence despite being independent of that essence is inherent in it. Or it is said: that essence necessitates that [that] existence be inherent in that essence. These two possibilities stand, and each of them does not impair the fact that existence is inherent in the essence despite existence being independent of the essence. By these two ways we allowed form to be a cause for the existence of matter, despite form being inherent in matter.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if existence (wujud) were independent of essence (mahiyya), it would be self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi) and could not be an attribute (sifa) of that essence. The author responds by saying the argument can be analyzed from two aspects, and here presents the first. He gives two possibilities: (1) Existence, though independent, can be the cause ('illa) for its own inherence (hulul) in the essence. (2) The essence itself can necessitate that existence inheres in it. Both possibilities show that independence does not preclude inherence. The author analogizes to the relationship between form (sura) and matter (madda) in Aristotelian physics: form is the cause of matter's existence yet inheres in matter.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
قائم بنفسه (qa'im bi nafsihi)
صفة (sifa)
علة ('illa)
حال (hal)
صورة (sura)
مادة (madda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is independent of another, it cannot be an attribute of that other.
Correction: The author shows that independence does not preclude inherence; something can be independent yet be an attribute either by causing its own inherence or by being necessitated by the subject.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first aspect of the answer?
A) Existence must depend on essence to be an attribute.
B) Existence can be independent yet still be an attribute of essence.
C) Existence and essence are identical.
D) The objection is rejected without argument.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that existence can be independent yet inherent either because it causes its own inherence or because essence necessitates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see your own existence—given by Allah—as independent of your essence, yet your actions inhere in your character? Reflect on a time when you wanted to influence someone but felt your independence required you to stand apart. How can you, like form in matter, be intimately present and effective without losing your own essence? Write down one person you will intentionally serve this week as both a cause and an inherent presence.

Second Answer: Existence Cannot Be Possible
The author argues that even if existence depends on essence, it cannot be described as possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence itself cannot be described as possible (mumkin), even if it depends on essence.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be possible because its possibility would need another existence for itself — an absurdity.

In this passage, the author tackles a tricky concession: what if existence depends on essence? Even then, he argues, existence cannot be called possible. The reason is that possibility requires an essence to alternate between existence and non-existence, but applying that to existence itself leads to an infinite regress or a contradiction. This is a subtle but crucial argument in the debate about the nature of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two main parts: a concession that existence is possible if dependent, and then a proof that it cannot be described as possible.
The proof starts with 'However, we say: Here there is a proof...'
2
Understand the definition of possible (mumkin)
The author defines possible as that whose reality can occur with existence at one time and with non-existence at another. Grasp this definition clearly.
Possibility involves alternation between existence and non-existence.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
The author applies this definition to existence itself, leading to two absurdities: two existences conjoined, or existence and non-existence together. Trace why each is impossible.
The argument shows that existence cannot be possible because it would require a second existence or a contradiction.

ههنا الوجه الثاني في الجواب : سلمنا أن بتقدير أنه يجب حصول ذلك الوجود في تلك الماهية ، فإنه يلزم كون الوجود مفتقرا إلى تلك الماهية ، ويلزم من كون الوجود مفتقرا إلى الماهية كون الوجود ممكنا، إلا أنا نقول : ههنا دليل يمنع كون الوجود موصوفا بالإمكان. وتقريره : وهو أن ممكن الوجود هو الذي لا يمتنع كون حقيقته حاصلة مع الوجود تارة ، ومع العدم أخرى . وهذا المعنى لا يعقل في حق الوجود ، لأنا لو قلنا : إن ماهية الوجود تارة تحصل مع الوجود ، وأخرى مع العدم، لزم كون الوجود على أحد التقديرين موصوفا بوجود آخر وعلى التقدير الثاني يكون موصوفا بالعدم . وكل ذلك محال أما الأول فلأنه يلزم اجتماع الوجودين للشيء الواحد . وأيضا : فلا يكن أحدهما بالموصوفية والآخر بكونه صفة للأول أولى من العكس . وأما الثاني فلانه يلزم الجمع بين الوجود وبين العدم، وهو محال . فيثبت : أن وصف الوجود بكونه ممكن الوجود والعدم محال . فيثبت : أن الدليل الذي ذكرتم ، وإن اقتضى كون الوجود موصوفا بالإمكان ، إلا أن الدليل الذي ذكرناه يمنع منه
Here is the second aspect of the answer: We concede that on the supposition that it is necessary for that existence to occur in that essence, it follows that existence is in need of that essence, and from existence being in need of essence it follows that existence is possible. However, we say: Here there is a proof that prevents existence from being described as possibility. Its exposition: That the possible of existence is that whose reality is not prevented from occurring with existence at one time and with non-existence at another. This meaning is not conceivable with respect to existence, because if we said that the quiddity of existence occurs with existence at one time and with non-existence at another, it would necessitate that existence on one of the two assumptions is described by another existence and on the second assumption is described by non-existence. All that is impossible. As for the first, because it necessitates the conjunction of two existences for one thing. Also, there is no reason why one of them is the subject and the other is the attribute more than the reverse. As for the second, because it necessitates the conjunction of existence and non-existence, which is impossible. Thus it is established that describing existence as possible of existence and non-existence is impossible. Thus it is established that the proof you mentioned, even if it implies that existence is described as possibility, the proof we have adduced prevents it.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is the second aspect of the answer to the objection. The author makes a concession: even if we grant that existence is dependent on essence and thus possible (mumkin), he provides a proof that existence cannot be described as possible at all. The reason is that possibility requires that the same essence can be with existence at one time and with non-existence at another. But if we apply this to existence itself, its quiddity would need to be accompanied by another existence or by non-existence. This is impossible: two existences cannot coexist for one thing, and existence cannot be conjoined with non-existence. Therefore, the very notion of describing existence as possible or impossible (as having the possibility of existence and non-existence) is incoherent. This refutes the opponent's assumption that dependence implies possibility.

Key Terms
ممكن (mumkin)
الوجود (al-wujud)
العدم (al-'adam)
اجتماع الوجودين (ijtima' al-wujudayn)
ماهية الوجود (mahiyyat al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence depends on essence, it must be possible (mumkin).
Correction: Even if dependence implies possibility in general, existence itself cannot be described as possible because the definition of possibility applied to existence leads to an absurdity (a second existence or a contradiction).

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that existence cannot be described as possible?
A) Because existence is necessary by definition.
B) Because describing existence as possible would require another existence or non-existence for existence itself, which is absurd.
C) Because the objection is based on a false premise.
D) Because possibility only applies to non-existent things.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be described as possible because applying the definition of possibility to existence itself results in an impossibility: either a second existence or a contradiction with non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that your own existence is contingent, depending on many things. But if existence itself cannot be described as possible, what does that say about the ultimate source of your existence? Are you resting your life on something that is merely possible, or on a necessary foundation? How does that change the way you face your own frailty?
?
The author argues that describing existence as possible leads to absurdity. Where in your life have you treated something as 'just possible'—your faith, your purpose, your relationship with God—when it actually requires a deeper certainty? What would it mean for you to stop treating that as optional?
?
If existence cannot be contingent, then the only true existence is necessary. Does your daily preoccupation with temporary things reveal that you are living as if they are necessary? Pause and ask: what are you actually depending on as if it cannot be taken away? How would your life change if you transferred that dependence to the Necessary Being alone?

Essence Itself Necessitates Existence
The author presents the main answer with two proofs that essence by itself is sufficient to necessitate existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a possible essence can necessitate existence by itself, without the essence being already existent.

Core Idea
Essence can cause existence without being existent itself.

The author now presents the definitive answer to the earlier objection. He argues that the essence of a possible being is sufficient to necessitate its existence, drawing an analogy with how essence necessitates possibility. This lesson unpacks his two proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage ends abruptly; the argument is essentially complete.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the author states the chosen view: the essence qua essence is sufficient for necessitating existence. He then provides two proofs (wujuh).
The first proof uses symmetry between receptivity and effectivity.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof argues that essence necessitates possibility by itself, prior to existence, showing that essence's necessitation of concomitants does not depend on existence.
This proof relies on the principle that what is by essence is prior to what is by another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الجواب (٢): وهو الجواب المعتمد أن نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: المقتضى لذلك الوجود هو الماهية؟ قوله: (اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود إما أن يكون مشروطا بكون تلك الماهية موجودة، وإما أن لا يكون مشروطا بذلك، قلنا: المختار أن الماهية من حيث هي هي، كافية في اقتضاء ذلك الوجود. والذي يدل على أن هذا الذي ذكرناه غير مستبعد، وجوه: الأول: لا نزاع أن ماهيات الممكنات مغايرة لوجوداتها، وأن تلك الماهيات قابلة لتلك الوجودات. فنقول: إن كان الشرط في كون تلك الماهيات قابلة لتلك الوجودات كونها موجودات في نفسها، الزمت المحالات التي ذكرتموها، وإن لم يكن كون الماهية قابلة للوجود مشروطا بكونها موجودة، بل الماهية من حيث إنها هي، تكون قابلة، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: المؤثر في ذلك الوجود هو تلك الماهية من حيث إنها هي؟ وبالجملة فكل ما تذكرونه في جانب القبول، لم لا يجوز مثله في جانب التأثير؟ الثاني: إن الماهية الممكنة مقتضية للإمكان لما هي هي، وأما الوجود فإنما يحصل من السبب المنفصل، وما بالذات قبل ما بالغير، فاقتضاء الماهية الممكنة للإمكان سابق على اتصافها بالوجود، فيثبت: أن اقتضاء الماهية للوازمها، سابق على اتصافها بالوجود، فيثبت: أن اقتضاء الماهية للوازمها لا يتوقف على كون تلك الماهية موجودة. وإذا عقل ذلك في الجملة، فلم لا يعقل مثله في
The second aspect of the answer (2): and it is the relied-upon answer: that we say: Why is it not allowed to say that the necessitator of that existence is the essence? He says: 'The necessitation of that essence for that existence is either conditioned by that essence being existent, or not conditioned by that.' We said: The chosen view is that the essence qua essence is sufficient for necessitating that existence. And what indicates that what we have mentioned is not far-fetched are several aspects: The first: There is no dispute that the essences of possibles are different from their existences, and that those essences are receptive of those existences. So we say: If the condition for those essences being receptive of those existences is that they are existent in themselves, it entails the impossibilities you mentioned; and if the essence's being receptive of existence is not conditioned by its being existent, rather the essence qua essence is receptive, then why is it not allowed to say that the efficient cause of that existence is that essence qua essence? In sum, everything you mention on the side of receptivity, why is the like not allowed on the side of effectivity? The second: The possible essence necessitatates possibility qua itself; as for existence, it is only obtained from a separate cause, and what is by essence is prior to what is by another. So the possible essence's necessitation of possibility is prior to its being qualified by existence. Thus it is established that the essence's necessitation of its concomitants is prior to its being qualified by existence. Thus it is established that the essence's necessitation of its concomitants does not depend on that essence being existent. And if that is intellectually conceivable in general, then why is the like not conceivable in
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is the relied-upon answer (al-jawab al-mu'tamad) to the original objection. The author presents the view that the essence itself is sufficient to necessitate existence, without requiring the essence to be already existent. The opponent had raised the question: is the essence's necessitation conditioned by the essence being existent? The author chooses the unconditioned view. He then provides two proofs (wujuh):

1. It is agreed that essences of possibles are distinct from their existences and are receptive of existence. If receptivity required existence, it would lead to the impossibilities the opponent mentioned. But if receptivity is not conditioned by existence, then the essence qua essence can be the efficient cause (mu'aththir) of existence. The opponent's logic on the side of receptivity should be symmetrical on the side of effectivity.

2. A possible essence necessitates possibility by itself, prior to existence. Existence comes from a separate cause (sabab munfasil). Since what is by essence (bi al-dhat) is prior to what is by another (bi al-ghayr), the essence's necessitation of its concomitants (lawazim) does not depend on its existence. If this is conceivable generally, it should be conceivable here.

The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is essentially complete.

المقتضى
الماهية
قبول
تأثير
سبب منفصل
ما بالذات
ما بالغير
لوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An essence must exist before it can be a cause of existence.
Correction: The author shows that essence's causal role is not conditional on its existence, as demonstrated by its necessitation of possibility prior to existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the relied-upon answer?
A) Essence must exist before it can necessitate existence.
B) Essence qua essence is sufficient to necessitate existence without being existent.
C) Existence is identical to essence.
D) The objection is conceded.

Key Takeaway
The essence of a possible being, considered in itself, is the necessitator of its existence without requiring its own prior existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have just read that an essence can necessitate existence without being existent itself. Does your faith in Allah as the Necessary Being rest on a similar understanding that His Essence is the ground of all existence, not dependent on any external cause? How does this reshape your reliance on secondary causes in your daily life?
?
The author argues that what is by essence is prior to what is by another. In your own life, what matters more: what you are in your essence (your inner character) or what you acquire from external circumstances? Are you living from your essence or from your circumstances? What one change can you make today to align your actions with your essence as a believer?
?
The proof uses the idea that essence necessitates its concomitants without needing existence. What does that imply about your own identity? Could it be that your true self (your fitrah) already contains what is necessary for your ultimate happiness, even before you act? What would it look like to trust that essence rather than constantly seeking validation from external achievements?

Third Proof: Unconditioned Essence and the Non-Existent Cause Objection
Sanusi presents the third proof and responds to an objection about non-existent cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third proof demonstrates that the essence's requirement for existence is not conditioned by its own existence, and how the objection of a non-existent cause is refuted by distinguishing the essence in itself from non-existence.

Core Idea
The essence in itself is neutral to existence; its causality does not require it to be existent.

This passage presents the third proof in a series, using the notion of receptivity as an extrinsic property. The author argues that since the essence's receptivity precedes existence, the essence can be a cause without being existent. An objection about an impossible non-existent cause is refuted by noting that the essence considered in itself is not a non-existent thing. Let's follow the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow: proof, objection, answer.
The passage uses technical terms like 'qabiliyya' (receptivity) and 'nisba' (relation).
2
Identify the core proof
The proof has several sub-steps: (1) existing essence is receptive, (2) receptivity precedes existence, (3) receptivity is an extrinsic relation, (4) therefore the essence's effecting is unconditioned by existence. The author then extends this to the essence's requirement for existence itself.
The key move is that the posterior cannot condition the prior.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection claims that an unconditioned essence would imply a non-existent cause, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between the essence in itself (which is not described as existent or non-existent) and a truly non-existent entity. Since the essence in itself is neutral, it does not violate the impossibility of a non-existent cause.
This distinction is crucial: 'al-mahiyya min haythu hiya hiya' is the essence in abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الماهية الموجودة قابلة للوجود . وقابلية الوجود سابقة على حصول الوجود . ثم نقول : قابلية الوجود صفة خارجة عن الماهية، لأن قابلية الوجود نسبة مخصوصة بين الماهية وبين الوجود، والنسبة بين الأمرين مغايرة لذات كل واحد منهما، فقابلية الماهية للوجود صفة خارجة عن الماهية لازمة لها، فتكون معلولة الماهية لقابلية الوجود لا تكون مشروطة بالوجود، لأن الوجود متأخر بالرتبة عن قابلية الوجود، والمتأخر لا يكون شرطا للمتقدم . فيثبت : أن اقتضاء الماهية لهذا الحكم، غير مشروط بالوجود، وإذا كان كذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن اقتضاء الماهية للوجود لا يكون مشروطا بوجود آخر ؟ وحينئذ يندفع ما ذكرتموه . • فإن قالوا : فالماهية إذا لم يكن اقتضاؤها للوجود، مشروطا بكونها موجودة . فحينئذ يجوز كونها مقتضية للموجود حال عدمها . فحينئذ يلزم كون المعدوم علة للموجود، وذلك محال . فنقول : لا شك أن قبول الماهية للوجود غير مشروط بكونها موجودة، ثم مع هذا لا يلزم أن يقال : المعدوم موصوف بالوجود . وإذا لم يلزم هذا في جانب القبول، فكذلك مثله في جانب التأثير وتحقيق القول فيه : أن الماهية من حيث إنها هي، مغايرة لكونها موجودة أو معدومة . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فقد سقطت هذه الحجة التي عولوا عليها من جميع الوجوه .
The third [aspect]: The existing essence is receptive to existence. Receptivity to existence precedes the occurrence of existence. Then we say: Receptivity to existence is a property extrinsic to the essence, because receptivity to existence is a specific relation between the essence and existence, and the relation between two things is distinct from the essence of each one of them. So the receptivity of the essence to existence is a property extrinsic to the essence, necessary to it. Therefore, the essence's effecting of receptivity to existence is not conditioned by existence, because existence is posterior in rank to receptivity to existence, and the posterior cannot be a condition for the prior. Thus it is established that the requirement of the essence for this judgment is not conditioned by existence. And if this is so, then why is it not permissible to say that the requirement of the essence for existence is not conditioned by another existence? And by that, what you mentioned is repelled. Then if they say: If the essence's requirement for existence is not conditioned by its being existent, then it would be permissible for it to be a requirement for an existent while it is non-existent, which would entail that a non-existent is a cause of an existent, and that is impossible. We say: There is no doubt that the acceptance of the essence for existence is not conditioned by its being existent, yet despite this, it does not entail that the non-existent is described by existence. And if this is not entailed on the side of acceptance, then likewise its like on the side of effectuation. And the precise statement concerning it: The essence in itself is distinct from its being existent or non-existent. And since the matter is so, this argument that they relied upon falls from all aspects.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage contains the third proof and a response to an objection. The proof: The existing essence is receptive to existence, and this receptivity precedes existence. Receptivity is a relation extrinsic to the essence. Since the essence causes this receptivity, and receptivity precedes existence in rank, the essence's effecting of receptivity is not conditioned by existence. Therefore, the essence's requirement for existence itself can also be unconditioned by existence. The objection: If the essence can require existence without being existent, then a non-existent would be the cause of an existent, which is impossible. The answer: Receptivity is not conditioned by existence, yet we do not say that the non-existent is described by existence. Likewise for effectuation. The key point is that the essence in itself is neutral to existence and non-existence; it is not 'non-existent' as a substance, but rather an essence considered in abstraction. Thus the objection fails because it conflates the essence considered in itself with a non-existent entity. The author concludes that the opponents' argument collapses.

Key Terms
القابلية (al-qabiliyya): receptivity
الصفة الخارجة (al-sifa al-kharija): extrinsic property
النسبة (al-nisba): relation
المعلول (al-ma'lul): effect
الرتبة (al-rutba): rank
المتقدم (al-mutaqaddim): prior
المتأخر (al-muta'akhkhir): posterior
الماهية من حيث هي هي (al-mahiyya min haythu hiya hiya): essence in itself

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if the essence can cause existence without being existent, then a non-existent (absolute nothing) would be a cause, which is impossible.
Correction: The essence in itself (al-mahiyya min haythu hiya hiya) is not a non-existent entity; it is an abstract consideration distinct from existence and non-existence. Hence causing does not require the cause to be a concrete existent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the third proof does not lead to the impossibility of a non-existent cause?
A) Because the essence in itself is distinct from being existent or non-existent, and the cause is considered in that neutral sense.
B) Because the essence must exist before causing existence.
C) Because receptivity is not a real property.
D) Because existence is prior to receptivity.

Key Takeaway
The essence's requirement for existence is not conditioned by its own existence, because the essence in itself is distinct from both existence and non-existence, thus avoiding the absurdity of a non-existent cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that your essence is distinct from your existence — your core identity does not depend on whether you are present or absent. What does that mean for how you view your worth before Allah? Do you tie your value to your achievements, your existence, or your state? Write down one lie you have believed about your identity that this truth overturns.
?
The author argues that the essence in itself is neutral to existence. If your essence is not defined by any worldly state, then no sin, failure, or loss can alter who you are in Allah's sight. Are there circumstances, past or present, that you allow to define you? Name one and commit to seeing yourself as Allah created you — an essence that is pure and capable of knowing Him.
?
Sanusi shows that demanding a condition of existence for causal efficacy is a mistake. In your life, do you wait for 'existence' — perfect conditions, certainty, or tangible outcomes — before acting on faith? What is one area where you are hesitating because you think you need a 'condition' that you already have? How can you act now, trusting that your essence is enough for Allah's command?

The Third Proof: Essence's Aptitude for Existence and Refutation of Objection
Sanusi presents the third proof and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that an essence's receptivity to existence is not conditioned by existence, and how an objection against it is refuted by distinguishing acceptance from effect.

Core Idea
An essence's receptivity to existence is prior to existence itself.

Sanusi presents the third proof in a series on the relationship between essence and existence. He argues that the essence's aptitude (qabiliyya) for existence is an external attribute that is not conditioned by existence. Then he addresses an objection that would make the non-existent a cause of the existent, showing it confuses acceptance with effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp Sanusi's argument about the essence's receptivity being prior to existence.
Notice the logical steps: from existent essence -> receptivity -> priority -> external attribute -> not conditioned by existence.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if the exigency is not conditioned by existence, then the non-existent essence would be a cause of existence.
This seems problematic because a non-existent cannot cause existence.
3
Examine the refutation
Sanusi distinguishes between acceptance (qabul) and effect (ta'thir). The essence's acceptance of existence is not conditioned by existence, but this does not imply it causes existence while non-existent.
The essence in itself is neutral to existence or non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الماهية الموجودة قابلة للوجود . وقابلية الوجود سابقة على حصول الوجود . ثم نقول : قابلية الوجود صفة خارجة عن الماهية ، لأن قابلية الوجود نسبة مخصوصة بين الماهية وبين الوجود ، والنسبة بين الأمرين مغايرة لذات كل واحد منهما ، فقابلية الماهية للوجود صفة خارجة عن الماهية لازمة لها ، فتكون معلولة الماهية لقابلية الوجود لا تكون مشروطة بالوجود ، لأن الوجود متأخر بالرتبة عن قابلية الوجود، والمتأخر لا يكون شرطا للمتقدم . فيثبت : أن اقتضاء الماهية لهذا الحكم، غير مشروط بالوجود ، وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن اقتضاء الماهية للوجود لا يكون مشروطا بوجود آخر ؟ وحينئذ يندفع ما ذكرتموه .
فإن قالوا : فالماهية إذا لم يكن اقتضاؤها للوجود ، مشروطا بكونها موجودة . فحينئذ يجوز كونها مقتضية للموجود حال عدمها . فحينئذ يلزم كون المعدوم علة للموجود ، وذلك محال . فنقول : لا شك أن قبول الماهية للوجود غير مشروط بكونها موجودة ، ثم مع هذا لا يلزم أن يقال : المعدوم موصوف بالوجود . وإذا لم يلزم هذا في جانب القبول ، فكذلك مثله في جانب التأثير وتحقيق القول فيه : أن الماهية من حيث إنها هي ، مغايرة لكونها موجودة أو معدومة . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فقد سقطت هذه الحجة التي عولوا عليها من جميع الوجوه .
The third: The existent essence is receptive to existence. And the receptivity of existence is prior to the attainment of existence. Then we say: The receptivity of existence is an attribute external to the essence, because the receptivity of existence is a specific relation between the essence and existence, and the relation between two things is other than the essence of each one of them. Thus the receptivity of the essence to existence is an attribute external to the essence, necessary for it, so the essence is caused by the receptivity of existence not being conditioned by existence, because existence is posterior in rank to the receptivity of existence, and the posterior cannot be a condition for the prior. So it is established that the essence's exigency for this judgment is not conditioned by existence. And if that is so, then why is it not permissible to say that the essence's exigency for existence is not conditioned by another existence? And thus what you mentioned is repelled.
If they say: If the essence's exigency for existence is not conditioned by its being existent, then it would be permissible for it to be exigent for the existent during its non-existence, and then it would entail that the non-existent is a cause of the existent, and that is impossible. We say: There is no doubt that the essence's acceptance of existence is not conditioned by its being existent, yet with this it does not entail that it is said that the non-existent is described by existence. And if this does not entail on the side of acceptance, then likewise on the side of effect. And the verification of the statement about it is that the essence insofar as it is itself is other than its being existent or non-existent. And if the matter is so, then this argument upon which they relied has fallen from all aspects.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof in a series concerning the relationship between essence and existence. It argues that an existent essence has an aptitude (qabiliyya) for existence, and this aptitude is prior to the actual existence. The author explains that this aptitude is an external attribute relative to the essence, because it is a relation between essence and existence, and a relation is distinct from both relata. Therefore, the essence is caused by this aptitude, and the aptitude is not conditioned by existence—indeed existence is posterior to it. The conclusion is that the essence's exigency (iqtida') for existence is not conditioned by existence itself. Then the author anticipates an objection: if the exigency is not conditioned by existence, then during non-existence the essence would be exigent for existence, making the non-existent a cause of the existent, which is impossible. The response distinguishes between acceptance (qabul) and effect (ta'thir): just as the essence's acceptance of existence is not conditioned by existence and does not entail describing the non-existent as existent, similarly the effect side follows the same logic. The essence considered in itself is other than being existent or non-existent, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الماهية
الوجود
قابلية
اقتضاء
معلولة
علة
معدوم
قبول
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection about the non-existent being a cause is valid.
Correction: The author shows that the objection confuses acceptance (which does not require existence) with effect (which does). The essence in itself is neutral, so the non-existent is not described as a cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the essence's aptitude for existence is not conditioned by existence?
A) Because existence is prior to the aptitude by rank.
B) Because the aptitude is an attribute external to the essence and existence is posterior to it.
C) Because the essence is always existent.
D) Because the aptitude is identical to existence.

Key Takeaway
The essence's receptivity to existence is an external attribute and is not conditioned by existence; the objection that this would lead to a non-existent cause is refuted by distinguishing acceptance from effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence's receptivity to existence is not conditioned by your own existence, what does that imply about your absolute dependence on the Creator for every moment of your being?
?
You have learned that the essence considered in itself is distinct from both existence and non-existence. How does this truth challenge your habit of defining yourself by your existence or achievements?
?
The objection that a non-existent essence cannot cause existence was refuted by distinguishing acceptance from effect. Are there areas in your life where you confuse your capacity to receive with your power to effect, thus taking credit where you are merely receptive?

Refutation of the First Argument for Intermediaries in Causation
Presents and refutes the first argument against intermediaries in causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by the mutakallimun to deny intermediaries in causation and how it is refuted by exposing the ambiguity in the phrase 'not more plausible'.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun's argument collapses when we distinguish objective from subjective priority.

In this passage, the author presents and then refutes the first argument proposed by the theologians to deny intermediaries in causation. The argument relies on a claim that no level is more plausibly originated or eternal than another. The refutation shows that this claim is either false in reality or only leads to suspension of judgment, not a definitive conclusion. Let's examine the argument and its critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument and response.
Pay attention to the structure: preface, argument, refutation.
2
Identify the mutakallimun's core claim
Find the statement that 'some levels are not more plausibly originated or eternal than the opposite' and see how they conclude that the world must be eternal.
This claim drives their dilemma.
3
Understand the refutation's two-pronged strategy
The refutation asks: What is meant by 'not more plausible'? It distinguishes between objective reality (fi nafs al-amr) and subjective human understanding.
Each interpretation leads to a different result.
4
Examine the consequences of each interpretation
If objective, the claim is denied; if subjective, it only forces suspension of judgment, not a definitive conclusion.
The mutakallimun's desired conclusion is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إقامة الدلالة على [نفي] (۱) هذه الوسائط . فاعلم أن المتكلمين ذكروا في هذا الباب طرقا واهية ونحن نذكرها وننبه على ضعفها . الحجة الأولى : قالوا : ليس القول بحدوث بعض تلك المراتب وقدم بعضها أولى من العكس . فإما أن نحكم بحدوث الكل ، وحينئذ يلزم ، إما التسلسل أو الدور ، أو نحكم بقدم هذا العالم وهو المطلوب . ولقائل أن يقول : ما المراد من قولكم : ليس البعض بأولى من البعض ؟ إن أردتم [عدم] (٢) الأولوية في نفس الأمر فهذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن بعض تلك المراتب أولى بالحدوث ، والمرتبة المغايرة للمرتبة الأولى أولى بالقدم ؟ وإن كنا لا تعرف سبب تلك الأولوية ، فإن عقولنا ضعيفة ، وأفهامنا قاصرة ، وليس كل ما لا تعرفه ، وجب أن لا يكون موجودا . وإن أردتم به عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وعقولنا ، فهذا لا يفيد إلا وجوب التوقف وعدم الجزم بقسم واحد من هذه الأقسام المحتملة فإما أن نجزم بنفي البعض وثبوت الباقي . وذلك باطل قطعا .
And as for establishing the proof for denying these intermediaries, know that the theologians (al-mutakallimun) have mentioned in this chapter weak ways, and we will mention them and point out their weakness. The first proof: They said: The statement that some of those levels are temporally originated and some are eternal is not more plausible than the opposite. So either we judge that all are originated, in which case either infinite regress or circularity follows, or we judge that this world is eternal, which is what is sought. It can be said: What do you mean by 'some are not more plausible than some'? If you mean the absence of priority in objective reality (fi nafs al-amr), this is denied. For why is it not permissible to say that some of those levels are more deserving of origination, and the level different from the first level is more deserving of eternity? Even if we do not know the reason for that priority, for our intellects are weak and our understandings are deficient, and not everything we do not know must be non-existent. And if you mean by it the absence of priority in our minds and intellects, then this only necessitates suspension of judgment and not being certain of one of these possible divisions, so that we might be certain of negating some and affirming the rest. That is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is the first argument (al-hujja al-ula) presented by the theologians (al-mutakallimun) to deny intermediaries, along with its refutation. The author prefaces by noting that the theologians have proposed weak arguments, which he will present and critique. The argument states: The claim that some levels (maratib) are originated (hadith) and some are eternal (qadim) is not more plausible than the opposite (i.e., that the originated ones are eternal and vice versa). Therefore, we have two options: either judge that all are originated, which leads to infinite regress or circularity, or judge that this world is eternal, which is their desired conclusion. The refutation (by the author) comes in the form of a question: What do you mean by 'not more plausible'? If you mean it in objective reality (fi nafs al-amr), then it is denied. Why can it not be that some levels are inherently more deserving of origination and others of eternity, even if we do not know the reason? Our intellects are weak, and not everything unknown is impossible. If you mean it in our minds, then that only forces us to suspend judgment, not to definitively negate some levels and affirm others, which would be clearly false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun's argument is sound and proves the world's eternity.
Correction: The refutation shows the argument equivocates between objective and subjective priority, and thus fails to prove the conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first argument, what is the consequence if the phrase 'not more plausible' is understood in terms of subjective human intellect?
A) It proves that all levels are originated.
B) It necessitates suspension of judgment, not a definitive conclusion.
C) It proves that the world is eternal.
D) It shows that intermediaries must exist.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's first argument fails because it conflates objective priority with subjective uncertainty, leading only to suspension of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because it seemed arbitrary or unproven to you, without considering that your intellect might be too weak to grasp its reason? How does this lesson call you to humility before divine wisdom?
?
The mutakallimun demanded certainty where only suspension was warranted. In what area of your faith do you demand absolute clarity, and are you willing to accept ambiguity as a test of your trust in Allah?
?
If you were to apply the distinction between objective reality and subjective understanding to your own theological doubts, which doubts might dissolve simply by admitting your own ignorance? What would it cost you to say, 'I do not know, but I submit'?

The Indifference Argument and Its Refutation
Presentation and refutation of the indifference argument against intermediaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument from indifference used against intermediaries and how it is refuted by distinguishing between real priority and epistemic priority.

Core Idea
The indifference argument fails because it confuses ignorance with impossibility.

The first argument against intermediaries claims that since we have no reason to prefer any particular level as originated or eternal, we must either deem all as originated (leading to absurdities) or accept the world as eternal. The rebuttal challenges the meaning of 'no preference', showing that either it is false in reality or only suspends our judgment, not certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its conclusion
Read the first part of the passage where the opponents present their proof from indifference. Note the two possible outcomes: all levels originated (impossible due to regress) or world eternal (their desired conclusion).
The argument tries to force a choice between two options.
2
Identify the objector's strategic question
The objector asks: 'What do you mean by 'no preference'?' This is crucial. The entire refutation hinges on clarifying this ambiguity.
The question is a classic dialectical move to expose a false premise.
3
Examine the two interpretations
The objector unpacks two possible meanings: lack of priority in reality, or lack of priority in our knowledge. Each leads to a different conclusion.
The first interpretation is rejected; the second only forces suspension of judgment.
4
Conclude why the argument fails
Neither interpretation yields the desired certainty. The argument is therefore invalid. Understand that the opponents cannot legitimately claim that the world is eternal based on this reasoning.
The author shows that the proof only demonstrates our ignorance, not the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : ليس القول بحدوث بعض تلك المراتب وقدم بعضها أولى من العكس . فإما أن نحكم بحدوث الكل ، وحينئذ يلزم ، إما التسلسل أو الدور ، أو نحكم بقدم هذا العالم وهو المطلوب . ولقائل أن يقول : ما المراد من قولكم : ليس البعض بأولى من البعض ؟ إن أردتم عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر فهذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن بعض تلك المراتب أولى بالحدوث ، والمرتبة المغايرة للمرتبة الأولى أولى بالقدم ؟ وإن كنا لا تعرف سبب تلك الأولوية ، فإن عقولنا ضعيفة ، وأفهامنا قاصرة ، وليس كل ما لا تعرفه ، وجب أن لا يكون موجودا . وإن أردتم به عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وعقولنا ، فهذا لا يفيد إلا وجوب التوقف وعدم الجزم بقسم واحد من هذه الأقسام المحتملة فإما أن نجزم بنفي البعض وثبوت الباقي . وذلك باطل قطعا .
The first proof: They said: The statement that some of those levels are temporally originated and some are eternal is not more preferable than the opposite. So either we judge that all are originated, and then either infinite regress or circularity follows, or we judge that this world is eternal, which is the desired conclusion. And one may say: What do you mean by your statement 'one part is not more preferable than another'? If you mean the lack of priority in reality itself, then this is denied. For why is it not permissible to say that some of those levels are more deserving of origination, and the level different from the first level is more deserving of eternity? Even if we do not know the cause of that priority, for our intellects are weak and our understandings deficient, and not everything we do not know must be non-existent. And if you mean by it the lack of priority in our minds and intellects, then this only necessitates suspension of judgment and not being certain of any one of these possible divisions, so that we either affirm the negation of some and the establishment of the rest, and that is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first proof against intermediaries. The proof argues from indifference: since no part of the cosmic levels is more likely to be originated or eternal, either all are originated (leading to infinite regress or circularity) or the world is eternal. The objector responds by clarifying the meaning of 'no priority'. If the priority is denied in reality, it is rejected because we cannot know the true cause of priority. If it is denied only in our knowledge, it forces us to suspend judgment, not to conclude certainty. Thus the proof is invalid. The author thus shows the weakness of this common argument.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main criticism of the indifference argument?
A) The argument assumes we know the cause of priority.
B) The argument only shows suspension of judgment, not certainty.
C) The argument proves the world is eternal.
D) The argument relies on circular reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The indifference argument against intermediaries fails because it confuses a lack of known priority with a lack of real priority, and can at most warrant agnosticism, not certainty.

?
The refutation forces humility about our intellects. Will you commit today to accepting truths from revelation even when your mind cannot fully grasp them, and to avoid using your ignorance as an argument against what is possible?

The Second Proof Against Essences as Causes
Presentation of the second proof against essences as causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second proof that no essence can cause another, and why its proponents consider it impossible.

Core Idea
If essences are equal, causing would be universal and self-referential.

In this passage, the mutakallimun present a second argument to show that essences cannot serve as causes for one another. They build on the premise that all essences are equal in essence-hood, and argue that if one could cause another, then every essence would cause every essence, leading to impossible consequences. This proof sets the stage for the author's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the proof (from 'الثانية' to 'منها') and its translation. Try to understand the flow of the argument without over-analysing.
Notice the use of 'المسألة المتقدمة' referencing a prior discussion.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) all essences are equal in essence-hood, and (2) difference is only through attributes. Find these in the text.
3
Trace the two consequences
From the premises, the proof derives two impossible consequences: (a) a specific essence would be cause of itself, and (b) the caused essence would be universal to its cause. Identify both.
Think why these are considered impossible in classical kalam.
4
Understand the conclusion
Because these consequences are impossible, the proof concludes that no essence can be a cause of another. This is the claim the refutation will challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : قالوا : لا يجوز أن يكون شيء من الذوات علة لذات أخرى . لأنا بينا في المسألة المتقدمة : أن الذوات بأسرها متساوية في الذاتية ، وأن الاختلاف ليس إلا بالصفات ، فلو كانت ذات علة لذات ، لكان كل ذات علة لكل ذات. فيلزم كون الذات المعينة علة لنفسها. ويلزم كون الذات المعلولة عامة للذات التي فرض كونها علة وكل ذلك محال . فيثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون شيء من الذوات علة لشيء منها . ولقائل أن يقول : هذا بناء على أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات، ونحن قد بينا بالبرهان اليقيني أن ذلك باطل ، إذ لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم أن تكون الأشياء المتساوية ، يلزمها لوازم مختلفة. وذلك محال في العقول . أما لو قلنا : إنها في أنفسها مختلفة إلا أنها مع اختلافها في حقائقها متشاركة في كونها ذوات ، بمعنى أنها أمور غير تابعة في وجودها لغيرها ، فعلى هذا التقدير فقد حصلت أشياء مختلفة في الماهية ، مع أنها تكون مشتركة في بعض اللوازم . فثبت : أن الحق ما ذكرناه . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فقط سقط كلامكم . وأيضا : فمدار هذه الحجة على مقدمتين : إحداهما : أن الذوات بأسرها متساوية في الذاتية . والثانية : أن الأشياء المتساوية وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما يصح على الآخر . فنقول : إن كان الأمر كذلك كما ذكرتم ، فحينئذ يلزمكم جواز أن ينقلب الخالق مخلوقا والمخلوق خالقا والممكن واجبا والواجب ممكنا ، وحينئذ يبطل عليكم كل ما ذكرتموه . ولما لم يلزم هذا ، فكذا مهنا.
Second proof: They said: It is not permissible that any essence be a cause of another essence. Because we have shown in the previous issue that all essences are equal in essence-hood, and that difference is only through attributes. So if an essence were a cause of an essence, then every essence would be a cause of every essence. This would necessitate that a specific essence be a cause of itself, and that the caused essence be general to the essence supposed to be its cause, and all that is impossible. Thus it is established that it is not permissible that any essence be a cause of any other. And one may say: This is based on the idea that essences are equal in being essences. And we have already proven by certain demonstration that this is false, for if that were the case, then equal things would necessitate different concomitants, and that is intellectually impossible. But if we say that they are different in themselves, yet despite their differences in realities they share in being essences, meaning that they are things not dependent in their existence on others, then on this estimation we have obtained things different in quiddity while they share in some concomitants. Thus it is established that the truth is what we have mentioned. And if that is the case, your argument collapses. Moreover, this proof rests on two premises: One: all essences are equal in essence-hood. Two: things that are equal must have the same properties. We say: If that is the case as you mentioned, then it would necessitate for you the possibility of the Creator becoming a creature and the creature becoming a creator, the contingent becoming necessary and the necessary becoming contingent, and then all that you have mentioned is invalidated. And since this does not follow, so too here.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second proof against intermediaries, which argues that no essence can be a cause of another because all essences are equal in essence-hood. The refutation points out that essences are not completely equal; they differ in reality but share the property of not being dependent. Moreover, if the premises were accepted, they would lead to absurd consequences such as the Creator becoming a creature. Since those consequences are impossible, the premises must be false. The author thus shows the proof is unsound.

Key Terms
ذوات
علة
ذاتية
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the proponents of this proof claim about essences?
A) Essences are completely different from each other.
B) Essences are equal in essence-hood but differ by attributes.
C) Essences are independent of causes.
D) Essences are caused by attributes.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses the equality of essences to argue that if one caused another, universal causation and self-causation would follow, which are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you ever feel that it is because of your own essence—your intelligence, talent, or hard work—forgetting that all causal power comes ultimately from Allah? How can this proof remind you to attribute success to His giving?
?
Think of a situation where you were convinced that something 'had to happen' because of its nature. Does this proof challenge that assumption? Will you now pause and reflect on the true cause behind events?
?
If no essence can cause another, then every effect you see around you—including your own actions—is directly dependent on Allah. Are you living as if that is true, or do you still feel self-sufficient? What will you change today to align your heart with this reality?
